Conflict of Evidence Without Dates: Two Shaykhs vs. Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conflicting undated purchase evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on conflicting undated purchase claims and explain the proof of the two shaykhs based on mutual cancellation of admissions.

Core Idea
When both parties present undated purchase claims, the two shaykhs void both testimonies; Muhammad upholds them.

When litigants each claim to have bought the same item from the other, but neither provides a date, how should a judge rule? This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute where the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) treat the purchase acts as mutual admissions that cancel each other out, while Imam Muhammad seeks to reconcile both testimonies by assuming a sequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Both parties present evidence (bayyina) that each purchased the disputed item from the other, but no dates are specified.
This creates a conflict of two equally credible testimonies with unknown temporal priority.
2
Understand the two shaykhs' ruling
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule that both pieces of evidence are invalid (tasqut al-bayyinatain), and the item remains with the current possessor.
This is based on the principle of mutual cancellation.
3
Grasp their proof
Each purchase inherently admits the seller's ownership. Thus, each party's evidence is effectively proof of the other's admission, leading to automatic cancellation (tahatur) by consensus.
The proof is analogical: like two simultaneous admissions, which cancel by consensus, so here.
4
Learn Muhammad's alternative
Imam Muhammad holds that both testimonies can be acted upon. He posits that the possessor bought first from the external, then the external bought back, so the external is awarded the item.
This is considered possible, though problematic for movables due to prohibition of sale before possession.
5
Note the practical difference
The thamarah al-khilaf: under the two shaykhs, the item stays with the possessor; under Muhammad, it goes to the external claimant.
The judge's chosen view determines the outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢]قوله : سقط البينتان...الخ ؛ هذا مذهب الشيخين، ودليلهما : أنَّ الإقدام على الشراء إقرار من المشتري بالملك للبائع ، فصار كأنَّ كلاً منهما أقام البينة على إقرار صاحبه بالملك، وفيه التهاتر بالإجماع ؛ لأنّ الثابت من الإقرارين بالبينة كالثابت من المعاينة، ولو عاينا إقرارهما معاً بطلا، فإن ما لا يعرف سبق أحدهما جعل كأنهما وقعا معاً، وفيه التهاتر بالإجماع، فكذا هاهنا.
[٣] قوله : وعند محمد ؛ يعني عنده يقضي بالبينتين، ويكون المتنازع فيه للخارج ؛ لأن العمل بالبينتين ممكن، فيجعل كان ذا اليد اشتراه من الخارج أولاً فيؤمرُ
His statement: 'the two testimonies are invalid... etc.' This is the school of the two shaykhs, and their proof is that proceeding to purchase is an admission from the buyer of the seller's ownership, so it becomes as if each of them has established evidence on the admission of his companion of ownership, and in it there is mutual cancellation (tahatur) by consensus; because what is established from the two admissions by evidence is like what is established by direct observation, and if we had directly observed their admissions together they would be void, for what does not have known priority of one over the other is treated as if they occurred together, and in it there is mutual cancellation by consensus; so here also. His statement: 'And according to Muhammad' means according to him, he judges by both testimonies, and the disputed item is for the external, because acting upon both testimonies is possible, so it is considered as if the possessor bought it from the external first, so he is ordered...
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute recorded in the sharh. It addresses the scenario where both parties present evidence (bayyina) that each purchased the disputed item from the other, without specifying a date. The two shaykhs (al-Shaykhan = Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) rule that both pieces of evidence are invalid (tasqut al-bayyinatain) and the item remains with the possessor. Their proof: each purchase act is inherently an admission (iqrar) by the buyer that the seller owned the item. Thus, when each claims to have bought from the other, it is as if each has established evidence of the other's admission of ownership. This leads to mutual cancellation (tahatur) because if we could observe these admissions directly without knowing which occurred first, they would cancel each other. Hence, the evidence is voided. Imam Muhammad differs. He argues that both testimonies can be upheld, and the item should be awarded to the external claimant (the one not in possession), because it is possible to interpret the events as: first, the possessor bought from the external, then later the external bought back from the possessor. This interpretation is valid for real estate (since sale before possession is allowed in some cases), but for movables, it is problematic because selling before possession is prohibited. Nevertheless, Muhammad considers it possible. The text cuts off at 'so he is ordered' possibly referencing that the external is ordered to take the item. The passage highlights a key ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school in evidence law.

Key Terms
التهاتر (al-tahatur): Mutual cancellation of two contradictory claims or admissions, rendering both ineffective.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Admission; here the act of purchasing is deemed an implicit admission of the seller's ownership.
الشيخان (al-shaykhan): Refers to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the two primary authorities in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), what is the ruling when each party presents evidence of purchasing the item from the other without a date?
A) The evidence of the possessor is accepted because he is in hand.
B) The evidence of the external claimant is accepted because he proved purchase.
C) Both pieces of evidence are invalid (tasqut al-bayyinatain).
D) The case is decided by the judge's discretion.

Key Takeaway
In an undated conflicting purchase claim, the two shaykhs cancel both testimonies, while Muhammad allows both and gives the item to the external claimant.

Conflicting Purchase Claims Without Dates
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conflicting purchase evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi default rule when both parties present evidence of mutual purchase without dates, and explain the disagreement between the Two Shaykhs and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, including the proof and practical consequences.

Core Idea
When two purchase claims cancel each other, the property stays with the possessor—unless stronger proof like manumission exists.

Imagine two people each claim to have bought the same item from the other, but neither can produce a date for the transaction. How should a judge rule? This lesson unpacks a classic Hanafi evidentiary puzzle from Sharh al-Wiqayah. The text presents the standard doctrine of the Two Shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) that both pieces of evidence cancel each other out, leaving the property with the current possessor. Then it introduces Imam Muhammad's alternative approach, which tries to give effect to both evidences by reconstructing a sequence. By working through the proofs, you will understand not only the rule but also the deeper logic of how Hanafi jurists handle conflicting evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
There are two parties: one in possession of an item (dhu al-yad) and one outsider (kharj). Each claims to have purchased the item from the other. Neither presents a date for the transaction.
Possession gives a default advantage, but the mutual purchase claims complicate matters because each is effectively acknowledging the other's ownership.
2
State the Two Shaykhs' ruling and its proof
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, both evidences are dropped (tasqut al-bayyinatan) and the property remains with the possessor. Their proof: proceeding to purchase is an acknowledgment by the buyer of the seller's ownership, so each is acknowledging the other's claim. Since two equal acknowledgments cancel each other by consensus (tahatur), the evidences cancel and the default (possession) prevails.
This is analogous to two simultaneous confessions – both are void because neither is preponderant.
3
Compare Imam Muhammad's alternative
Imam Muhammad holds that both evidences can be given effect. He reconstructs the sequence: the possessor must have bought from the outsider first, then sold to the outsider but failed to deliver possession. Since sale before possession is invalid, the transaction is void, so the outsider still owns the property. Muhammad gives the outsider the item.
Muhammad's view is rejected by the Two Shaykhs because the sequence is arbitrary and the cause (purchase) does not yield the ruling (ownership) when unknown.
4
Apply the ruling to a real case with additional claims
The text notes that the possessor's preference applies only if the outsider does not claim manumission (itq) along with prior ownership. If the outsider proves he manumitted a previous owner and thus has a stronger claim, his evidence takes priority over the possessor's mere offspring claim.
This shows that the default rule can be overridden by stronger evidence, preserving equity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

So the possessor of the hand has more right, even if each presents evidence of purchase from the other without a date, both (evidences) are dropped, and the property is left in the hand of the one with whom it is. And if each presents evidence of purchase from the other without a date, both are dropped, and the property is left in the hand of the one with whom it is. That is: each of the possessor and the outsider presents evidence of purchase from the other, and they do not mention a date, both evidences are dropped, and the property is left in the hand of the possessor. And the reverse is not considered, that the outsider bought from the possessor first then sold it to the possessor, because that would necessitate selling the sold item before taking possession, which is not permissible according to Muhammad, even if in real estate. And the response from the two shaykhs is that the cause, if beneficial for the ruling, is considered, otherwise not here, and the cause, which is each's purchase from the other, does not benefit the ruling, which is ownership; and judgment for the possessor cannot be given except by rightful ownership, so judgment for him remains merely by the cause, and it does not benefit him. And according to Muhammad, judgment is given for the outsider, because his sale before possession is not valid, so it remains in his ownership. And if they establish possession, judgment is given for the possessor, because both sales are valid according to both opinions. And if the time of the possessor is earlier, judgment is given for the outsider in both cases, so it is considered that the possessor bought it, took possession, then sold and did not hand over, or handed over then it came to him through another cause. End. [2] His statement: And I have collected them from al-Dhakhira, precisely and concisely; the gist of the discourse in controlling these divisions is: If the two claimants present evidence, then if the date of one of them at the time of presenting the evidence is earlier, he is more deserving. And if it is not earlier but equal, such as if they dated in agreement, or they did not date at all, or they dated.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Wiqayah deals with a peculiar scenario in the law of evidence (da'wa) where two parties both claim to have purchased the same property from each other. The text first states the default ruling: if both present evidence (beyyinah) of purchase from the other without providing a date, both pieces of evidence are cancelled (tasqut al-bayyinatan) and the property remains with the current possessor (dhu al-yad). This is the view of the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), who analogize the purchase transaction to an acknowledgment (iqrar) of the seller's ownership, leading to mutual cancellation (tahatur). However, Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees, arguing that both evidences can be given effect by considering that the possessor bought from the outsider first, then sold to him but failed to deliver possession, which indicates the outsider's prior right (as possession after sale suggests earlier purchase). Muhammad's position is based on the principle that selling before taking possession (bay' qabl al-qabd) is invalid, so the reverse scenario (outsider selling to possessor first) would be impermissible, but his preferred sequence avoids that. The commentary then clarifies that the preference for the possessor's evidence only applies in cases of offspring (natal) if the outsider does not additionally claim manumission (itq), because a claim of manumission combined with prior ownership is stronger. Finally, the author notes a summary from al-Dhakhira: if there is a date and one is earlier, that party prevails; if dates are equal or absent, the default rule applies. The passage also discusses the application to real estate (aqar) where the two shaykhs allow sale before possession, unlike Muhammad.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, specifically testimony or proof presented in court to support a claim.
ذو اليد (dhu al-yad): The person who currently possesses the property in dispute, literally 'the one with the hand'.
خارج (kharj): The outsider, i.e., the person not in possession who claims ownership.
تهاتر (tahatur): Mutual cancellation or setting off; here, the two evidences cancel each other out because they are contradictory and neither is preponderant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), when two parties both present evidence of purchasing the item from each other without a date, what is the ruling?
A) The property is divided equally between them.
B) The evidence of the possessor is accepted and the outsider's is rejected.
C) Both evidences are cancelled and the property remains with the possessor.
D) The evidence of the outsider is accepted because his purchase is more recent.

Key Takeaway
Conflicting evidences of purchase without dates cancel each other, and the property remains with the possessor; but stronger claims like manumission or earlier dates override.

Sheikhayn vs Muhammad: Date Priority Without Possession
Intra-Hanafi dispute on date vs possession priority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of the Sheikhayn and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the priority of date vs possession in real estate claims, and explain the legal reasoning behind each view.

Core Idea
Possession can override an earlier purchase date if sale before delivery is valid for the asset type.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute on how a judge should rule when two claimants present evidence with dates, but neither proves possession. The key is whether the asset is real estate, and whether sale before taking possession is permitted. The Sheikhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) allow such sales for real estate, while Muhammad does not. This difference leads to opposite judgments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and positions
Understand the exact situation: two claimaints present dated evidence for a real estate property, but neither proves possession (qabd). The outsider's date is earlier. State the Sheikhayn's ruling and Muhammad's ruling.
The dispute only concerns real estate because for movables, all agree sale before qabd is invalid.
2
Analyze the Sheikhayn's reasoning
The Sheikhayn treat the case as if the outsider bought first, then sold to the possessor before delivery. Since they consider sale before delivery valid for real estate, the possessor becomes the owner. Their response: a cause (purchase) that does not effect the ruling (ownership) is disregarded.
They rely on istihsan to except real estate from the general prohibition of selling before possession.
3
Analyze Muhammad's reasoning
Muhammad holds that sale before delivery is invalid even for real estate, based on the general hadith forbidding it. Therefore, the outsider never transferred ownership, so he remains owner and deserves judgment.
Muhammad takes the literal application of the prohibition to all goods, including real estate.
4
Apply the rulings to the possessor's earlier date scenario
If the possessor's date is earlier, both schools agree the outsider wins. The reasoning is that the possessor is deemed to have bought, taken possession, then sold without delivery (or another cause), which favors the outsider.
This symmetric outcome shows the centrality of possession in both views.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is the Sheikhayn's view. Recognize that in a Hanafi court following this view, the possessor wins when his date is later but he has possession.
Later authorities adopted the Sheikhayn's view as the default for real estate claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى باب دعوى الرجلين وإنما قال : بلا وقتِ حتّى لو أرخا ، ففيه تفصيل مذكور في «الهداية» فطالعها إن شئت. واعلم أن صاحب الهداية» ذكَرَ هذه المسائل من غير ضبط، وإني جمعتها من «الذخيرة» مضبوطة موجزة ، فأقول : بالتسليم إلى الخارج، ثم باعه من الخارج ولم يُسَلّم؛ لأنَّ القبض له دلالة السبق؛ لأنّ تمكنه من قبضه يدل على سبق شرائه. ولا يعكس الأمر بأنَّ الخارج كان اشترى من ذي اليد أولاً ثم باعه من ذي اليد؛ لأنَّ في ذلك يلزم بيع المبيع قبل القبض، وهو ليس بجائز عند محمد ه ، وإن كان في العقار. والجواب من جانب الشيخين : إنَّ السبب إذا كان مفيداً للحكم يعتبر، وإلا فلا هاهنا والسبب وهو شراء كلّ منهما من صاحبه لا يفيد الحكم، وهو الملك؛ ولا يمكن القضاء لذي اليد إلا بملك مستحق، فبقي القضاءُ له بمجرد السبب، وأنه لا يفيده. [١]قوله : تفصيل مذكور في «الهداية»؛ قال في «الهداية»: وإن وقتت البينتان في العقار ولم تُثبتا قبضاً، وَوَقْتُ الخارج أسبق يقضى لصاحب اليد عندهما، فيجعل كأنَّ الخارج اشترى أولاً ثمّ باعَ قبل القبض من صاحب اليد، وهو جائز في العقار عندهما. وعند محمد له يقضى للخارج؛ لأنه لا يصح بيعه قبل القبض، فبقي على ملكه، وإن أثبتا قبضاً يقضى لصاحب اليد؛ لأنَّ البيعين جائزان على القولين، وإذا كان وقت صاحب اليد أسبق يقضى للخارج في الوجهين، فيجعل كأنه اشتراه ذو اليد، وقبض ثمَّ باعَ ولم يُسَلّم ، أو سَلَّمَ ثم وصل إليه بسبب آخر. انتهى.
The Book of Claims, Chapter of the Claim of Two Men. He (the author) only said: 'without specifying a time' such that if they date, there is a detail mentioned in al-Hidayah, so consult it if you wish. Know that the author of al-Hidayah mentioned these issues without organization, and I have collected them from al-Dhakhirah in an organized and concise manner. I say: (ruling) is to surrender to the outsider, then he sold it to the outsider without delivery, because possession indicates priority; his ability to take possession indicates the priority of his purchase. And the reverse does not apply: that the outsider bought from the possessor first then sold to the possessor, because that would involve selling before taking possession, which is not permissible according to Muhammad, even in real estate. The response from the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is: A cause, if it is effective in bringing about the ruling, is considered; otherwise not. Here the cause—each buying from the other—does not effect the ruling, which is ownership. Judgment in favor of the possessor cannot be given except on the basis of established ownership, so judgment for him remains merely on the basis of the cause, which does not benefit him. [1] His statement: 'a detail mentioned in al-Hidayah'; he said in al-Hidayah: If the two evidences are dated in real estate and they did not prove possession, and the outsider's date is earlier, judgment is given to the possessor according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). They deem it as if the outsider bought first then sold before taking possession to the possessor, which is permissible in real estate according to them. According to Muhammad, judgment is given to the outsider, because his sale before taking possession is not valid, so it remains his property. If they prove possession, judgment is given to the possessor because both sales are valid on both opinions. If the possessor's date is earlier, judgment is given to the outsider in both cases, deeming it as if the possessor bought it, took possession, then sold without delivery, or he delivered then it reached him through another cause. End.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling when two claimants each present evidence (bayyinah) with dates, but no proof of possession (qabd) exists. The core issue is whether a prior date of purchase should determine ownership despite lack of possession. The author first notes that al-Hidayah discussed this with details, then provides a summary from al-Dhakhirah. The dispute is between the 'Sheikhayn'—Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf—and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The scenario: two people claim ownership of a real estate property. The outsider's evidence shows an earlier purchase date than the possessor's, but neither proves actual possession. The Sheikhayn rule in favor of the possessor, reasoning that the outsider's earlier purchase would have required him to take possession before selling to the possessor, which is valid for real estate in their view. They treat it as if the outsider bought first, then sold to the possessor before taking possession—a sale that is permissible for real estate. Muhammad disagrees: he deems the sale before possession invalid even for real estate, so the outsider remains owner. Thus Muhammad gives judgment to the outsider. If both prove possession, both sales are valid, so the possessor wins due to his possession. If the possessor's date is earlier, both agree the outsider wins, as the possessor is deemed to have bought, taken possession, then sold without delivery or via another means. This dispute highlights a key difference within the Hanafi school regarding the permissibility of selling real estate before taking possession.

Key Terms
بينتان (bayyinatan): Dual form of bayyinah, meaning two pieces of evidence (usually from two witnesses each) presented by two claimants.
قبض (qabd): Taking physical possession of a purchased item, which is necessary for ownership transfer in many cases.
خارج (khārij): The 'outsider' claimant who does not have possession of the disputed property.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that allows departure from a clear analogy in favor of a more suitable ruling based on custom, necessity, or public interest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, if two claimants present dated evidence for a house and the outsider's date is earlier, but neither proves possession, who wins?
A) The possessor
B) The outsider
C) The property is divided
D) The case is dismissed

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the mufta bihi is the Sheikhayn's view: the possessor prevails over an earlier date when no possession is proven for real estate.

Rules for Two Claimants with Evidence and Possession
Rules when both claimants present evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for resolving property disputes when both claimants present evidence, applying the factors of date, possession, and type of claim.

Core Idea
When both claimants bring proof, the earlier date wins; if dates equal, the outsider prevails in absolute ownership claims, unless an additional act is claimed.

In judicial proceedings, a qadi often faces two claimants with seemingly equal evidence. Hanafi fiqh provides clear priority rules based on three key factors: the date of evidence, possession of the property, and the nature of the claim (absolute ownership vs. ownership with an additional act like freeing a slave). This lesson organizes these rules into a memorable framework for practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three decisive factors
Recognize that the rules depend on (1) presence of a date (tārīkh) on the evidence, (2) possession (yad) of the disputed property, and (3) whether the claim concerns absolute ownership (milk mutlaq) or ownership with a cause (milk bi-sabab) like freeing a slave.
These factors interact in a specific order: date trumps, then possession, then type of claim.
2
Apply the date rule first
If one claimant's evidence has an earlier date, that claimant is preferred regardless of possession or claim type. If dates are equal or absent, move to the next step.
Equality means both have the same date, both lack dates, or both date but neither is earlier.
3
Distinguish absolute ownership from ownership with a cause
In absolute ownership (e.g., 'This slave is mine'), if one claimant is a possessor and the other an outsider, the outsider wins when dates are equal. But if the claim includes an act like freeing or tadbir, the possessor wins because such acts are established by possession.
Exception: claims of kitabah make both outsiders, so they are equal.
4
Handle ownership by cause and multiple causes
If both received from the same transferor, the possessor wins. If from different transferors, the outsider wins when dates equal. If different causes (e.g., purchase vs. gift), compare the strength of the causes as per fiqh principles.
This is only after checking dates and possession status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Likewise if each is an outsider in absolute ownership. This is when they do not date, or one of them dates, or they date but one is not earlier. Likewise in ownership by cause, except if they received (the property) from one person and only one of them dated, then he is more entitled. Contrary to if each says: 'He is my slave, I made a kitabah contract with him,' then they are equal, because both are outsiders, since the kitabah contract requires capacity of both parties, and when they contract, the slave becomes a mukatab, so no possession can be imagined over him. Unlike the freed slave, who is in the possession of the master whether young or old, not knowing his freedom. If one says: 'He is my slave, I made a kitabah contract with him' and the other says: 'I made him mudabbar' or 'I freed him,' then the latter is preferable, because whichever evidence proves more is more entitled. This is in the case of an outsider and a possessor in absolute ownership. As for ownership by cause: if the outsider and possessor mention one cause and received from one person, the possessor is more entitled. If they received from two persons, the outsider is more entitled when dates are equal. If they mention two causes, like purchase and gift and so on, one looks at the strength of the cause, so it is mentioned. 11 His statement: 'Comprehensive for the mentioned scenarios' which are: when they do not date, or one of them dates, or they date but one is not earlier.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage provides a systematic summary of the rules when two claimants both present evidence (bayyinah) in a property dispute. The rules are categorized based on three factors: the presence of a date (tārīkh), the status of possession (yad), and the type of claim (absolute ownership or ownership with a specific act). The key principles are: (1) If one claimant's evidence has an earlier date, he is preferred. (2) If dates are equal or absent, then we look at possession: if both are possessors or both outsiders, they are equal (unless one received from the same third party and has a date). (3) If one is a possessor and the other an outsider, normally the outsider wins in absolute ownership claims (because the possessor's possession is presumed to be from the outsider's earlier purchase), except when the claim involves an act that changes the property's status, such as claiming a slave is freed or mudabbar—then the possessor wins because such acts are established by possession. The text also addresses specific cases involving freeing (itq), tadbir, and kitabah contracts, noting that in kitabah both become outsiders since the mukatab slave is freed from physical possession. Finally, for ownership by cause (e.g., purchase from a chain), if both received from the same person, the possessor wins; if from different persons, the outsider wins when dates equal; if different causes, the stronger cause prevails. This is a memorization anchor for judges (qadis) to resolve conflicting evidence.

Key Terms
الملك المطلق (al-milk al-mutlaq): Absolute ownership claimed without any additional act like freeing or tadbir.
اليد (al-yad): Possession; the person currently holding the property is called dhu al-yad (possessor).
تدبير (tadbir): A declaration by a master that a slave will be free upon the master's death. Such a slave is called mudabbar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute over absolute ownership, both claimants have evidence with the same date. One is a possessor, the other an outsider. According to Hanafi law, who is more entitled?
A) The possessor
B) The outsider
C) Both are equal
D) The case is dismissed

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi evidentiary priority, earlier date wins; absent that, the outsider prevails in absolute ownership, but if an act is claimed, the possessor prevails.

Preference of Bayyinah in Competing Claims of Ownership
Ruling on whose evidence is preferred when two people claim the same property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for preferring bayyinah in competing claims of ownership and apply them to scenarios involving absolute ownership, ownership by cause, possession, and dispositive acts.

Core Idea
In property disputes, the outsider's proof prevails over the possessor's in absolute ownership, unless a dispositive act is claimed.

When two people claim the same property and both present evidence (bayyinah), a judge must decide whose proof is preferred. The Hanafi school provides detailed rules based on possession (yad), the type of ownership claimed (absolute or by cause), and whether a dispositive act (like freeing a slave) is asserted. This lesson unpacks these rules from the commentary of al-Wiqayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimants' statuses
Determine if each claimant is a possessor (dhu yad) or an outsider (kharj). Possession gives a presumption but does not always win.
2
Classify the ownership claim
Is the claim of absolute ownership (milk mutlaq) or ownership by a specific cause (milk bi sabab) like sale or gift? The rules differ.
3
Apply the general rule for absolute ownership
If both are possessors or both outsiders, they are equal. If one possessor and one outsider, the outsider is preferred unless a dispositive act is claimed.
4
Apply the rule for ownership by cause
If both trace to the same person, the possessor prevails. If to different persons, the outsider prevails when dates are equal. If two causes from one person, the stronger cause is considered.
Later commentary clarifies: when claimants derive from different owners, both proofs are accepted and property is divided equally.
5
Handle exceptions and special cases
If a dispositive act (i'taq, tadbir) is claimed along with ownership, the possessor is preferred. For kitabah, both are outsiders and equal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن بَرْهَنَ المدعيان، فإن كان تاريخ أحدهما سابقا، فهو أحق، وإن لم يكن، فإن كان كل منهما ذا يد، فهما متساويان، وكذا إن كان كلّ منهما خارجاً في الملكِ المطلق، وهذا إذا لم يؤرخا، أو أرخ أحدهما، أو أرخا ولم يكن أحدهما سابقاً حتّى إن كان، فقد مر أن السابق أحق، وكذا في الملك بسبب إلا إذا تلقيا من واحد وأرح أحدهما فقط، فإنَّه أحق. وإن كان أحدهما ذا يد والآخر خارجاً، فالخارج أحقُّ في الملكِ المطلقِ. للصور المذكورة، إلا إذا ادعيا مع الملكِ المطلق فعلاً كما إذا قال : هو عبدي أعتقتُه، أو دبرته، فذو اليد أحق أحدهما، وكان كل واحد منهما صاحب يد، أو كان كل منهما خارجاً في الملك المطلق، أو في الملك بسبب فهما متساويان، إلا إذا تلقيا من واحد، وأرخ أحدهما فهو أحق.
شاملاً

[١]قوله : إذ لا يد على المكاتب؛ أوردَ عليه : إنَّ عدم اليد في العتق المطلق أظهر منه في المكاتب.
(٢) وأجيب عنه : بأنَّ الكتابة عقد معاوضة، فلا بُدَّ من أهلية العاقدين وقبولهما، فإذا عقدها يكون معتقاً يداً، فلا يتصور اليد عليه، فمن قال : إنه عبدي كاتبته، فقد اعترف أنه لا يد عليه، ويكون خارجاً بالضرورة سواء كان مراد المدعي نفسه أو ولاؤه، بخلاف التدبير والإعتاق، فإنَّ كلاً منهما تصرُّفٌ لازم، فلا يستدعي قبول العبد له. ويجوز أن يكون العبد صغيراً يكون في يد مولاه أو كبيراً لا يعرف عنقه، فيكون في يد مولاه، كما إذا كان، فإذا قال : هو عبدي أعتقتُه، فقد أثبت قيداً زائداً، فيثبت له الولاية قطعاً، هكذا قالوا. (٢) قوله : ينظر إلى قوة السبب؛ فيه بحث، فإنّ النظر إلى قوة السبب إنما يكون إذا كانت الدعوى من واحد، وأما إذا كانت من اثنين تقبل البيِّنتان وينصفُ بينهما، ولا ينظر إلى قوة السبب. كما صرح به في «الهداية» وشروحها. ووجه الفرق : إنهما إذا ادّعيا الشراء والهبة من واحدٍ لا يحتاجان إلى إثبات الملك لمن ملكها، فإنّه ثابت بتصادقهما، وإنّما الحاجزُ في إثبات سبب الملك عليه، وفي إثباتِ الملك لنفسها، الشراء أقوى من الهبة؛ لكونه معاوضة من الجانبين؛ ولأنه يثبتُ الملك لنفسه، والملك في الهبة يتوقف على القبض.
سبب
وأما إذا ادعيا الشراء والهبة من اثنين لهما محتاجان إلى إثباتِ الملك عن ملكهما، وينتصب كل واحد منهما خصماً عمّن ملكه في إثبات الملك له أولاً ثم لنفسه،
(۱) ومثله في «إيضاح الإصلاح»(ق۱۲۱ /ب).
If the two claimants present proof, then if the date of one is earlier, he is more entitled. If not, then if each of them is in possession, they are equal; likewise if each of them is an outsider in absolute ownership. This is when they are not dated, or one is dated, or both are dated but neither is earlier; however if one is earlier, it has passed that the earlier is more entitled. Likewise in ownership by cause, except if they both received from one person and only one is dated, then he is more entitled. And if one of them is a possessor and the other an outsider, the outsider is more entitled in absolute ownership for the mentioned cases, except if they claim along with absolute ownership an act, such as if he says: 'He is my slave, I freed him' or 'I made him a mudabbar', then the possessor is more entitled. And if each one is a possessor, or each is an outsider in absolute ownership or in ownership by cause, they are equal, except if they received from one person and one is dated, then he is more entitled.
Including
And if one of them is a possessor and the other an outsider, the outsider is more entitled in absolute ownership when the dates are equal, except if they claim along with ownership an act, such as if he says: 'He is my slave, I freed him' or 'I made him a mudabbar', then the possessor is more entitled.
Contrary to if each says: 'He is my slave, I made a kitabah contract with him', they are equal because they are both outsiders, since in the contract of kitabah the capacity of the contracting parties is necessary, so when they contract, the slave becomes free in terms of possession, so possession over him is inconceivable, unlike the freed slave, for he is in the hand of the master if he is young or old, not knowing his freedom.
And if one says: 'He is my slave, I made a kitabah contract with him,' and the other says: 'I made him a mudabbar' or 'I freed him,' then this one is more entitled; because whichever proof is more confirming is more entitled. This is regarding the outsider and the possessor in absolute ownership. As for ownership by cause, if the outsider and possessor mention one cause and both received from one person, the possessor is more entitled; if they received from two persons, the outsider is more entitled when the dates are equal; and if they mention two causes such as purchase and gift and others, one looks to the strength of the cause (1), so it is mentioned.
11 His saying: 'Including the mentioned cases' – these are when they are not dated, or one is dated, or they are dated but neither is earlier.
(1) See: 'Majma' al-Anhur' (2:280).
Contrary to if each says: 'He is my slave, I made a kitabah contract with him,' they are equal because they are both outsiders, since there is no possession over the mukatab. And if one says: 'He is my slave, I made a kitabah contract with him,' and the other says: 'I made him a mudabbar' or 'I freed him,' then this one is more entitled. The rule is that whichever proof is more confirming is more entitled. This is regarding the outsider and the possessor in absolute ownership. As for ownership by cause, if they mention one cause and both received from one person, the possessor is more entitled; if they received from two persons, the outsider is more entitled including the mentioned cases; and if they mention two causes such as purchase and gift and others, one looks to the strength of the cause "" as in the matn). [1] His saying: 'Since there is no possession over the mukatab' – an objection is raised: that the absence of possession in absolute freeing is more apparent than in the mukatab. (2) And it is answered: that kitabah is a commutative contract, so the capacity of the contracting parties and their acceptance is necessary. When he contracts it, the slave becomes free in terms of possession, so possession over him is inconceivable. Thus whoever says: 'He is my slave, I made a kitabah contract with him' has admitted that he has no possession over him, and he becomes an outsider by necessity, whether the claimant intends himself or his wala'. Unlike tadbir and i'taq, for each is a binding disposition, so it does not require the slave's acceptance. It is possible that the slave is young, being in the hand of his master, or old not knowing his own freedom, so he is in the hand of his master. So if he says: 'He is my slave, I freed him,' he has established an additional qualification, so he establishes the wala' definitively. Thus they said. (2) His saying: 'One looks to the strength of the cause' – there is investigation into this. Indeed, looking to the strength of the cause is only when the claim is from one person. As for when it is from two persons, both proofs are accepted and they divide between them, and one does not look to the strength of the cause, as is clearly stated in al-Hidayah and its commentaries. The face of the difference: When they claim purchase and gift from one person, they do not need to prove ownership for the one who owned it, for it is established by their mutual agreement. The only barrier is in proving the cause of ownership upon him and in proving ownership for themselves; purchase is stronger than gift because it is a mutual exchange from both sides, and because it establishes ownership for himself, while ownership in gift depends on taking possession.
Cause
And when they claim purchase and gift from two persons who owned it, they need to prove ownership from their ownership, and each one becomes a litigant against the one from whom he claims ownership, first to prove ownership for him and then for himself. (1) And similar is in 'Idah al-Islah' (fol. 121b).
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on al-Wiqayah, deals with the rules of evidence (bayyinah) when two people claim the same property. It is a ruling statement that explains various scenarios of preference between claimants. The discussion is part of the Book of Claims (Kitab al-Da'wa).

The passage begins with the general rule: if both claimants present proof (bayyinah), the one with the earlier date on his document is entitled. If dates are equal or lacking, the principle depends on possession (yad) and the type of ownership claimed.

For absolute ownership (milk mutlaq) without reference to a specific cause: if both are in possession, they are equal; if both are outsiders (not in possession), they are equal; if one is in possession and the other is an outsider, the outsider is preferred. This is based on the idea that the possessor is already enjoying the property, so the outsider's proof is given precedence to possibly displace him. However, there is an exception: if the claim includes a dispositive act (like freeing or tadbir) in addition to ownership, then the possessor is preferred because he has additional evidence of his right.

The text then addresses claims involving a specific cause of ownership (milk bi sabab), such as purchase or gift. If both claimants trace their ownership to the same person, the possessor is preferred if he is the one in possession; if they receive from two different persons, the outsider is preferred when dates are equal. If they mention two different causes, the strength of the cause is considered. However, a later commentary points out that this rule of looking to the strength of the cause applies only when the claim is from a single person, not when two are claiming from two different owners; in that case both proofs are accepted and the property is divided.

Special cases are discussed regarding slaves: if both claim to have made a kitabah contract, they are equal because the mukatab slave is free in terms of possession (no hand over him), so both are outsiders. But if one claims kitabah and the other claims tadbir or i'taq, the latter is preferred because tadbir and i'taq are stronger proofs—they are binding dispositions that do not require the slave's acceptance and confirm the master's control (yad) over the slave.

The commentary also addresses an objection: why is there no hand over a mukatab but there is over a freed slave? The answer is that kitabah is a commutative contract requiring acceptance, so the slave gains independence, while freeing and tadbir are unilateral acts; the slave may still be physically in the master's hand if he is young or unaware.

Finally, the passage distinguishes between claims from one transferor and claims from two different transferors. In the latter case, each claimant must prove ownership for his transferor first, and then for himself, so the strength of cause is not considered; instead both proofs are accepted and the property is divided equally.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Legal proof or evidence presented by a claimant to support his claim.
يد (yad): Possession or physical control over property, which gives a presumption of ownership.
ملك مطلق (milk mutlaq): Absolute ownership without reference to a specific cause of acquisition.
ملك بسبب (milk bi sabab): Ownership based on a specific cause such as purchase, gift, inheritance, etc.
كتابة (kitabah): A contract of manumission where a slave purchases his freedom on agreed terms.
تدبير (tadbir): Declaring a slave to be free upon the master's death (mudabbar).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Zayd and Amr both claim ownership of a house. Zayd is living in the house (possessor) and presents a deed of purchase dated 2020. Amr is an outsider and presents a deed of gift (hiba) dated 2020, both from different owners. According to the Hanafi position in this passage, how does the court decide?
A) Zayd wins because he is in possession.
B) Amr wins because the outsider is preferred in absolute ownership when dates are equal.
C) Both proofs are accepted and the house is divided equally between them.
D) The stronger cause (purchase) is considered, so Zayd wins.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, preference of bayyinah in competing claims depends on possession, type of ownership, date, and whether a dispositive act is claimed; the strength of cause is only considered when both trace to the same owner.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
8 min
Preference in Conflicting Claims: The Half/All House Case
Explains rules of preference when two claimants present evidence on ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of tarjih bi al-wasf to the case of competing ownership claims on a house and state the intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab positions with their practical differences.

Core Idea
Preference in evidence is by strength, not number; in the half/all claim, Imam Abu Hanifa gives the claimant of all 3/4 due to the undisputed half.

This lesson explores how a Hanafi judge decides between two claimants who both present valid evidence. The key principle is that preference (tarjih) is based on the strength of the proof, not the quantity of witnesses. We then examine a specific dispute where one outsider claims a whole house and another claims half, with each having evidence. How should the house be divided? The Hanafi school has an internal difference, and other schools offer alternative solutions. Understanding this case reveals the practical impact of each school's methodology in handling conflicting evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi general rule
Identify the principle that preference is by strength (wasf) not number (adad). Understand why quantity of witnesses does not outweigh quality.
This is based on the idea that strong evidence stands on its own; adding weak evidence does not strengthen it.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute in the half/all case
Recall the positions: Imam Abu Hanifa gives the claimant of all 3/4 and the claimant of half 1/4. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) give 2/3 and 1/3. Understand the reasoning behind each: Imam looks at undisputed share and then equal division; Sahibayn apply 'awl (proportional reduction).
The term 'awl is borrowed from inheritance law where shares are proportionally reduced when the sum exceeds the whole.
3
Compare with the Shafi'i and Maliki positions
Note that Shafi'i (in one view) nullifies both evidences (tahatur) and lets the possessor keep the house after oath. Malik agrees with the Sahibayn. Recognize the thamarah al-khilaf: the practical outcome differs significantly.
Tahatur means both evidences cancel out, leading to a default return to the status quo (possession).
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to tahatur
Understand why Hanafis reject complete nullification: evidence should be given effect if possible. The Hanafi method gives partial effect to both claims, preserving some value of each proof.
This reflects a general Hanafi tendency to avoid setting aside evidence when a compromise is possible.
5
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Consider a case where one claimant presents two witnesses and the other presents three. Per the Hanafi rule, the number does not matter; the judge must evaluate the strength (e.g., reliability, consistency) of each witness.
Strength can come from the witness's integrity, directness of testimony, or corroboration through circumstantial evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى باب دعوى الرجلين

بخلاف ما إذا قال : كلُّ واحد هو عبدي كاتبته ، فهما سواء لأنهما خارجان إذ لا يد على المكاتب، ولو قال أحدهما : هو عبدي كاتبته، وقال الآخر : دبرته ، أو اعتقته، فهذا أولى. فالضابط أنَّ كلَّ بينةٍ تكون أكثر إثباتاً ، فهي أحق ، هذا في الخارج وذي اليد في الملك المطلق، وأما في الملكِ بسبب، فإن ذَكَرَا سبباً واحداً، فإن تلقيا من واحدٍ، فذو اليد أحق، وإن تلقيا من اثنين فالخارج أحق شاملاً للصور المذكورة، وإن ذَكَرَا سببين كالشراء، والهبة، وغير ذلك، يُنظر إلى قوة السبب كما في «المتن».

[1] قوله : إذ لا يد على المكاتب ؛ أوردَ عليه : إنَّ عدم اليد في العتق المطلق أظهر منه في المكاتب. وأجيب عنه : بأنَّ الكتابة عقد معاوضة، فلا بُدَّ من أهلية العاقدين وقبولهما، فإذا عقدها يكون معتقاً يداً، فلا يتصور اليد عليه، فمن قال : إنه عبدي كاتبته، فقد اعترف أنه لا يد عليه، ويكون خارجاً بالضرورة سواء كان مراد المدعي نفسه أو ولاؤه ، بخلاف التدبير والإعتاق، فإنَّ كلاً منهما تصرُّفٌ لازم، فلا يستدعي قبول العبد له. ويجوز أن يكون العبد صغيراً يكون في يد مولاه أو كبيراً لا يعرف عنقه، فيكون في يد مولاه، كما إذا كان، فإذا قال : هو عبدي أعتقتُه ، فقد أثبت قيداً زائداً، فيثبت له الولاية قطعاً، هكذا قالوا.

[2] قوله : ينظر إلى قوة السبب ؛ فيه بحث، فإنّ النظر إلى قوة السبب إنما يكون إذا كانت الدعوى من واحد، وأما إذا كانت من اثنين تقبل البيِّنتان وينصفُ بينهما، ولا ينظر إلى قوة السبب. كما صرح به في «الهداية» وشروحها. ووجه الفرق : إنهما إذا ادّعيا الشراء والهبة من واحدٍ لا يحتاجان إلى إثبات الملك لمن ملكها، فإنّه ثابت بتصادقهما ، وإنّما الحاجزُ في إثبات سبب الملك عليه، وفي إثباتِ الملك لنفسها، الشراء أقوى من الهبة ؛ لكونه معاوضة من الجانبين ؛ ولأنه يثبتُ الملك لنفسه، والملك في الهبة يتوقف على القبض. وأما إذا ادعيا الشراء والهبة من اثنين لهما محتاجان إلى إثباتِ الملك عن ملكهما، وينتصب كل واحد منهما خصماً عمّن ملكه في إثبات الملك له أولاً ثم لنفسه، والحجّتان في إثباتِ الملك بهما سواء، فيقضى به بينهما. كذلك كما صرحوا به.

ولا يرجح بكثرة الشهود : فإنّ الترجيح عندنا بقوة الدليل لا بكثرته. (ولو ادعى أحد خارجين نصف دار ، والآخر كلها ، فالربع للأول، وقالا : الثلث، والباقي للثاني)

[1] قوله : ولا يرجح بكثرة الشهود؛ يعني أنّ أحد الخصمين إذا أقام شاهدين ، والآخر أكثر لا يرجح بكثرة شهوده ؛ لأنَّ الترجيح يكون لقوَّة في العلة لا بكثرة العلل ؛ لأنَّ ما يصلح دليلاً مستقلاً لا يصلح للترجيح. وإنما يرجح بالوصف ؛ ولهذا لا ترجّح الآية بآية، ولا خبر بخبر، وإنما يرجح بالقوة، بأن كان أحدهما متواتراً، والآخرُ من الآحاد أو كان أحدهما مفسراً والآخرُ مجملاً، فيرجح المفسر على المجمل، والمتواتر على الآحاد ؛ لقوة وصف فيه، وكذا لا يرجح قياس بقياس لما ذكرنا.

[2] قوله : ولو ادعى...الخ ؛ توضيح الكلام بحيث ينكشف به المرام : إِنَّ الدَّارَ إِذا كانت في يد رجل ادعاها اثنان أحدهما جميعها ، والآخر نصفها ، وأقام كلُّ واحدٍ منهما البينة على ما ادعاه ، فلصاحب الجميع ثلاثة أرباع الدار، ولصاحب النصف ربعها. وهذا عند الإمام اعتباراً لطريق المنازعة ؛ لأنَّ مدعي الكل لا ينازعه أحد في فيُسلم له من غير منازعة ، ثمَّ استوت منازعتهما في النصف الآخر، فيكون النصف بينهما ، فسلّم لمدعي الكلِّ ثلاثة أرباعها، ولمدعي النصف سلّم ربعها. وقالا : لمدعي الكل تلثاها، ولمدّعي النصف: ثلثها ؛ لأن مدّعي الكلّ يضرب بكلّها، ومدعي النصف بنصفها بطريق العول، وبه قال مالك له في رواية، وعند الشافعي له في قول : تهاترت البينتان ويحلف ذو اليد كقابض لهما، فإذا حلف بقي في يد ذي اليد.

واعلم أن جنس القسمة على أربعة أنواع : أحدها : ما يقسم بطريق العولِ إجماعا، وهي : ثمان مسائل : الميراث، والديون،
Book of Lawsuit, Chapter of the Claim of Two Men. Contrary to when he says: 'Each one is my slave whom I have made a mukatab,' so they are equal because both are outsiders since there is no hand (control) over a mukatab. If one says: 'He is my slave whom I have made a mukatab,' and the other says: 'I made him mudabbar' or 'I freed him,' then this (latter) is more preferable. The rule is that every evidence that is more establishing is more deserving. This applies to an outsider and the possessor in absolute ownership. As for ownership due to a cause, if they mention a single cause, and they received it from one person, then the possessor is more deserving; if they received it from two persons, then the outsider is more deserving, including the mentioned scenarios. If they mention two causes like purchase, gift, etc., one looks to the strength of the cause, as in the Matn. [1] His saying: 'since there is no hand over a mukatab'; objection: 'The absence of hand in absolute manumission is more apparent than in the mukatab.' Answer: Kitabah is a contract of exchange, so it requires capacity of the contracting parties and acceptance. When he contracts it, he becomes like a freed person in terms of hand, so hand over him is inconceivable. So whoever says 'he is my slave whom I made a mukatab' has admitted that he has no hand over him, and he is necessarily an outsider, whether the claimant means himself or his wala'. Unlike tadbir and itq, because each is a binding disposition that does not require the slave's acceptance. It is possible that the slave is a minor in his master's hand, or an adult who does not know his neck, thus in his master's hand, as if he were. So when he says 'he is my slave whom I freed,' he has proven an additional condition, so wala' is established for him definitively. Thus they said. [2] His saying: 'one looks to the strength of the cause'; there is discussion, because looking to the strength of the cause applies only when the claim is from one person. As for when it is from two persons, both evidences are accepted and they divide equally, and one does not look to strength of cause. As clarified in al-Hidayah and its commentaries. The difference is: if both claim purchase and gift from one person, they do not need to prove ownership of the one who owned it, for it is established by their mutual agreement. The only need is to prove the cause of ownership upon him, and in proving ownership for themselves, purchase is stronger than gift because it is a mutual exchange and because it establishes ownership for oneself, while ownership in gift depends on possession. If they claim purchase and gift from two persons, they need to prove ownership from their owners, and each becomes a litigant against the one from whom he claims ownership, first for establishing ownership for him, then for himself. The two proofs in establishing ownership are equal, so judgment is given between them. Thus they clarified. (And preference is not by multitude of witnesses): for preference with us is by strength of evidence, not by its multitude. (If one of two outsiders claims half a house, and the other claims all of it, then a quarter is for the first, and they said: a third, and the rest for the second). [1] His saying: 'and preference is not by multitude of witnesses' means that if one litigant produces two witnesses and the other more, he is not given preference by his many witnesses, because preference is for strength in the cause, not by multitude of causes, because that which is suitable as independent proof is not suitable for preference. Preference is only by description; hence one verse is not preferred over another, nor one hadith over another. Preference is only by strength, such as one being mutawatir and the other ahad, or one being mufassar and the other mujmal, so the mufassar is preferred over the mujmal, and the mutawatir over the ahad, due to strength of description. Likewise, one qiyas is not preferred over another for what we mentioned. [2] His saying: 'and if one claims...' etc.; clarification of the speech such that the intended meaning is revealed: If a house is in the hand of a man, and two persons claim it, one claiming all of it and the other half of it, and each establishes evidence for what he claims, then the claimant of all gets three-quarters of the house, and the claimant of half gets one-quarter. This is according to Imam Abu Hanifa, considering the path of dispute, because the claimant of all is not disputed by anyone in half? so it is given to him without dispute, then their dispute is equal in the other half, so the half is between them. So the claimant of all gets three-quarters, and the claimant of half gets one-quarter. They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: the claimant of all gets two-thirds, and the claimant of half gets one-third, because the claimant of all strikes with all, and the claimant of half strikes with half by way of 'awl (proportional reduction). Malik also said this in one narration. According to al-Shafi'i, in one opinion: both evidences are nullified and the possessor takes an oath as a possessor for both, and if he swears, it remains in the hand of the possessor. Know that the types of division are four: One of them: what is divided by way of 'awl by consensus, and there are eight issues: inheritance and debts...
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh of Al-Wiqayah discusses rules of preference in property claims. It opens with a ruling: if both claimants claim the slave as a mukatab, they are equal because neither has hand over the mukatab. If one claims mukatab and the other claims tadbir or itq, the latter is preferred because those are stronger dispositions. The author then states the general principle: the evidence that is more establishing (more conclusive) is preferred. This applies differently depending on whether the dispute is over absolute ownership or ownership by a cause. When a specific cause is mentioned, if both received from one person, the one in possession is preferred; if from two, the outsider is preferred. If different causes (e.g., purchase vs. gift) are claimed, the stronger cause is considered. The commentary then addresses two specific points from the matn: (1) the objection about mukatab and its answer, clarifying that kitabah is a contract requiring acceptance, unlike tadbir and itq. (2) The ruling that when two claimants each present evidence from different owners, both evidences are accepted and they divide equally; the strength of the cause is not considered because the ownership must first be proven from the previous owners. The text then explicitly states that preference is not by number of witnesses, but by strength of evidence. It gives the example of two outsiders claiming a house: one claims all, the other half. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the one claiming all gets three-quarters, the other gets one-quarter (because half is undisputed and given to the claimant of all, then the other half is divided equally). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say two-thirds and one-third by 'awl. Shafi'i has a view that both evidences cancel out and the possessor's oath prevails. The passage concludes by noting that division by 'awl is agreed upon for inheritance and debts.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence or proof presented by a litigant, typically through witnesses or documents, to establish a claim.
ترجيح (tarjih): Preference given to one evidence over another due to its strength or conclusiveness, not due to quantity.
عول ('awl): Proportional reduction of shares when the total sum of prescribed shares exceeds the whole, used in inheritance and here in claims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if one outsider claims all of a house and another claims half, both with evidence, what share does the claimant of half receive?
A) One quarter
B) One third
C) One half
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when conflicting claims arise, the judge must prefer the stronger evidence, not the more numerous; the half/all house case shows how this principle leads to a specific division that differs from other schools.

Preference by Strength, Not Number: The Hanafi Principle of Tarjih in Testimony
Establishment of the principle that tarjih is based on quality, not quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that preference (tarjih) in testimony is based on the strength of evidence (quwwa), not the number of witnesses, and identify the criteria for tarjih (e.g., mutawatir vs ahad, mufassar vs mujmal).

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, more witnesses do not outweigh fewer if the evidence is equal in strength — quality trumps quantity.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah establishes a fundamental principle of judicial evidence: the judge must base his preference on the intrinsic strength of the evidence (such as whether it is mutawatir, mufassar, etc.), not on how many witnesses present it. This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on the quality (wasf) of proof, and the commentator provides clear examples to illustrate the rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The text explicitly states: 'ولا يرجح بكثرة الشهود' (preference is not given by the number of witnesses). This is the core rule.
2
Understand the rationale
Preference is by strength (quwwa), not quantity. The commentator explains that evidence suitable as an independent proof is not suitable for preference; rather, preference is by the description (wasf).
3
Examine the examples
The commentator gives examples: mutawatir is preferred over ahad, mufassar over mujmal—because the description of the evidence is stronger in one than the other.
4
Apply the principle to qiyas
Similarly, one analogy (qiyas) is not preferred over another merely by number; the strength of the reasoning matters.

(ولا يرجح بكثرة الشهود) : فإنّ الترجيح عندنا بقوة الدليل لا بكثرته. (ولو ادعى أحد خارجين نصف دار ، والآخر كلها ، فالربع الأول، وقالا : الثلث، والباقي للثاني)
والحجّتان في إثباتِ الملك بهما سواء، فيقضى به بينهما. كذلك كما صرحوا به. 11 ] قوله : ولا يرجح بكثرة الشهود؛ يعني أنّ أحد الخصمين إذا أقام شاهدين ، والآخر أكثر لا يرجح بكثرة شهوده ؛ لأنَّ الترجيح يكون لقوَّة في العلة لا بكثرة العلل ؛ لأنَّ ما يصلح دليلاً مستقلاً لا يصلح للترجيح.
وإنما يرجح بالوصف ؛ ولهذا لا ترجّح الآية بآية، ولا خبر بخبر، وإنما يرجح بالقوة، بأن كان أحدهما متواتراً، والآخرُ من الآحاد أو كان أحدهما مفسراً والآخرُ مجملاً، فيرجح المفسر على المجمل، والمتواتر على الآحاد ؛ لقوة وصف فيه، وكذا لا يرجح قياس بقياس لما ذكرنا.

وهذا عند الإمام اعتباراً لطريق المنازعة ؛ لأنَّ مدعي الكل لا ينازعه أحد في فيُسلم له من غير منازعة ، ثمَّ استوت منازعتهما في النصف الآخر، فيكون النصف، بينهما ، فسلّم لمدعي الكلِّ ثلاثة أرباعها، ولمدعي النصف سلّم ربعها. وقالا : لمدعي الكل تلثاها، ولمدّعي النصف: ثلثها ؛ لأن مدّعي الكلّ يضرب بكلّها، ومدعي النصف بنصفها بطريق العول، وبه قال مالك له في رواية، وعند الشافعي له في قول : تهاترت البينتان ويحلف ذو اليد كقابض لهما، فإذا حلف بقي في يد ذي اليد.
واعلم أن جنس القسمة على أربعة أنواع : أحدها : ما يقسم بطريق العولِ إجماعا، وهي : ثمان مسائل : الميراث، والديون، والوصية بما دون الثلث إذا اجتمعت وزادت على الثلث، والمحاباة، والدراهم المرسلة: يعني التي لم يذكر فيها تسقط من نصف أو ثلث أو ربع أو سدس، والسعاية بدينهم، والعبد إذا قلع عين رجل وقتل آخر خطأ، فدفع بهما، والمدير إذا جنى على هذا الوجه. والثاني : ما يقسم بطريق المنازعة إجماعاً ، وهي مسألة واحدة، فضولي باع عبداً لغيره من رجل آخر، وفضولي آخر باغ نصفه من آخر، فأجاز المولى البيعين، فاختار المشتري الأخذ، يكون لمشتري الكل ثلاثة أرباعه، ولمشتري النصف الربع بطريق المنازعة.
والثالث : ما يقسم بطريق المنازعة عند أبي حنيفة ، وعندهما: بطريق العول، وهي : ثلاثة مسائل : أحدها : مسألة الكتاب. وهي . والثانية : إذا أوصى لرجل بجميع ماله والآخرُ بنصف ماله، وأجازه الورثة. والثالثة : إذا أوصى لرجل بعبد بعينه، والآخرُ بنصف ذلك العبد، وليس له مال غيره.

والخامسة : أم ولد قتلت مولاها وأجنبياً عمداً ، ولكلِّ منهما وليان، فعفى أحد
The cream of the final goal for the support of the care. And preference is not given by the number of witnesses. If one of two claimants claims half a house, and the other claims the whole, then a quarter is for the first, and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: a third, and the rest for the second. (And preference is not given by the number of witnesses): for preference with us is by the strength of evidence, not by its abundance. (If one of two claimants claims half a house, and the other claims the whole, then a quarter is for the first, and the two said: a third, and the rest for the second). The two proofs (evidences) are equal in establishing ownership, so judgment is given between them accordingly. That is as they have stated. [11] His statement: and preference is not given by the number of witnesses; meaning that if one of the two litigants produces two witnesses, and the other produces more, one does not prefer by the number of his witnesses; because preference is due to strength in the cause, not by the number of causes; because that which is suitable as an independent proof is not suitable for preference. Preference is only by the description; and for this reason one verse is not preferred over another verse, nor one report over another report; rather preference is by strength, as when one of them is mutawatir and the other is ahad, or one is mufassar (explicit) and the other is mujmal (general); the explicit is preferred over the general, and the mutawatir over the ahad, due to the strength of the description in it. Similarly, an analogy (qiyas) is not preferred over another analogy for the reason we mentioned. [2] His statement: if one claims...etc; clarification of the speech so that the intent is revealed: Indeed, if a house is in the hand of a man, and two men claim it, one claiming all of it and the other claiming half of it, and each of them establishes the evidence for what he claimed, then the claimant of the whole gets three-quarters of the house, and the claimant of the half gets a quarter of it. This is according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa), by consideration of the method of contentious division (munaza'ah); because the claimant of the whole is not contested by anyone in that part, so it is conceded to him without contest, then their contest is equal in the other half, so the half is between them. Thus three-quarters are conceded to the claimant of the whole, and a quarter is conceded to the claimant of the half. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): the claimant of the whole gets two-thirds, and the claimant of the half gets one-third; because the claimant of the whole claims the whole, and the claimant of the half claims half, by way of proportional allocation ('awl). And Malik said this in one narration. And according to al-Shafi'i, in one saying: the two evidences cancel each other (tahaarat al-bayyinatan) and the possessor (dhul yad) takes an oath as if he were a possessor for both; if he swears, the property remains in his hand. Know that the categories of division are of four types: First: what is divided by the method of 'awl by consensus, and these are eight matters: inheritance, debts, bequest of less than a third when they meet and exceed a third, favor (muhabah), unspecified dirhams (meaning those in which it is not mentioned to fall from half or third or quarter or sixth), the endeavor (sa'ayah) with their debt, the slave who gouges out the eye of a man and kills another by mistake, and he is handed over for both, and the mudabbar (slave whose freedom is deferred after death) if he commits an injury in this manner. Second: what is divided by the method of munaza'ah by consensus, and it is one case: a person without authorization (fuduli) sells a slave belonging to someone else to a man, and another fuduli sells half of that slave to another, then the master ratifies both sales, and the buyer chooses to take, then the buyer of the whole gets three-quarters, and the buyer of the half gets a quarter by the method of munaza'ah. Third: what is divided by the method of munaza'ah according to Abu Hanifa, and according to the two by the method of 'awl, and these are three cases: one of them is the case of the book (the house case). The second: if a man bequeaths all his property to one person and half his property to another, and the heirs ratify. The third: if a man bequeaths a specific slave to one person and half of that slave to another, and he has no other property. Fourth: what is divided by the method of 'awl according to Abu Hanifa, and according to the two by the method of munaza'ah, five cases: First: a slave permitted to trade (ma'dhun lahu fi al-tijarah) between two men: one creditor lends him a hundred, and a third party lends him a hundred; then the debt of the master drops by half because it is impossible for a debt to be owed to the slave, and half (of the master's debt) remains in the share of his partner; so when the slave is sold for the debt, they divide the price according to the difference we mentioned. Second: if two third parties lend him a hundred and two hundred respectively, they divide his price accordingly. Third: a slave kills a man by mistake and another by intent; the intentional victim has two guardians (walis); one of them pardons; then the slave is handed over for both; the proceeds are between them according to the difference. Fourth: if the injurer is a mudabbar in this manner, and his value is paid, it is between them likewise. Fifth: an umm walad (mother of a child) kills her master and a third party intentionally, each of them has two guardians; one of them pardons...
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage from the sharh (commentary) of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on Al-Wiqayah addresses two key issues: first, the principle that in judicial testimony, preference (tarjih) is based on the strength of evidence, not the number of witnesses. The commentator explains that evidence that is stronger in description (such as mutawatir over ahad, or explicit over general) is given preference, but mere quantity does not weigh. This is a rational (dalil 'aqli) argument. The second and main part presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the allocation of a jointly claimed property when one claimant claims the whole and the other claims half, and both present equal evidence. According to Imam Abu Hanifa (the Imam), the property is divided using the method of munaza'ah (contentious division): the claimant of the whole is conceded the half that is uncontested, leaving the other half to be divided equally, resulting in three-quarters for the whole-claimant and one-quarter for the half-claimant. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) use the method of 'awl (proportional allocation), where the claims are totaled and then reduced proportionally: the whole claim (1) plus half claim (1/2) gives 3/2 as denominator, so the whole-claimant gets 1 / (3/2) = 2/3, the half-claimant gets (1/2) / (3/2) = 1/3. Both methods are valid Hanafi positions, but the text goes on to classify all cases of division into four categories: (1) cases where all agree on 'awl, (2) one case where all agree on munaza'ah, (3) three cases where Abu Hanifa uses munaza'ah and the two use 'awl, and (4) five cases where Abu Hanifa uses 'awl and the two use munaza'ah. The commentator provides examples for each category, including inheritance, debt, bequests, sales by unauthorized agents (fuduli), and injuries by slaves. The discussion reflects the Hanafi approach to resolving conflicting claims based on the nature of the contest and the strength of the legal reasoning (talil).

Key Terms
ترجيح (tarjīḥ): Preference given to one piece of evidence over another based on strength, not quantity.
عول (ʿawl): Proportional reduction: a method of dividing a limited fund among multiple claimants by reducing shares proportionally to match the total.
منازعة (munāzaʿa): Contentious division: a method where the uncontested portion is given to the claimant with priority, and the contested portion is divided equally among those contesting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle taught in this lesson, what is the basis for preferring one party's evidence over another's?
A) The number of witnesses
B) The strength of the evidence's description (wasf)
C) The wealth of the party
D) The gender of the witnesses

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, preference among conflicting evidences is determined by the strength of the description (wasf), not by the number of witnesses.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Division of Joint Claims: Munaza'ah vs 'Awl
Intra-Hanafi disagreement on allocation methodology for joint claims with equal evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the methods of munaza'ah and 'awl in dividing joint claims, apply them to the case where one claimant claims the whole and another claims half, and classify division cases into the four categories described in the passage.

Core Idea
When two claimants present equal evidence, division follows either contentious division (munaza'ah) or proportional allocation ('awl), leading to different shares — a legitimate intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, on how to divide a jointly claimed property when one party claims the whole and the other claims half. Abu Hanifa uses the method of munaza'ah (contentious division), while the Sahibayn use 'awl (proportional reduction). The commentator also provides a detailed classification of all division cases into four categories based on consensus or disagreement, showing the practical scope of the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Set up the case
Two claimants: one claims the whole house (1), the other claims half (1/2). Both have equal evidence. The house is in the possession of a third party? Actually, the text says 'الدار في يد رجل' but the dispute is between the two claimants, not the possessor. For division, the focus is on allocation between claimants.
2
Apply Abu Hanifa's method (munaza'ah)
The claimant of the whole is uncontested in half the property, so that half is conceded to him. The remaining half is contested equally, so it is split equally between both claimants. Thus, whole-claimant gets 1/2 + 1/4 = 3/4; half-claimant gets 1/4.
3
Apply the Sahibayn's method ('awl)
Total claims = 1 (whole) + 1/2 (half) = 3/2. Each gets a proportion: whole-claimant gets 1 / (3/2) = 2/3; half-claimant gets (1/2) / (3/2) = 1/3.
4
Understand the difference
Munaza'ah prioritizes the uncontested portion; 'awl treats all claims as competing shares in a limited fund. The disagreement stems from different views on the nature of the claim.
5
Classify cases
The commentator divides all division cases into four types: (1) 'awl by consensus (8 cases), (2) munaza'ah by consensus (1 case), (3) munaza'ah for Abu Hanifa and 'awl for the two (3 cases), (4) 'awl for Abu Hanifa and munaza'ah for the two (5 cases).
