والثالث : ما يقسم بطريق المنازعة عند أبي حنيفة ، وعندهما: بطريق العول، وهي : ثلاثة مسائل : أحدها : مسألة الكتاب. وهي . والثانية : إذا أوصى لرجل بجميع ماله والآخرُ بنصف ماله، وأجازه الورثة. والثالثة : إذا أوصى لرجل بعبد بعينه، والآخرُ بنصف ذلك العبد، وليس له مال غيره.
والرابع : ما يقسم بطريق العولِ عند أبي حنيفة ، وعندهما: بطريق المنازعة، خمس مسائل :
أحدها : عبد مأذون له في التجارة بين رجل أدانه أحدهما مئة، وأدانه أجنبي مئة، فدين المولى يسقط نصفه ؛ لاستحالة وجوب الدين على عبده ، وثبت نصفه الذي في نصيب شريكه، فإذا بيع بالدين يقسمان الثمن على الخلاف الذي ذكرناه. والثانية : إذا أدائه أجنبيان أحدهما ،مئة ، والآخر مئتين يقتسمان ثمنه على هذا. والثالثة : عبد قتل رجلاً خطأ، والآخر عمداً للمقتول عمداً وليان، فعفى أحدهما فدفع بهما، كان بينهما على الخلاف. والرابعة : لو كان الجاني مدبّراً على هذا الوجه، فدفعت قيمته كانت بينهما كذلك.
والخامسة : أم ولد قتلت مولاها وأجنبياً عمداً ، ولكلِّ منهما وليان، فعفى أحد
The cream of the final goal for the support of the care. And preference is not given by the number of witnesses. If one of two claimants claims half a house, and the other claims the whole, then a quarter is for the first, and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: a third, and the rest for the second. (And preference is not given by the number of witnesses): for preference with us is by the strength of evidence, not by its abundance. (If one of two claimants claims half a house, and the other claims the whole, then a quarter is for the first, and the two said: a third, and the rest for the second). The two proofs (evidences) are equal in establishing ownership, so judgment is given between them accordingly. That is as they have stated. [11] His statement: and preference is not given by the number of witnesses; meaning that if one of the two litigants produces two witnesses, and the other produces more, one does not prefer by the number of his witnesses; because preference is due to strength in the cause, not by the number of causes; because that which is suitable as an independent proof is not suitable for preference. Preference is only by the description; and for this reason one verse is not preferred over another verse, nor one report over another report; rather preference is by strength, as when one of them is mutawatir and the other is ahad, or one is mufassar (explicit) and the other is mujmal (general); the explicit is preferred over the general, and the mutawatir over the ahad, due to the strength of the description in it. Similarly, an analogy (qiyas) is not preferred over another analogy for the reason we mentioned. [2] His statement: if one claims...etc; clarification of the speech so that the intent is revealed: Indeed, if a house is in the hand of a man, and two men claim it, one claiming all of it and the other claiming half of it, and each of them establishes the evidence for what he claimed, then the claimant of the whole gets three-quarters of the house, and the claimant of the half gets a quarter of it. This is according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa), by consideration of the method of contentious division (munaza'ah); because the claimant of the whole is not contested by anyone in that part, so it is conceded to him without contest, then their contest is equal in the other half, so the half is between them. Thus three-quarters are conceded to the claimant of the whole, and a quarter is conceded to the claimant of the half. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): the claimant of the whole gets two-thirds, and the claimant of the half gets one-third; because the claimant of the whole claims the whole, and the claimant of the half claims half, by way of proportional allocation ('awl). And Malik said this in one narration. And according to al-Shafi'i, in one saying: the two evidences cancel each other (tahaarat al-bayyinatan) and the possessor (dhul yad) takes an oath as if he were a possessor for both; if he swears, the property remains in his hand. Know that the categories of division are of four types: First: what is divided by the method of 'awl by consensus, and these are eight matters: inheritance, debts, bequest of less than a third when they meet and exceed a third, favor (muhabah), unspecified dirhams (meaning those in which it is not mentioned to fall from half or third or quarter or sixth), the endeavor (sa'ayah) with their debt, the slave who gouges out the eye of a man and kills another by mistake, and he is handed over for both, and the mudabbar (slave whose freedom is deferred after death) if he commits an injury in this manner. Second: what is divided by the method of munaza'ah by consensus, and it is one case: a person without authorization (fuduli) sells a slave belonging to someone else to a man, and another fuduli sells half of that slave to another, then the master ratifies both sales, and the buyer chooses to take, then the buyer of the whole gets three-quarters, and the buyer of the half gets a quarter by the method of munaza'ah. Third: what is divided by the method of munaza'ah according to Abu Hanifa, and according to the two by the method of 'awl, and these are three cases: one of them is the case of the book (the house case). The second: if a man bequeaths all his property to one person and half his property to another, and the heirs ratify. The third: if a man bequeaths a specific slave to one person and half of that slave to another, and he has no other property. Fourth: what is divided by the method of 'awl according to Abu Hanifa, and according to the two by the method of munaza'ah, five cases: First: a slave permitted to trade (ma'dhun lahu fi al-tijarah) between two men: one creditor lends him a hundred, and a third party lends him a hundred; then the debt of the master drops by half because it is impossible for a debt to be owed to the slave, and half (of the master's debt) remains in the share of his partner; so when the slave is sold for the debt, they divide the price according to the difference we mentioned. Second: if two third parties lend him a hundred and two hundred respectively, they divide his price accordingly. Third: a slave kills a man by mistake and another by intent; the intentional victim has two guardians (walis); one of them pardons; then the slave is handed over for both; the proceeds are between them according to the difference. Fourth: if the injurer is a mudabbar in this manner, and his value is paid, it is between them likewise. Fifth: an umm walad (mother of a child) kills her master and a third party intentionally, each of them has two guardians; one of them pardons...
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage from the sharh (commentary) of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on Al-Wiqayah addresses two key issues: first, the principle that in judicial testimony, preference (tarjih) is based on the strength of evidence, not the number of witnesses. The commentator explains that evidence that is stronger in description (such as mutawatir over ahad, or explicit over general) is given preference, but mere quantity does not weigh. This is a rational (dalil 'aqli) argument. The second and main part presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the allocation of a jointly claimed property when one claimant claims the whole and the other claims half, and both present equal evidence. According to Imam Abu Hanifa (the Imam), the property is divided using the method of munaza'ah (contentious division): the claimant of the whole is conceded the half that is uncontested, leaving the other half to be divided equally, resulting in three-quarters for the whole-claimant and one-quarter for the half-claimant. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) use the method of 'awl (proportional allocation), where the claims are totaled and then reduced proportionally: the whole claim (1) plus half claim (1/2) gives 3/2 as denominator, so the whole-claimant gets 1 / (3/2) = 2/3, the half-claimant gets (1/2) / (3/2) = 1/3. Both methods are valid Hanafi positions, but the text goes on to classify all cases of division into four categories: (1) cases where all agree on 'awl, (2) one case where all agree on munaza'ah, (3) three cases where Abu Hanifa uses munaza'ah and the two use 'awl, and (4) five cases where Abu Hanifa uses 'awl and the two use munaza'ah. The commentator provides examples for each category, including inheritance, debt, bequests, sales by unauthorized agents (fuduli), and injuries by slaves. The discussion reflects the Hanafi approach to resolving conflicting claims based on the nature of the contest and the strength of the legal reasoning (talil).

Key Terms
ترجيح (tarjīḥ): Preference given to one piece of evidence over another based on strength, not quantity.
عول (ʿawl): Proportional reduction: a method of dividing a limited fund among multiple claimants by reducing shares proportionally to match the total.
منازعة (munāzaʿa): Contentious division: a method where the uncontested portion is given to the claimant with priority, and the contested portion is divided equally among those contesting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if one claimant claims a whole house and another claims half, and both have equal evidence, what share does the half-claimant receive?
A) 1/3
B) 1/4
C) 1/2
D) 2/3

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the method of dividing joint claims depends on whether the uncontested portion is set aside (munaza'ah) or all claims are proportionally reduced ('awl), leading to a legitimate intra-school dispute.

Munaza'ah vs. 'Awl: Categories and Intra-Hanafi Dispute in Qisma
Classification of division cases with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the four categories of division (qisma) in Hanafi law, state the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on categories 3 and 4, and apply the principles of equality of strength and simultaneity to determine the method.

Core Idea
Equal strength in a common part triggers 'awl; distinction or different times triggers munaza'ah—but Sahibayn add simultaneity as criterion.

This passage from the Book of Claims (Kitab al-Da'wa) classifies cases of division (qisma) into categories, highlighting an important dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The dispute concerns whether division should be by munaza'ah (separate entitlement) or by 'awl (proportional reduction). Understanding the underlying principles—equality of rights and simultaneity—will enable you to predict the method in new cases.

Guided Path
1
Define the two methods
Identify munaza'ah (sequential satisfaction giving priority to one claimant) and 'awl (proportional reduction of shares when they exceed the whole). Note that both are valid methods but yield different shares.
Munaza'ah gives the first claimant his full right; 'awl equalizes all claims proportionally.
2
Recognize the four categories
The passage lists categories 2, 3, and 4 (category 1, 'awl by consensus, is not detailed here). Category 2 is unanimous munaza'ah. Categories 3 and 4 are disputed.
In category 2, the buyer of the whole gets 3/4, buyer of half gets 1/4 by munaza'ah.
3
State the intra-Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa holds munaza'ah for category 3 and 'awl for category 4. The two students reverse: 'awl for category 3 and munaza'ah for category 4.
This is a classic example of ijtihad within the Hanafi school.
4
Work through the examples
For category 3: bequest of all and half with heir consent. Under Abu Hanifa (munaza'ah): 3/4 and 1/4. Under Sahibayn ('awl): 2/3 and 1/3. For category 4, five cases: joint debts, homicide by slave/mudabbar/umm walad. Calculate shares according to each method.
Focus on the practical impact (thamarah al-khilaf) in each scenario.
5
Extract the underlying principles
Abu Hanifa's principle: 'awl when rights are equal in strength (both in dhimma or in a common part); munaza'ah when one right is distinguished. Sahibayn's principle: 'awl when rights are established simultaneously; munaza'ah when sequential or distinguished.
The origin of 'awl is inheritance (fara'id), where all shares are in a common part.

فيعطى الربع لشريك العافي آخراً والنصف للآخر بينه وبين شريك العافي أولاً أثلاثاً، ثلثاه لشريك العافي أولاً، والثلث للعافي آخراً عنده، وعندهما أرباعاً، ذكره العيني، والشمني، والزيلعي، وغيرهم في شروحهم وفتاواهم.
[1] قوله: اعتبر... الخ؛ اعلم أن الأصل عند الإمام أن القسمة متى وجبت لحق ثابت في الذمة، أو لحق ثابت في العين على وجه الشيوع في البعض دون الكل، كانت القسمة عولية، ومتى وجبت لحق ثابت على وجه التمييز أو كان حق أحدهما في البعض الشائع، وحق الآخر في الكل، كانت قسمة منازعة.
وعندهما: إن الحقين إذا ثبتا على وجه الشيوع في وقت واحد كانت القسمة على العول، وإذا ثبتا على التمييز أو في وقتين كانت على المنازعة. كذا في «كمال الدراية»، وغيرها.
وبيانه: إن الحقوق متى وجبت [في] الذمة فقد استوت في القوة؛ لأن الذمة متسعة، فيضرب كل واحد بجميع حقه في العين، وكذا إذا كان حق كل واحد في العين، لكن في الجزء الشائع، فقد استوت في القوة؛ لأن ما من جزء ثبت فيه حق أحدهما إلا وللآخر أن يزاحمه فيه فكانت الحقوق مستوية في القوة.
والأصل في قسمة العول الميراث، وفيه حق كل واحد منهم ثبت في البعض الشائع، فإذا ثبت الحقان على وجه التمييز لم يكن في معنى الميراث.
Book of Claims, Chapter of Claims of Two Men. And the bequest of less than one-third when they accumulate and exceed one-third, and the act of favoring (muhabah), and loose dirhams: meaning those in which it is not mentioned that they deduct half, third, quarter, or sixth, and the earning (sa'ayah) by their debt, and the slave who gouges out a man's eye and kills another by mistake, so he is handed over for both, and the mudabbar if he injures in this manner. And it is [?]. The second: if he bequeaths all his property to one man and half his property to another, and the heirs permit. The third: if he bequeaths a specific slave to one man and half of that slave to another, and he has no other property. The fourth: what is divided by way of 'awl according to Abu Hanifa, and according to them by way of munaza'ah, five cases: One: a slave authorized to trade, owned by two men, one of them lent him one hundred, and a stranger lent him one hundred, so the debt of the master drops by half due to the impossibility of the debt being obligatory upon his slave, and half of it is established in the share of his partner, so when the slave is sold for the debt, they divide the price according to the difference we mentioned. The second: if two strangers lent him, one one hundred and the other two hundred, they divide his price on this. The third: a slave kills one man by mistake and another intentionally; the intentionally killed person has two guardians, and one of them pardons, so he is handed over for both, it is between them according to the difference. The fourth: if the perpetrator was a mudabbar in this manner, and his value is paid, it is between them likewise. The fifth: an umm walad kills her master and a stranger intentionally, and each of them has two guardians, and one guardian of each of them pardons successively, so she serves three-quarters of her value, and it is divided between the silent ones. Then one quarter is given to the partner of the one who pardoned last, and half to the other between him and the partner of the one who pardoned first in thirds: two-thirds to the partner of the one who pardoned first, and one-third to the one who pardoned last according to him (Abu Hanifa), and according to them (the two) in fourths, mentioned by al-'Ayni, al-Shumunni, al-Zayla'i and others in their commentaries and fatwas. [1] His statement: 'He considered...' etc.; know that the principle according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa) is that when division becomes obligatory due to a right established in the dhimmah, or due to a right established in the 'ayn on the basis of common ownership in part without the whole, the division is by 'awl. And when it becomes obligatory due to a right established on the basis of distinction (tamyiz) or when one's right is in a part in common and the other's right is in the whole, the division is by munaza'ah. And according to them (the two): when two rights are established on the basis of common ownership at one time, the division is by 'awl, and when they are established on the basis of distinction or at two times, the division is by munaza'ah. This is in 'Kmal al-Dirayah' and others. Its explanation: When rights become obligatory in the dhimmah, they are equal in strength because the dhimmah is capacious; so each one strikes with all his right in the 'ayn. Similarly, if each one's right is in the 'ayn but in the common part, they are equal in strength because there is no part in which one's right is established except that the other can compete with him in it; so the rights are equal in strength. The origin of division by 'awl is inheritance, and in it each one's right is established in a common part. So if two rights are established on the basis of distinction, it is not similar to inheritance.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Claims (Kitab al-Da'wa) discusses the rules of division (qisma) when multiple parties have competing rights over a single asset, such as a slave or property. The author categorizes cases into four types, but this window begins from the second type. The second type: cases where all jurists agree division is by munaza'ah—meaning each claimant's share is determined separately, with the first claimant taking his full right first. Example: two fuduli (unauthorized agents) sell a slave, one sells the whole, the other half; when the owner ratifies and the buyer chooses to take, the buyer of the whole gets 3/4 and the buyer of half gets 1/4. The third type: cases where Abu Hanifa holds munaza'ah but the two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold 'awl (proportional reduction). These include testamentary dispositions exceeding one-third when heirs consent. The fourth type: the opposite—Abu Hanifa holds 'awl, the students hold munaza'ah, with five examples involving joint ownership of a trading slave, debts by co-owners and strangers, homicide by a slave or mudabbar, and an umm walad killing her master and a stranger. The commentary clarifies the underlying principles: 'awl applies when rights are equal in strength (e.g., both in dhimma or in a common part), like inheritance; munaza'ah applies when one right is distinguished (e.g., right in whole vs. part) or rights arise at different times. The students add the condition that simultaneity of establishment requires 'awl, while successive establishment leads to munaza'ah. This detailed analysis is typical of later Hanafi commentators like al-'Ayni, al-Shumunni, and al-Zayla'i.

Key Terms
منازعة (munaza'ah): A method of division where each claimant's right is satisfied sequentially; the first claimant takes his full right, and the remainder goes to the next.
عول ('awl): A method of division where shares are proportionally reduced when they exceed the total; concept from inheritance law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the fourth category (Abu Hanifa's 'awl), which of the following is NOT one of the five cases?
A) A slave authorized to trade with debts from one owner and one stranger.
B) Bequest of all property to one person and half to another with heirs' consent.
C) A slave who kills one person by mistake and another intentionally.
D) An umm walad who kills her master and a stranger intentionally.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi division, the method depends on whether rights are equal in strength (→'awl) or distinct/successive (→munaza'ah), but Sahibayn add simultaneity as an additional criterion.

Division of a Slave's Value: Munaza'a vs 'Awl
Intra-Hanafi dispute on dividing a slave's value

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Abu Hanifa's munaza'a method and the Sahibayn's 'awl method for dividing a slave's value, and apply the correct method based on the nature of the rights.

Core Idea
When rights differ in scope, division by munaza'a applies; when rights are equal in strength, division by 'awl applies.

This passage, from a later Hanafi commentary, explores a subtle yet practically important dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. At issue is how to divide the value of a slave when multiple parties have claims arising from a crime (jinaayah). The disagreement turns on whether the rights are considered established in the whole asset or in distinct parts, leading to different methods of division. Understanding this difference sharpens your ability to apply Hanafi principles of property law and inheritance analogies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
Focus on the case described: one claimant demands the entire slave, the other demands half. One of them forgoes a share. Describe the situation in your own words.
The slave's value is being divided because rights have been established (e.g., due to a crime).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position (munaza'a)
Explain that Abu Hanifa considers the uncontested half safe for the claimant of the whole, and the contested half is split equally. Compute the fractions: 3/4 for the claimant of the whole, 1/4 for the claimant of half.
Munaza'a means 'dispute' – only the disputed portion is divided.
3
State the Sahibayn's position ('awl)
The two companions analogize to inheritance and mudarabah: both rights share equally in the whole asset, so division should be by proportional reduction ('awl). Calculate: the slave's value is divided into shares proportional to the original claims, leading to different fractions (e.g., fourths in the earnings).
'Awl is the method used in inheritance when shares exceed the estate.
4
Analyze the underlying principle
The passage explains that the choice between methods depends on whether the rights are established in the dhimmah (liability) or in the asset itself, and whether they are in a common part or differentiated. Under Abu Hanifa, if one right is in the whole and the other in a part, munaza'a applies. Under the Sahibayn, if both are in the same common part at the same time, 'awl applies.
The key distinction: the Sahibayn view all rights as coexisting in the entire asset.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Demonstrate the numerical difference: Abu Hanifa gives 3/4 and 1/4; the Sahibayn produce a different distribution (e.g., using 'awl the shares might be 4/7 and 3/7). Identify which party benefits under each view.
This affects the actual payout and is not just a theoretical difference.

[٢] قوله : والمضاربة ؛ يعني إنّ لكلّ واحدٍ من المدعيين حقاً في العين على معنى أن حق كل منهما شائع فيها، فما من جزء إلا وصاحب القليل يزاحم فيه صاحب الكثير بنصيبه ؛ فلهذا كانت القسمة فيه بطريق العول. (۱) (حاشية الطحطاوي» (٣: ٣١٦). (۲) ينظر: حاشية الشلبي (٤) : ۳۲۳)، و (رد المحتار (٥ : ٧٨٦).
Know that Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, considered in this issue the method of dispute (munaza‘a), which is that half is safe for the claimant of the whole without dispute; the remaining half is disputed equally between them, so it is halved. Thus, the claimant of the whole gets three-quarters, and the claimant of half gets one-quarter. The slave girl will be taken by each one of them successively, so she will earn (s‘i) in three-quarters of her value, and it will be divided between the silent partners. One-quarter is given to the partner of the later forbearer, and half to the other, between him and the partner of the earlier forbearer in thirds: two-thirds to the partner of the earlier forbearer, and one-third to the later forbearer according to him (Abu Hanifa), but according to them (the Sahibayn) in fourths. This is mentioned by al-‘Ayni, al-Shumnu, al-Zayla‘i, and others in their commentaries and fatwas. [Marginal note:] His saying: “he considered” etc.; know that the principle according to the Imam is that whenever division is obligated due to a right established in the dhimmah (liability), or a right established in the ‘ayn (specific property) on the basis of common ownership (shuyu‘) in part of it but not all, then the division is by ‘awl (augmentation). And whenever it is obligated due to a right established on a differentiated basis, or one’s right is in a common part and the other’s right is in the whole, then it is a division by dispute (munaza‘a). According to them (the Sahibayn): if the two rights are established on a common basis at one time, then the division is by ‘awl; if they are established on a differentiated basis or at two different times, then it is by dispute. This is in “Kamal al-Dirayah” and others. Explanation: When rights are established in the dhimmah, they are equal in strength because the dhimmah is capacious; so each one strikes with his entire right against the ‘ayn. Similarly, if each one’s right is in the ‘ayn but in a common part, they are equal in strength because there is no part in which one’s right is established except that the other can compete with him in it; thus rights are equal in strength. The basis for division by ‘awl is inheritance, where each heir’s right is established in a common part. So if the two rights are established on a differentiated basis, it is not analogous to inheritance. [Page 195] Book of Claims, Chapter on the Claim of Two Men [2] And they (the Sahibayn) considered the method of ‘awl and mudarabah (silent partnership). Similarly, if one’s right is in a common part and the other’s right is in the whole, it is not analogous to inheritance because the owner of the whole competes with the owner of the part in everything, whereas the owner of the part does not compete with the owner of the whole, so it is not analogous to inheritance. And because when each one’s right is in a common part, and what each takes by virtue of division is separated and is not common, then what is taken is in substitution for his right, not the original right itself, so it is analogous to inheritance and an estate in which debts are combined. In matters of division, it is only obligated due to a right established in the dhimmah, because each one’s right is in the consequence of the injury, and its consequence is in the dhimmah, so the division therein is by ‘awl; upon this the issues are derived. Our teacher, the scholar al-Tahtawi mentioned this in his gloss on al-Durr al-Mukhtar. [Marginal note:] His saying: “they considered the method of ‘awl”; that is when many prescribed shares accumulate in an outcome such that the total is insufficient, so ‘awl is needed, as in a woman who died leaving a husband, a full sister, and a consanguine sister: for the husband half, for the full sister half, for the consanguine sister one-sixth to complete two-thirds; the estate then increases ('awl) to seven, whereas originally it was from six. 'Awl linguistically is used to mean inclination, i.e., injustice; one says “fulan ya‘ulu ‘alayya” meaning he inclines unjustly; also meaning overcoming; one says “‘ila sabruhu” meaning his patience was overcome; also meaning raising; one says “‘ala al-mizan” when it is lifted. Technically, it means to add something to the total sum of parts (al-makhraj) beyond the prescribed shares, with the purpose of causing the deficiency to enter the shares of all heirs proportionally. Its details are in the treatises of inheritance. His saying: “and mudarabah”; meaning that each of the two claimants has a right in the ‘ayn in the sense that each one’s right is common in it, so there is no part except that the owner of the small share competes with the owner of the large share therein; therefore the division in it is by the method of 'awl.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal method for dividing the value of a slave when multiple parties claim rights due to a crime (jinaayah). The author explains the difference between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding whether the division should be according to the principle of 'awl (augmentation, as in inheritance) or munaza‘a (dispute-based division). Abu Hanifa holds that when one claimant's right is in the whole and the other's in a part, the uncontested portion goes to the claimant of the whole, and the contested portion is divided equally—this is the munaza‘a method. The Sahibayn analogize the situation to inheritance or mudarabah (silent partnership), where both rights are common in the entire asset, so division is by 'awl. The commentary then elaborates on the general rules: if rights are established in the dhimmah (liability) or in a common part of an asset, division is by 'awl; if rights are established in distinct parts or one has full ownership, division is by munaza‘a. The text includes an example from inheritance to illustrate 'awl. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not resolved in this passage, but the difference is practical: it affects how the slave's earnings are divided among the parties.

Key Terms
المنازعة (al-munaza‘a): A method of dividing jointly owned property where some portions are undisputed and only the contested portions are divided equally.
العول (al-‘awl): A proportional reduction of shares in inheritance when the sum of prescribed shares exceeds the estate; used here analogically for other divisions.
المضاربة (al-mudarabah): A silent partnership; here used as an analogy for the relationship between claimants where each has a share in the whole asset.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, how is the slave's value divided when one claimant demands the whole and the other demands half?
A) Both get half equally
B) The claimant of the whole gets 3/4, the claimant of half gets 1/4
C) The claimant of the whole gets 2/3, the claimant of half gets 1/3
D) The claimant of the whole gets all, the claimant of half gets nothing

Key Takeaway
The division method hinges on whether rights are established in the whole or in part, affecting whether munaza'a or 'awl applies.

Conflicting Claims with Evidence: Hanafi Ruling on the House Dispute
A Hanafi explanation of conflicting claims with evidence and proportional division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling when two claimants present evidence over property in both hands, and distinguish it from the Shafi'i/Maliki position.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, the half-claimant keeps his half without judgment; the all-claimant gets the other half by judgment.

This lesson examines a classic dispute in Hanafi fiqh: when two claimants both have evidence over a house that is in both their hands, one claiming the entire house and the other claiming half. The Hanafi school reaches a proportional division—the all-claimant gets two-thirds, the half-claimant gets one-third—while other schools leave the house unchanged. Understanding this ruling clarifies how Hanafi jurists weigh possession, evidence, and the legitimacy of claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
Note that one claimant asserts full ownership of the house, while the other claims only half. The house is in both their hands physically.
Both have evidence (bayyinah).
2
Apply the Hanafi principle of 'hand' and 'outside'
The half-claimant's claim is directed to the half in his own hand, making his hand legitimate. The all-claimant claims both halves; for the half in the other's hand, he is an outsider (khaarij).
Hanafi rule: an outsider's evidence is preferred over the possessor's when their claims intersect.
3
Determine the outcome: half by judgment, half without
The half in the half-claimant's hand is left without formal judgment (since he is not challenged for his own half). The other half is awarded to the all-claimant by judgment, because he is the outsider and his evidence prevails.
Thus the all-claimant gets the whole house—half by abandonment, half by judgment.
4
Calculate the proportional shares (yadrib)
Both claimants 'strike' with their full claims: the all-claimant with 1 (representing the whole), the half-claimant with 1/2. To find a common denominator, use 2: all-claimant gets 2 shares, half-claimant gets 1 share, so total 3 shares. Thus all-claimant gets 2/3, half-claimant gets 1/3.
This method is analogous to 'awl in inheritance.
5
Compare with the opposing school's view
Shafi'i, Malik, and some Hanbali narrations hold that the house remains in both hands as before, because each hand gives preponderance to its possessor's evidence. The Hanafi response: the half-claimant's hand is only valid for his own half, not for the other half.
The practical difference: Hanafi reduces the half-claimant's share from 1/2 to 1/3; the opponent keeps it at 1/2.

[1] قوله: يضرب؛ قال في «الكفاية» نقلاً عن «المغرب» (ص 281): قال الفقهاء: فلان يضرب فيه بالثلث: أي يأخذ منه شيئاً بحكم ما له من الثلث. انتهى. [2] قوله: وإن كانت...الخ؛ يعني أن الدار إذا كانت في أيديهما، وادعى أحدهما نصفها والآخر كلها، وبرهن كل منهما على دعواه، فسلم لصاحب الجميع نصفها على القضاء، ونصفها لا على وجه القضاء، وهو رواية عن أحمد، وقال الشافعي ومالك وأحمد في رواية: تبقى الدار في يديهما كما كانت؛ لترجح بينة صاحب اليد باليد. ولنا: إن مدعي النصف تنصرف دعواه إلى ما في يده؛ لتكون يده يداً محقة؛ لأن حمل أمور المسلمين على الصحة واجب ومدعي الكل يدعي ما في يد نفسه وما في يد الآخر، ولا ينازعه أحد. فما في يده يترك في يده لا على وجه القضاء، واستوت منازعتهما فيما في يد صاحبه، فكانت بينته أولى؛ لأنه خارج فيه، فيقضى له في كل النصف، فسلم له كل الدار، نصفها بالترك، لا على وجه القضاء، والنصف الآخر بالقضاء. [1] قوله: فيترك في يده؛ حاصله: إنه إذا لم يدع مدعي النصف النصف الذي في يد مدعي الجميع، ولا قضاء بدون الدعوى، فيترك ذلك النصف في يد مدعي الجميع بلا قضاء، ولو كانت في يد ثلاثة فادعى أحدهم كلها، وآخر ثلثها، وآخر نصفها، وبرهنوا، فهي مقسومة عنده بطريق المنازعة، وعندهما بطريق العول. وبيانه ينظر في «الكافي».
If the house is in both their hands, then for the second (the one claiming half) half by judgment and half not by judgment. So each one strikes with his entire claim, and we need a number that has a correct half, and the least is two; the one claiming the whole strikes with that, and the claimant of half strikes with one share, so it becomes a third between them. This is in 'Inaya. [1] He said: 'yadrib'; quoted from 'Kifaya' from 'Mughrib': the jurists say: so-and-so strikes in it with a third: meaning he takes something from it by virtue of his right to a third. End. [2] He said: 'If it is in both...' meaning that if the house is in both hands, and one claims half and the other claims all, and each provides evidence for his claim, then the one claiming all gets half by judgment and half not by judgment; this is a narration from Ahmad. Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad in another narration said: the house remains in both hands as it was, due to the preponderance of the evidence of the one in possession. Our position: the claimant of half directs his claim to what is in his hand so that his hand is a legitimate hand, because it is obligatory to assume Muslim affairs are sound. The one claiming all claims what is in his own hand and what is in the other's hand, and no one disputes him regarding what is in his own hand. So what is in his hand is left in his hand without judgment. Their dispute is equal regarding what is in the other's hand, so the evidence of the outside claimant is preferable because he is an outsider; thus judgment is given for him regarding that half. So he gets the entire house, half by abandonment (not by judgment) and the other half by judgment. [1] He said: 'so it is left in his hand'; summary: if the half-claimant does not claim the half that is in the hand of the all-claimant, and there is no judgment without claim, so that half is left in the hand of the all-claimant without judgment. If there were three, one claiming all, another claiming a third, and another claiming half, and they provide evidence, then according to Abu Hanifa it is divided by the method of contestation, while according to the two companions it is divided by 'awl. Its explanation is in 'Kafi'.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute where two claimants have evidence over a house that is in both their hands: one claims the entire house, the other claims half. The Hanafi ruling, as explained in the commentary, is that the half-claimant gets the half that is in his hand without formal judgment because his hand is considered legitimate (his claim is directed to his own possession), while the all-claimant gets the other half by judgment because he is an outsider regarding that half and his evidence prevails. This results in a proportional division: the all-claimant effectively gets two-thirds (his own half plus half of the other's half) and the half-claimant gets one-third (his own half). The commentary notes that this is a narration from Ahmad, but Shafi'i, Malik, and another narration from Ahmad hold that the house remains as is. The Hanafi response is based on the principle that the half-claimant's claim is confined to his own hand, so for that portion his hand gives preponderance, while for the other half the all-claimant is an outsider and his evidence is preferred. The commentary also discusses the method of proportional allocation (yadrib) and analogizes to 'awl in inheritance. It further notes a variant if there are three claimants.

Key Terms
يضرب (yadribu): In this context, means to take a portion proportionally, as in striking a share of the property.
العول (al-'awl): Proportional reduction of shares when the total of prescribed shares exceeds the estate; here applied analogously to the division of the house.
خارج (khaarij): An outside claimant, i.e., one who is not in possession of the property in question.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when two claimants both present evidence over a house in both their hands, one claiming all and the other claiming half, what is the final division?
A) The house remains in both hands as before.
B) The all-claimant gets the whole house.
C) The half-claimant gets 1/3 and the all-claimant gets 2/3.
D) The half-claimant gets the whole house.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school divides the house proportionally (2/3 and 1/3) based on the rule that possession only validates a claim for the possessed portion, and an outsider's evidence prevails for the rest.

Ruling on Two External Claimants Over an Animal With Dated Proofs
Explaining the Hanafi ruling on evidence priority for animal ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two external animal claimants present dated birth evidence and apply the principle of date-matching over possession.

Core Idea
When an animal's age matches a claimant's date, that date is a sign of truth and outweighs possession or other evidence.

In Hanafi law, when two outsiders both claim an animal and each provides evidence of its birth with a date, the animal's known age becomes the decisive factor. This lesson explores how the date-matching criterion establishes the truthful claimant and clarifies the fallback rule when the age is ambiguous.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two claimants are external (khaarij), meaning neither currently possesses the animal. Each presents evidence (bayyinah) that the animal was born in his ownership, and each includes a date.
External claimants are contrasted with the case where one claimant has possession; here possession does not decide because of the date.
2
Apply the primary rule
If the animal's known age matches one of the dates, the judge gives the animal to that claimant.
The match is a sign of truth (alama al-sidq) that makes that claimant's evidence preponderant (tarjih).
3
Understand the rationale
The matching date indicates the truthful claimant; the other claimant's evidence is shown to be false and is rejected.
This applies regardless of who holds the animal at the time of the claim.
4
Note the fallback rule
If the age is unclear or matches no date, the animal is divided equally between the two claimants.
This is the default when neither claimant's evidence is stronger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن برهن خارجان على نتاج دابة، وأرّخا، قضى لمن وافق تاريخه ووقته سنها، وإن أشكل فلهما. قوله: وإن برهن.... الخ؛ يعني إن الخارجين إذا تنازعا في دابة، وأقام كل واحد منهما بينة أنها نتجت عنده، وذكرا تاريخاً وسن الدابة يوافق أحد التاريخين، قضى بها لمن وافق بتاريخه ووقته سنها؛ لأن علامة الصدق ظهرت فيمن وافق تاريخه، فترجحت بينته بذلك وفي الأخرى ظهرت علامة الكذب، فيجب ردها ولا فرق في ذلك بين أن تكون الدابة في أيديهما أو في يد أحدهما، أو في يد ثالث؛ لأن المعنى لا يختلف بخلاف ما إذا كانت الدعوى في النتاج من غير تاريخ حيث يحكم بها لذي اليد إن كانت في يد أحدهما، أو لهما إن كانت في أيديهما، أو في يد ثالث. صرح به الزيلعي، وغيره. (۳) قوله: وإن أشكل ... الخ؛ أي إن أشكل من الدابة كانت الدابة بينهما؛ لأن أحدهما ليس بأولى من الآخر، هذا إذا كانا خارجين بأن كانت الدابة في يد ثالث، وكذا إذا كانت في يد أحدهما قضى لهما بها.
If two outside claimants present evidence on the birth of an animal and they give a date, judgment is for the one whose date and time match its age; if it is unclear, it is for both. He said: 'If they both present evidence...' meaning that if two outside claimants dispute over an animal, and each provides evidence that it was born in his possession, and they mention a date and the animal's age matches one of the two dates, judgment is given to the one whose date and time match its age, because the sign of truth appears in the one whose date matches, so his evidence is preponderant by that, while in the other, the sign of falsehood appears, so it must be rejected. There is no difference in this between the animal being in both their hands, in the hand of one of them, or in the hand of a third party, because the meaning does not differ, unlike when the claim is about birth without a date, where judgment is for the one in possession if it is in his hand, or for both if it is in both their hands, or for the third party if it is in his hand. Al-Zayla'i and others explicitly stated this. (3) He said: 'If it is unclear...' meaning if the animal's age is unclear, then the animal is between them, because one is not more entitled than the other. This is when both are outsiders and the animal is in a third party's hand; similarly, if it is in one of their hands, he gives judgment to both.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage deals with the case of two external claimants both asserting ownership of an animal, each providing evidence of its birth with a specific date. The Hanafi ruling is that if the animal's known age corresponds to one claimant's date, that claimant wins; if the ages are ambiguous and no match is clear, the animal is divided equally between them. The rationale is that the match of date and age is a clear sign of truth, making that claimant's evidence preponderant, while the other claimant's evidence is shown to be false and is rejected. The ruling applies regardless of who physically possesses the animal at the time. The commentary also contrasts this with cases where no dates are given, where the default rule is decision by possession.

Key Terms
نتاج (nataj): The birth of an animal; here, the event of the animal being born under a claimant's ownership.
خارج (khaarij): An outside claimant, i.e., one who is not in possession of the animal at the time of the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of two external claimants over an animal, both providing evidence with dates, and the animal's age matches one claimant's date, what is the ruling?
A) The animal is divided equally.
B) The animal is given to the one whose date matches.
C) The animal is given to the one in possession.
D) The case is dismissed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when two external claimants provide dated birth evidence for an animal, the one whose date matches the animal's age wins; if unclear, they share equally.

Equal Claims of Ghasb and Wadi'ah: The Legal Transformation Upon Denial
Ruling on equal claims of ghasb and wadi'ah from external claimants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that denial of a trust (juhud al-wadi'ah) transforms the trustee into a usurper, resulting in equal claims between a deposit claimant and a usurpation claimant.

Core Idea
Denial of a deposit turns the trustee into a usurper, making both ghasb and wadi'ah claims equal.

When two external claimants both present evidence against a possessor, one claiming ghasb (usurpation) and the other claiming wadi'ah (deposit), the Hanafi school rules that they are equal. This might seem surprising because ghasb is a tort and wadi'ah is a trust. But the key lies in the depositary's denial: by denying the deposit, he becomes a usurper, thus both claims rest on the same wrongful taking. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the practical implication: the property is divided equally.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
There is a property in the hand of a possessor (dhu al-yad). Two external claimants (kharjani) each present evidence (bayyinah). One claims ghasb (the possessor took it from him), the other claims wadi'ah (he deposited it with the possessor).
2
State the ruling
The judge divides the property equally between the two claimants (yunassafu baynahuma).
3
Analyze the reasoning (ta'lil)
The depositary, by denying the deposit (juhud al-wadi'ah), legally becomes a usurper (ghasib). Therefore, the cause (sabab) of entitlement for both claimants is the same: wrongful taking by the possessor.
Without denial, the depositary remains a trustee and the claims would not be equal.
4
Apply the principle
Equality in the cause necessitates equality in entitlement. Thus, the claimants are equal and split the property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن بَرْهَنَ أحد الخارجين على غَضب شيء، والآخر على وديعته استويا (فإن بَرْهَنَ أحد الخارجين على غَصْبِ شيء، والآخر على وديعته استويا) ، ادعى أحد الخارجين على ذي اليد أنك غصبت هذا الشيء مني، والآخرُ ادعى أني أودعت هذا الشيء عندك ، وبَرْهَنا ، يُنَصِّفُ بينهما لاستوائهما، فإن المودع إذا جحد الوديعة صارَ غاصباً ] قوله : فإن برهن... الخ ؛ يعني أن عيناً إذا كانت في يد رجل فأقام رجلان عليها البينة، أحدهما بغصب، والآخر بوديعة، استوت دعواهما حتى يقضي بينهما. (٢) قوله : لاستوائهما ؛ أي في الاستحقاق ؛ لأن المودع لما جحد الوديعة صار كالغاصب، فصار دعوى الوديعة والغصب سواء، والتساوي في سبب الاستحقاق يوجب التساوي في نفس الاستحقاق واجي
If one of the two external claimants adduces evidence (of) usurpation (ghasb) of something, and the other (adduces evidence) of his deposit (wadi'ah), they are equal. (If one external claimant adduces evidence of usurpation of something, and the other of his deposit, they are equal.) He (the author) said: one external claimant claims to the possessor, 'You usurped this thing from me,' and the other claims, 'I deposited this thing with you,' and they both adduce evidence; it is divided between them because of their equality. Because the depositary, when he denies the deposit, becomes a usurper. [Commentary:] His statement: 'If one adduces evidence... etc.' means that if a specific property is in the hand of a man, and two men both adduce evidence regarding it, one for usurpation and the other for deposit, their claims are equal, so judgment is given between them. (2) His statement: 'because of their equality' means in entitlement, because when the depositary denies the deposit, he becomes like a usurper, so the claim of deposit and usurpation become the same. Equality in the cause of entitlement necessitates equality in the entitlement itself. And (God knows best?).
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage states a ruling from the chapter of claims (da'wa) regarding two external claimants (kharjani) who both present evidence (bayyinah) against the same person in possession (dhu al-yad). One claimant claims ghasb (usurpation) — that the possessor took the property from him — and the other claims wadi'ah (deposit) — that he himself deposited the property with the possessor. The ruling is that they are equal (istawaya), and the property is divided equally between them (yunassafu baynahuma). The reasoning (ta'lil) is that the depositary, by denying the deposit (juhud al-wadi'ah), becomes effectively a usurper (ghasib). Therefore, both claims are based on the same cause: the possessor wrongfully took the property from each claimant. Although the original legal statuses of ghasb and wadi'ah differ, after denial the depositary's position is identical to a usurper, so the claims are equal in entitlement (istihqaq). Equality in the cause (sabab) leads to equality in the ruling (hukm). The commentary clarifies that this applies when the property is in the hand of a third person (the possessor), not in the hands of either claimant. The phrase 'until judgment is given between them' (hatta yuqda baynahuma) means they share equally.

Key Terms
الغصب (al-ghasb): Usurpation or wrongful possession of another's property without right.
الوديعة (al-wadi'ah): A deposit – entrusting property to another for safekeeping without compensation.
الجحد (al-juhd / al-jahd): Denial; here, the depositary's denial of the deposit, which changes his legal status.
الاستحقاق (al-istihqaq): Entitlement or right to the property based on evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are a claim of ghasb (usurpation) and a claim of wadi'ah (deposit) treated equally when two external claimants present evidence?
A) Because both claims are based on the same type of contract
B) Because the depositary, by denying the deposit, becomes like a usurper
C) Because the possessor has admitted both claims
D) Because the property is in the hand of both claimants

Key Takeaway
Denial of a trust (juhud al-wadi'ah) transforms the trustee into a usurper, making a claim of deposit equal to a claim of usurpation when both are presented by external claimants.

Equal Division for Claims of Usurpation and Deposit
Ruling on equal division when two claimants prove different claims (ghasb and wadi'ah)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two claimants provide evidence—one for usurpation and one for deposit—and explain the reasoning that denial of deposit transforms the depositary into a usurper.

Core Idea
Denial of a deposit makes the depositary a usurper, equalizing the claims of usurpation and deposit.

In judicial claims, when two individuals each claim the same item from a possessor—one claiming usurpation (ghasb), the other deposit (wadi'ah)—and both produce valid evidence (bayyinah), the Hanafi school rules that they are equal in entitlement. This lesson explores that ruling and its rationale, which hinges on a key legal transformation: the depositary’s denial turns him into a usurper, making both claims equivalent and leading to an equal division of the property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the judicial scenario
Understand the parties: a possessor (third party), two claimants (one for ghasb, one for wadi'ah), and the requirement that both bring valid evidence (bayyinah). The item is in the hands of the possessor, and both claimants assert rights based on their respective claims.
2
State the ruling
The judge divides the property equally between the two claimants. The ruling is clear: 'they are equal' (استويا).
The division is per capita, not based on value.
3
Analyze the talil (reasoning)
The commentary explains that when the depositary denies the deposit, he becomes like a usurper. Therefore, the claim for deposit becomes identical in nature to the claim for usurpation. Both claims now rest on the same wrongful act by the possessor.
The denial effectively transforms the legal status of the possessor retroactively.
4
Extract the legal principle
Equality in the cause of entitlement (sabab al-istihqaq) leads to equality in the entitlement itself. Since both claims now stem from the same wrongful possession, they are treated equally.
This principle is specific to cases where the possessor denies a deposit, nullifying the trust relationship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى باب دعوى الرجلين
فإن بَرْهَنَ أحد الخارجين على غَضب شيء، والآخر على وديعته استويا (فإن بَرْهَنَ أحد الخارجين على غَصْبِ شيء، والآخر على وديعته استويا) ، ادعى أحد الخارجين على ذي اليد أنك غصبت هذا الشيء مني، والآخرُ ادعى أني أودعت هذا الشيء عندك ، وبَرْهَنا ، يُنَصِّفُ بينهما لاستوائهما، فإن المودع إذا جحد الوديعة صارَ غاصباً
[Commentary] قوله : فإن برهن... الخ ؛ يعني أن عيناً إذا كانت في يد رجل فأقام رجلان عليها البينة، أحدهما بغصب، والآخر بوديعة، استوت دعواهما حتى يقضي بينهما. (٢) قوله : لاستوائهما ؛ أي في الاستحقاق ؛ لأن المودع لما جحد الوديعة صار كالغاصب، فصار دعوى الوديعة والغصب سواء، والتساوي في سبب الاستحقاق يوجب التساوي في نفس الاستحقاق
Book of Claims, Chapter: Claim of Two Men. If one of the two claimants proves (with evidence) regarding the usurpation of a thing, and the other regarding its deposit, they are equal. (If one of the two claimants proves regarding the usurpation of a thing, and the other regarding its deposit, they are equal.) One of the two claimants says to the possessor: 'You usurped this thing from me,' and the other says: 'I deposited this thing with you,' and they both provide evidence, it is divided equally between them because of their equality. For the depositary, when he denies the deposit, becomes a usurper. [Commentary] His saying 'if he proves... etc.' means that if an article is in the hand of a man, and two men establish evidence over it, one for usurpation and the other for deposit, their claims become equal until judgment is made between them. (2) His saying 'because of their equality' means in entitlement; because when the depositary denies the deposit, he becomes like a usurper, so the claim of deposit and usurpation become the same, and equality in the cause of entitlement necessitates equality in the entitlement itself.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage states a ruling in judicial claims (qada). The scenario: an item is in the possession of a third party. Two claimants come forward: one claims the possessor usurped (ghasb) the item from him; the other claims he deposited (wadi'ah) the item with the possessor. Both claimants bring valid evidence (bayyinah). The ruling is that they are equal in their claim, and the item is divided equally between them. The reasoning (talil) is that when the depositary (the possessor) denies the deposit, he effectively becomes a usurper. Thus, both claims—originally one for deposit and one for usurpation—become equivalent in nature. Since the cause (sabab) of entitlement is equal, the entitlement itself must be equal, leading to an equal division. This ruling is from the Hanafi school, as found in the commentary on Al-Hidayah. It highlights the principle that denial of a deposit transforms the depositary's status to that of a usurper for legal purposes.

Key Terms
الخارجان (al-kharijan): The two claimants in a legal dispute, each claiming the same property from a third party possessor.
الغصب (al-ghasb): Usurpation or wrongful taking of property without the owner's consent.
الوديعة (al-wadi'ah): Deposit trust; property left with a person for safekeeping.
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence, typically testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the depositary, by denying the deposit, becomes like a usurper.
C) Because the item is equally valuable to both claimants.
D) Because the possessor admits the claims are true.

Key Takeaway
When a depositary denies the deposit, he is treated as a usurper, making the claim of deposit equivalent to a claim of usurpation, leading to equal division of property between the two claimants who both provide evidence.

Child's Claim of Freedom and Confession to Slavery in Hanafi Law
Ruling with proof on child's statement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a child's claim of freedom is accepted based on his inherent hand (yad) over himself, but his confession to slavery while in another's possession is effective only for the possessor.

Core Idea
A child's word on his own freedom is trusted; but he cannot harm himself by confessing slavery to a non-possessor.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi principle in judicial disputes: how a child's statement is treated when it conflicts with a possessor's claim. The source passage explores the tension between the default presumption of freedom (al-asl al-hurriyah) and the evidentiary weight of physical possession (yad). You will see how the Hanafi school reconciles these principles through a rational proof (dalil aqli) and addresses objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The passage focuses on a child (sabi) who can express himself. When a claimant asserts slavery and the child denies it, the child's word is accepted. When the child confesses slavery to a non-possessor, the possessor's claim prevails. Identify these two scenarios and their respective rulings.
The first ruling prioritises the child's own hand (yad) over himself; the second prioritises the possessor's hand.
2
Locate the Dalil (Proof)
The primary proof is the principle that every human has a 'hand' over himself by virtue of honour (كرامة). This is a rational (aqli) proof, not a textual one. The passage also uses the concept of yad as evidence of ownership.
The dalil is zanni in both thubut (transmission) and dalala (indication).
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The principle that each person has a hand over himself means his statement about his own status (freedom) is accepted. When he is in another's possession, that external hand becomes a stronger indicator of ownership, overriding the default freedom.
Possession (yad) is an amarah (indication) of ownership that can overcome the asl (presumption) of freedom.
4
Analyse the Objection and Response
Objection: A child's statements in harmful matters are not binding, so his confession to slavery should be rejected. Response: Slavery is not proven by the child's word but by the possessor's claim; the child can later claim freedom. Objection: The principle of original freedom overrides possession. Response: Possession is stronger evidence; it overrides the presumption.
The Hanafi response relies on the concept that yad is a stronger indicator than the default assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(وجالس البساط) والمتعلق به سواء كمن معه ثوب وطرفه مع آخر. والقولُ الصبي " يُعبِّرُ في أنا حرّ، وإن قال : أنا عبدُ فلان قُضي من معه كَمَن لا يعبر) [1]قوله : وذو حملها ؛ يعني إذا تنازعا في بعير مثلاً، وعليه حمل لأحدهما، وللآخر كوز معلّق فصاحب الحمل أولى ؛ لأنه هو المتصرف تصرفاً معتاداً، فكان في يده كما إذا ادعى جماعة سفينة، وكان واحد منهم راكبها ، والآخر ممسك لسكانها والآخر يجدف فيها، والآخر يمدها فهي بينهم إلا من يمدُّها ، فإنّه لا شيء له فيها ؛ لأنه لا يد له فيها ؛ لأنَّ الباقين هم المتصرفون فيها تصرفا معتاداً. ولو كان الحمل لهما كان البعير بينهما لاستوائهما ، ولا يرجح أحدهما بكثرة ما في الحمل ؛ لأحدهما لأنك عرفت أنَّ الترجيح يقع بالقوة لا بالكثرة). [٢]قوله : وجالس البساط ... الخ ؛ يعني إذا تنازعا في بساط أحدهما جالس عليه، والآخر متعلق به، فالبساط بينهما سواء، يعني لا على وجه القضاء، بل يترك في يدهما ؛ لأنَّ القعود ليس بيد عليه فاستويا ، فإنَّ اليد على البساط لا تثبت إلا بالنقل والتحويل، أو يكون في يده حكماً بأن كان في بيته ولم يوجد شيء من ذلك ؛ ولهذا لا يصير غاصباً بمجرد القعود. بخلاف الركوب على الدابة، فإنّه يصير غاصباً بمجرد الركوب عليه بغير الإذنِ كما صرحوا به، وإذا كان الثوب في يد رجل وطرفٌ منه في يد آخر، فالثوب بينهما نصفان؛ لأن كلّ واحدٍ منهما يتمسك باليد، إلا أن أحدهما أكثر استمساكاً، فالزيادة من جنس الحجة، فلا يوجب الزيادة في الاستحقاق، إذ لا ترجيح بكثرة العلل كما عرفت مرارا. (۳) قوله : والقول لصبي ... الخ ؛ يعني إذا كان صبي في يد رجل وهو يعبر عن نفسه ، يعني يتكلّم ويعلم ما يقول، فحين دعوى رجل بأنه عبد، قال: أنا حر، فالقول قوله ؛ لأن ذلك الصبي في يد نفسه، فكان هو صاحب اليد، والمدعي كان خارجاً، والقول قول صاحب اليد ؛ وهذا لأنَّ الأصل أن يكون لكل إنسان يد على نفسه إبانة لمعنى الكرامة، فلا يقبل دعوى أحد عليه أنه عبده عند إنكاره ، إلا ببينته كالبالغ. وإن قال ذلك الصبي : أنا عبد لفلان، وهو غير ذي اليد، فهو عبد لذي اليدِ بالإجماع ؛ لأنه لما أقر أنه عبد الفلان، أقر أنه ليس له استقلال ولا قدرة على نفسه، فلا يعمل بإقراره، ويكون عبداً لذي اليد لا للخارج، إلا بالبينة، وشهادة العبد ليست بحجة. فإن قلت : الإقرار بالرق ضرر، وأقوال الصبي في المضارّ ليست بموجبة ، فكان الواجب أن لا يعتبر في حق الصبي. قلنا : الرق لم يثبت بقول الصبي بل بدعوى ذي اليد ؛ لعدم المعارض بدعوى الحرية ؛ لأنه لما صار في يد المدعي بقي كالقماش في يده، فيقبل إقراره عليه، ولا نُسَلِّمُ أنَّ الإقرار بالرقّ من المضارّ ؛ لأنه يمكنه التدارك بعده بدعوى الحرية، إذ التناقض فيه لا يمنع صحة الدعوى بخلاف الإقرار بالدين. وإن قلت : الأصل في الآدمي الحرية ؛ لأنه ولد آدم وحواء على نبينا وعليهما الصلاة والسلام، وهما حرّان، فيجب أن لا يقبل دعوى الرق إلا بالبينة، وكون ذلك الصبي في يد صاحب اليد لا يجب قبول قوله عليه كما لا يقبلُ قولُ الملتقط : إن هذا اللقيط عبده، وإن كان في يده. قلنا : إن الأصل يبطل إذا اعترض عليه ما يدلُّ على خلافه، وثبوت اليد دليل على خلاف ذلك الأصل ؛ لأنه دليلُ الملك، فيبطل به ذلك الأصل، ولا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ اللقيط إذا عبر عن نفسه وأقرَّ بالرق يخالفه في الحكم، وأمّا إذا لم يعبر عن : نفسه فلیس في يد الملتقط من كل وجه ؛ لأنه أمين فيه، والأمين يده قائمة مقام غيره، فكانت غير ثابتة حكماً. وأما إذا كان الصبي لا يعبر عن نفسه فهو عبد للذي هو في يده ؛ لأنه بمنزلة المتاع، فيكون ملكاً لمن هو في يده إن ادعاه ؛ لعدم المعارض من يد على نفسه حقيقة أو

(And the sitter on the mat) and the one attached to it are equal, like someone with a garment and its edge with another. And the statement of the child who expresses himself: 'I am free', and if he says 'I am the slave of so-and-so', judgement is given for the one with him, like one who does not express himself. [1] His statement: 'And the owner of its load' means if two dispute over a camel, for example, with a load belonging to one and a water pot hung by the other, then the owner of the load is more deserving because he is the one who acts in the customary manner, so he has possession, as when a group claims a ship and one of them is riding it, another is holding its rudder, another is rowing it, and another is towing it; then it is among them except the one towing it, for he has nothing in it because he has no hand in it; because the rest are the ones who act in the customary manner. And if the load belonged to both of them, then the camel would be between them because they are equal, and one is not preferred by the quantity of what is in the load because you know that preference is by strength not by quantity. [2] His statement: 'And the sitter on the mat...' means if two dispute over a mat, one sitting on it and the other attached to it, then the mat is between them equally, meaning not by way of judgement, but it is left in their hands because sitting is not a hand over it, so they are equal. For the hand over the mat is only established by transport and transfer, or by being in his hand implicitly by being in his house, and if none of that exists, then one does not become a usurper by merely sitting. Unlike riding an animal, for one becomes a usurper by merely riding it without permission as they have stated. And if a garment is in the hand of one man and an edge of it in the hand of another, then the garment is half between them because each of them holds it with the hand, except that one holds more; the increase is of the same kind as the proof, so it does not cause an increase in entitlement, since there is no preference by multiplicity of causes as you have known repeatedly. (3) His statement: 'And the statement of a child...' means if a child is in the hand of a man and he expresses himself, i.e., speaks and knows what he says, then when a man claims that he is a slave, he says: 'I am free'. The statement is his statement because that child is in his own hand, so he is the possessor, and the claimant is external, and the statement of the possessor is accepted. And this is because the principle is that every human being has a hand over himself by way of honor, so one cannot accept the claim of someone against him that he is his slave when he denies it, except with evidence, like an adult. And if that child says: 'I am the slave of so-and-so', while he (so-and-so) is not the possessor, then he is a slave of the possessor by consensus, because since he confessed that he is the slave of that person, he confessed that he has no independence and no power over himself, so his confession is not effective, and he becomes a slave of the possessor, not of the external person, except with evidence. And the testimony of a slave is not a proof. If you say: Confession to slavery is harmful, and the statements of a child regarding harmful matters are not binding, so it should not be considered regarding the child. We say: Slavery is not established by the child's statement but by the claim of the possessor, because there is no opponent with a claim of freedom. For when he came into the hand of the claimant, he remained like cloth in his hand, so his confession upon it is accepted. And we do not concede that the confession to slavery is among harmful things, because he can subsequently remedy it by claiming freedom, since contradiction in it does not prevent the validity of the claim, unlike confession to debt. And if you say: The principle regarding humans is freedom, because he is born of Adam and Eve - upon our Prophet and upon them peace and blessings - and they were free, so it is necessary that the claim of slavery not be accepted except with evidence. And that the child being in the hand of the possessor does not necessitate accepting his statement against him, just as the statement of the finder is not accepted: 'This foundling is my slave', even if he is in his hand. We say: The principle is invalidated when something that indicates otherwise arises, and the establishment of the hand is evidence contrary to that principle because it is evidence of ownership, so that principle is invalidated by it. And we do not concede that the foundling, if he expresses himself and confesses to slavery, would be different in ruling. As for if he does not express himself, then he is not in the finder's hand from every aspect, because he is a trustee, and the hand of a trustee stands in place of another, so it is not established implicitly. And if the child does not express himself, then he is a slave for the one in whose hand he is, because he is like chattel, so he becomes property of the one in whose hand he is if he claims him, due to the absence of an opponent with a hand over himself, whether in reality or
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn (likely from Al-Wiqayah) expanding the rulings related to possession and claims in the chapter of da'wa (litigation). The author, ‘Umdat al-Ri‘ayah, explains three scenarios: (1) dispute over a camel with a load and a hanging water pot—the possessor of the load is preferred because he has habitual control (yad); (2) dispute over a carpet—if one sits and another holds it, they share equally because sitting does not constitute a hand (yad), unlike riding which does; (3) dispute over a garment held by two—they share half each regardless of how much they hold. The main focus is on a child (sabi) who can express himself. If a child claims freedom when someone says he is a slave, his word is taken because every human has a hand over himself (yad ‘ala nafsihi) by virtue of honour (karamah). However, if the child confesses to being the slave of someone who is not the possessor, then by consensus (ijma‘) he becomes the slave of the possessor because his confession is not accepted to cause harm to himself—it is like a confession on property in his possession. The text addresses two objections: (a) that a child’s confession of harm should not be binding—the response is that slavery is not proven by the child’s word but by the possessor’s claim, and the child can later claim freedom; (b) that the principle of original freedom (al-asl al-hurriyah) overrides the possessor’s claim—the response is that the presence of possession (yad) as evidence of ownership overrides the principle of freedom. The passage concludes that a child who cannot express himself is like chattel, so the possessor becomes the owner if he claims him, because there is no opposing hand over himself.

Key Terms
صبي (sabi): A minor child who has not reached puberty; the text distinguishes between those who can express themselves (yu‘abbir) and those who cannot.
يد (yad): Possession or control; either physical (like holding) or legal (like being in one's house). It is a key factor in determining ownership in disputes.
إقرار (iqrar): Confession or acknowledgment; a statement that may be used as evidence against oneself. In this text, a child's iqrar to slavery is not accepted to harm himself, but is accepted in favor of the possessor.
حرية (hurriyyah): Freedom; the default state of a human being according to Islamic law. The principle 'al-asl fi al-adami al-hurriyah' is used here.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a child who can express himself says 'I am the slave of Zaid' while he is in the physical possession of Amr, who is his legal master?
A) Zaid, because the child said so.
B) Amr, because the child's confession is not harmful to himself and the possessor's hand prevails.
C) Neither, because a child's statement is not accepted in any case.
D) The child is free, because the principle of original freedom overrides all.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a child's word on his freedom is trusted, but he cannot use confession to transfer himself to a non-possessor; the possessor's hand prevails.

Child's Claim of Slavery or Freedom: The Role of Expression (Al-Mu'abbir vs Ghayr al-Mu'abbir)
Ruling on child's claim of slavery vs. freedom based on ability to express

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a child who can express himself (mu'abbir) and one who cannot (ghayr mu'abbir), apply the Hanafi ruling on claims of slavery for each, and evaluate the qiyas with the foundling case.

Core Idea
A child who speaks and understands is in his own hand; a child who cannot is like merchandise in the possessor's hand.

This lesson examines a nuanced Hanafi ruling on what happens when an adult claims a child as a slave. The outcome depends entirely on whether the child can coherently express his own legal status. The passage introduces the concept of 'yad' (hand) and a critical qiyas with a foundling to resolve an objection. By the end, you will understand how capacity to speak transforms the legal presumption.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of children
The passage defines 'mu'abbir' as a child who speaks and understands what he says. 'Ghayr mu'abbir' cannot express himself coherently. Identify the legal consequence for each when a claim of slavery is made.
The child's ability to claim freedom is the key variable.
2
Apply the principle of 'yad' (hand)
A mu'abbir child is considered to have a hand over himself, so his statement 'I am free' prevails. A ghayr mu'abbir child lacks this, so his hand is absent; the possessor's hand becomes complete evidence of ownership.
The hand is a proxy for control and legal personality.
3
Trace the qiyas with the foundling (laqeet)
The opponent argues: a foundling in a finder's hand is not considered a slave when the finder claims him, so why should a non-expressive child be different? Identify the Hanafi response that distinguishes the foundling because the finder is a trustee (amin), not a full possessor.
The illa is the incomplete hand of the amin vs. complete hand of a non-amin.
4
Examine the objection regarding harm
The opponent objects that acknowledging slavery is harmful, and a child's harmful statements are ineffective. The Hanafi response: slavery is established by the possessor's claim, not the child's acknowledgment, and the harm is repairable by later claiming freedom.
This distinguishes from acknowledgment of debt, which is irrevocable.
5
Synthesize the two scenarios
Summarize: mu'abbir child's statement of freedom is accepted (he is in his own hand). Ghayr mu'abbir child's statement is ineffective; he belongs to the possessor. If the child later becomes expressive and claims freedom, he must bring evidence.
The ruling is mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.

Source Text

كتاب الدعوى باب دعوى الرجلين
يعني يتكلّم ويعلم ما يقول، فحين دعوى رجل بأنه عبد، قال: أنا حر، فالقول قوله ؛ لأن ذلك الصبي في يد نفسه، فكان هو صاحب اليد، والمدعي كان خارجاً، والقول قول صاحب اليد ؛ وهذا لأنَّ الأصل أن يكون لكل إنسان يد على نفسه إبانة لمعنى الكرامة، فلا يقبل دعوى أحد عليه أنه عبده عند إنكاره ، إلا ببينته كالبالغ. وإن قال ذلك الصبي : أنا عبد لفلان، وهو غير ذي اليد، فهو عبد لذي اليدِ بالإجماع ؛ لأنه لما أقر أنه عبد الفلان، أقر أنه ليس له استقلال ولا قدرة على نفسه، فلا يعمل بإقراره، ويكون عبداً لذي اليد لا للخارج، إلا بالبينة، وشهادة العبد ليست بحجة.
فإن قلت : الإقرار بالرق ضرر، وأقوال الصبي في المضارّ ليست بموجبة ، فكان الواجب أن لا يعتبر في حق الصبي. قلنا : الرق لم يثبت بقول الصبي بل بدعوى ذي اليد ؛ لعدم المعارض بدعوى الحرية ؛ لأنه لما صار في يد المدعي بقي كالقماش في يده، فيقبل إقراره عليه، ولا نُسَلِّمُ أنَّ الإقرار بالرقّ من المضارّ ؛ لأنه يمكنه التدارك بعده بدعوى الحرية، إذ التناقض فيه لا يمنع صحة الدعوى بخلاف الإقرار بالدين.

وأما إذا كان الصبي لا يعبر عن نفسه فهو عبد للذي هو في يده ؛ لأنه بمنزلة المتاع، فيكون ملكاً لمن هو في يده إن ادعاه ؛ لعدم المعارض من يد على نفسه حقيقة أو

Book of Claims, Chapter of Two Men's Claim. Meaning: he speaks and knows what he says. So when a man claims that a child is his slave, and the child says: I am free, then the child's statement is accepted because that child is in his own hand, so he is the owner of the hand, and the claimant is external, and the statement of the owner of the hand is accepted. And this is because the principle is that every human has a hand over himself due to dignity, so the claim of anyone that he is his slave is not accepted when he denies it, except with evidence, like an adult. I say: the hand over a person is not an apparent evidence of ownership, for if someone sees a person in another's hand, behaving like owners, it is not permissible to testify that he is his property, because the principle is freedom of humans, so the fact that a child who does not express himself is a slave of the owner of the hand is problematic. (And the wall belongs to the one whose beams are upon it legally, and if he grows up and claims freedom, his statement is not accepted, so the hand of the one in whose hand he is does not cease except with evidence, because slavery was established upon him during his childhood, so that is not overturned except with evidence, as they have clarified). [1] His statement: 'it is not permissible to testify...' etc. requires consideration; because he has clarified in the Book of Testimony in this book and in all books that if a man sees a child who does not express himself, it is permissible for him to testify that he is his, since he has no authority over himself according to law, so he belongs to the owner of the hand. So reflect on it.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Da'wa discusses the legal status of a child (sabi) when there is a claim of slavery. The central issue is whether the child can be considered as having a 'hand' over himself (yad 'ala nafsihi) for the purpose of claim. The text distinguishes two scenarios: (1) The child is mu'abbir (able to express himself coherently) – in this case, he is considered to be 'in his own hand', meaning he has legal capacity regarding claims of personal status. Therefore, if he says 'I am free', his statement is accepted as the default principle is freedom and he is the owner of his own hand. The claimant must bring evidence. (2) The child is ghayr mu'abbir (unable to express himself) – he is treated like inanimate property, so if someone claims him as a slave and he is in that person's hand, the claim is accepted due to possession (yad) being evidence of ownership. The text addresses several objections, including the principle of original freedom (asl al-hurriyah) by birth from free parents. The Hanafi response is that the yad (hand/possession) overrides this principle because it is evidence of ownership, akin to other property. The author [the commentator] also criticizes the statement that one cannot testify that a child in someone's hand is his property, clarifying that according to the Book of Testimony, if the child cannot express himself, such testimony is allowed. The passage ends with a brief note about the wall (which transitions to the next topic).

Key Terms
المعبر (al-mu'abbir): A child who can speak coherently and understand what he says, capable of expressing his legal status.
اليد (al-yad): Possession or control; a legal concept that establishes a presumption of ownership unless challenged.
الرق (al-riqq): The status of being a slave, entailing lack of legal capacity and ownership of self.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, if a child who cannot express himself (ghayr mu'abbir) is in the hand of someone who claims him as a slave, and the child remains silent, what is the ruling?
A) The child is presumed free and the claimant must bring evidence.
B) The child is considered a slave of the claimant because the hand is evidence of ownership.
C) The child is considered a slave of the claimant only if the child acknowledges it.
D) The case is dismissed because a child cannot be owned.

Key Takeaway
A child's ability to express himself determines his legal capacity to claim freedom; a non-expressive child is effectively property in the hand of the possessor.
