Wall Ownership by Beams and the Hanafi View on Customary Signs
Ruling on wall ownership with beam evidence and rebuttal of al-Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wall ownership in a beam dispute, distinguish it from the Shafi'i position, and apply the principle of customary signs (amarat) and qiyas to similar property disputes.

Core Idea
In a dispute over a wall, the Hanafi school awards ownership to the one whose beams are on it — custom and usage are decisive signs.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents a classic Hanafi ruling on resolving property disputes when two parties claim a wall. The Hanafi school relies on customary evidence (amarat) and analogical reasoning to determine ownership, countering al-Shafi'i's objection that such signs are uncertain. You will learn how the Hanafi jurists argue for the probative value of beams as a sign of ownership, and how this connects to broader principles of evidence in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school rules: the wall belongs to the owner of the beams, not the one with planks attached.
The key term is 'jadh'u' (beams) vs 'muradi' (plank attacher).
2
Identify the opposing view
State al-Shafi'i's objection: beams are not a decisive sign because they could be from ownership, loan (ariyah), or usurpation (ghasb).
The uncertainty argument is rooted in epistemological caution.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Examine the evidence: custom (urf) and analogy (qiyas). The wall is built for beams, so beams are an integral sign of ownership. Analogies are drawn to disputes over household goods (suitability) and a beast with a saddle.
The Hanafi school upholds that probable signs (amarat) are sufficient in conflicting claims.
4
Evaluate the rebuttal
Understand the Hanafi response: the beams are a stronger indicator because they are integral to the wall's use, whereas planks are mere attachments. Possibility of loan or usurpation does not negate the probative value in a dispute.
The rebuttal relies on the principle that amarat can overrule mere possibility.
5
Determine the practical difference
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence): under Hanafi law, the wall is awarded to the beam owner without further evidence; under Shafi'i law, additional proof (e.g., witnesses or oaths) would be required.
This difference affects the burden of proof in property disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢] قوله : والحائط... الخ ؛ صورة المسألة أنَّ الحائط لرجل عليه جذوع، أو هو متصل ببنائه، وللآخر عليه خشبات توضع على الجذوع ، فذلك الحائط يكون لصاحب الجذوع، والاتصال دون المرادي، وقال الشافعي : لا يرجح بوضع الجذوع ؛ لأنه يحتمل أن يكون ملكه أو عارية أو غصباً ، فلا يكون حجة مع الاحتمال. ولنا : إن صاحب الجذوع هو المستعمل ، وصاحب المرادي صاحب تعلق ، والبناء يبنى للجذوع عادة، لا لوضع المرادي، فوضع الجذوع علامة ملكه، ومثل هذا يصلح أن يكون علامة كما إذا اختلف الزوجان في متاع البيت حتى جعل الترجيح بالصلاحية كما تقدم ذكره، فصار نظير دابَّةٍ تنازعا فيها ؛ ولأحدهما [حمل] عليها، (۱) الأصل في الإنسان الحرية يبطل إذا اعترض عليه ما يدلُّ على خلافه، وثبوت اليد دليل على خلاف ذلك الأصل ؛ لأنه دليلُ الملك ، فيبطل به ذلك الأصل. ينظر : ((الإيضاح»(ق۱۲۲/أ). (۲) ينظر: التبيين (٤) : (۳۲۸) ، و«النتائج»(٧ : ٢٦٥).
[2] His statement: 'And the wall...' etc.; the picture of the issue is that the wall belongs to a man who has beams upon it, or it is connected to his building, and another person has planks placed on those beams. So the wall belongs to the owner of the beams, and the connection is not for the one with planks. Al-Shafi'i said: it is not decided by the placement of beams, because it is possible that it is his property, or a loan, or usurpation, so it is not proof with possibility. Our proof: the owner of the beams is the user, while the owner of the planks is merely an attacher. Buildings are usually built for beams, not for placing planks, so the placement of beams is a sign of his ownership. And such a thing can be a sign, just as when spouses differ over household goods, preference is given based on suitability (salihiyyah) as previously mentioned. So it becomes analogous to a beast that they dispute over, and one of them has a saddle on it. (1) The principle of freedom in humans is nullified when something indicating otherwise intervenes, and the establishment of the hand (yad) is evidence contrary to that principle, because it is evidence of ownership, so that principle is nullified. See: al-Idah (122a). (2) See: al-Tabyin (4:328) and al-Nata'ij (7:265).
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage discusses a dispute between two parties over ownership of a wall. One party has beams (jadh'u') placed on the wall, and the other has planks (khashabat) placed on those beams. The Hanafi ruling is that the wall belongs to the owner of the beams, not the one with the planks. This is based on custom (urf) and usage: beams are integral to the wall's construction, whereas planks are merely attached. Al-Shafi'i objects that the placement of beams could be due to ownership, loan, or usurpation, so it is not decisive. The Hanafi response uses analogy (qiyas): just as in disputes between spouses over household goods, preference is given based on suitability (salihiyyah) for each item, similarly here the beams indicate ownership because the wall is typically built for beams. A further analogy is made with a dispute over a beast of burden: the one who has a saddle on it is more likely the owner. The passage also includes a footnote reiterating the principle that freedom is the default but can be overridden by evidence like possession (yad).

Key Terms
الجذوع (al-jadh'u): Wooden beams placed on top of a wall to support a roof or structure; a sign of ownership in disputes.
المرادي (al-muradi): The one who attaches planks (khashabat) to the beams on the wall, i.e., someone who uses the wall but does not own it.
الصلاحية (al-salihiyyah): Suitability; the legal principle that in disputes over property, the item is assigned to the party for whose use it is customarily suited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, if two people dispute over a wall, one having beams on it and the other having planks placed on those beams, who is the owner of the wall?
A) The one with the planks, because he is using the wall.
B) The one with the beams, because the wall is typically built for beams.
C) Both are considered co-owners.
D) The wall belongs to the one who can provide witnesses.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi property disputes, customary signs like beams are decisive evidence of ownership over mere attachments.

Definition and Priority of Irtibāṭ Tarbīʿ in Wall Disputes
Intra-Hanafi dispute over the definition of Tarbīʿ

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi majority definition of Irtibāṭ Tarbīʿ and explain why it takes priority over other attachments like beams (Jidhʿ) and poles (Harādī).

Core Idea
Irtibāṭ Tarbīʿ: interlocking bricks on both sides establish the strongest claim to a shared wall.

The Hanafi school recognizes different levels of attachment to a disputed wall, with Irtibāṭ Tarbīʿ (interlocking connection) conferring the highest priority. This lesson unpacks the precise meaning of Tarbīʿ, the intra-madhhab disagreement between al-Karkhī and the majority (Abū Yūsuf and most later scholars), and the practical consequences for wall ownership disputes. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for qaḍā' (judicial rulings) in property cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contending attachments
List the types of attachment mentioned: Tarbīʿ, Jidhʿ (beams), Harādī (poles), and juxtaposition (mulāzaqa). Note their priority order.
The matn gives highest priority to Tarbīʿ over Harādī, but the commentary expands the hierarchy.
2
Define Irtibāṭ Tarbīʿ
From the commentary, extract the core meaning: interlocking of half-bricks between two walls, forming a square enclosure. Distinguish this from mere adjacency or piercing.
The term 'Tarbīʿ' derives from the square shape intended.
3
Analyse the intra-madhhab dispute
Compare al-Karkhī's requirement (interlocking on both sides plus a third opposite wall) with the majority view (only interlocking on both sides, no third wall required).
Use the madhhab_dispute_map to trace the arguments and tarjīḥ.
4
Apply the majority definition to a scenario
Given a wall with bricks interlocking on both sides into a neighbour's wall, rule that the neighbour has Irtibāṭ Tarbīʿ and is preferred over a beam owner.
The beam owner is not forced to remove beams because the Tarbīʿ owner's right is manifest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

" وعن أبي يوسف : تفسير اتصال التربيع الذي به ترجح صاحب الاتصال على صاحب الجذوع اتصال جانبي الحائط المتنازع فيه بمداخلة أنصاف اللبن بحائط لأحدهما. فأَمَّا اتصالُ الحائطين بحائط أخرى في مقابلة الحائط المتنازع فيه غير معتبر، وعليه أكثر المشايخ ، منهم : شمس الأئمة السَّرَخْسِي الله ، فهو أولى من صاحب الجذوع، ولا يؤمر صاحب الجذوع برفع الجذوع ؛ لأنَّ صاحب الاتصال استحق الحائط المتنازع فيه بنوع ظاهر ، فلا يستحق به برفع الجذوع على صاحب الجذوع. انتهى. وذكر في «الكفاية» (۳) نقلا عن ( حيطان) «الذخيرة» تفسير التربيع : إذا كان الحائط مـن مـدر أو أجر أن تكون أنصاف لين الحائط المتنازع فيه داخلة في أنصاف لبن الغير المتنازع فيه، وأنصاف لبن غير المتنازع فيه داخلة في المتنازع فيه، وإن كان الجدار من خشب فالتربيع أن يكون ساحة أحدهما مركبة في الأخرى، وأما إذا نقبَ فادخل لا يكون تربيعاً، ويكون اتصال مجاورة وملازقة. انتهى.
Or (a wall) connected to his building with a Tarbīʿ connection, not for one who has upon it Harādī, or connected to his building with a Tarbīʿ connection. The connection of Tarbīʿ: the joining of a wall to a wall such that the bricks of this wall interlock with the bricks of that wall. It is called a Tarbīʿ connection because they are built to surround a square place with two other walls. [1] His statement: 'Irtibāṭ Tarbīʿ' – he said in al-Muḥīṭ: The hands over the wall are of three ranks: Tarbīʿ connection, juxtaposition and adjacency, placement of beams, and alignment of building. So the most entitled is the owner of Tarbīʿ, then the owner of beams, then the owner of alignment. End. And the scholar al-Baḥr Qāḍī Khān said in his Fatāwā: They disputed over the interpretation of Irtibāṭ Tarbīʿ. Al-Karkhī said in his interpretation: The interlocking of half-bricks from the two sides of the disputed wall with two walls belonging to one of them, the two walls being connected to a wall of his opposite the disputed wall, so that it becomes square like a dome, thus all is in the rule of one building, and some mashāyikh adopted this. And from Abū Yūsuf: The interpretation of Irtibāṭ Tarbīʿ by which the owner of connection is preferred over the owner of beams is the connection of the two sides of the disputed wall with interlocking of half-bricks with a wall of one of them. As for the connection of the two walls with another wall opposite the disputed wall, it is not considered, and upon this are the majority of mashāyikh, among them Shams al-A’immah al-Sarakhsī, so he is more entitled than the owner of beams, and the owner of beams is not ordered to remove the beams, because the owner of connection has become entitled to the disputed wall by a manifest type, so he does not become entitled to removal of beams over the owner of beams. End. And it is mentioned in al-Kifāyah quoting from al-Dhakhīrah the interpretation of Tarbīʿ: If the wall is of mud or brick, that half-bricks of the disputed wall enter into half-bricks of the non-disputed wall, and half-bricks of the non-disputed wall enter into the disputed one. If the wall is of wood, then Tarbīʿ is that the courtyard of one is inserted into the other. But if he pierced and inserted, it is not Tarbīʿ, and it is a connection of adjacency and juxtaposition. End.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute over the definition of Irtibāṭ Tarbīʿ (interlocking connection) and its priority in wall ownership disputes. The matn states that a person whose building is connected by Tarbīʿ has priority over one who has only Harādī (poles). The commentary explains Tarbīʿ as interlocking bricks between two walls to form a square enclosure, hence the name. Al-Muḥīṭ ranks owners: first Tarbīʿ, then beams (Jidhūʿ), then alignment. The main dispute: Al-Karkhī requires that both sides of the disputed wall interlock with two walls of the claimant, and those two walls connect to a third opposite wall forming a square. Abū Yūsuf and the majority (including al-Sarakhsī) require only that both sides of the disputed wall interlock with one wall of the claimant; the opposite wall is not needed. The majority view is relied upon. The quote from al-Kifāyah adds that for mud/brick walls, half-bricks must interlock; for wood, the courtyards must interpenetrate; mere insertion by piercing does not count. The practical consequence: According to the majority, the Tarbīʿ owner is preferred over the beam owner, and the beam owner is not forced to remove his beams because the Tarbīʿ owner's right is manifest.

Key Terms
اتصال التربيع (Irtibāṭ al-Tarbīʿ): Interlocking connection of two walls such that bricks interpenetrate, forming a square enclosure.
هَرَادي (Harādī): Poles or ends of reeds placed on top of beams for roofing.
جذع (Jidhʿ): A beam or palm trunk used for roofing, placed on the wall.
بواري (Bawārī): Reed mats used for roofing, similar to Harādī in not establishing a hand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars (Abū Yūsuf and most mashāyikh), what is the minimum requirement for Irtibāṭ Tarbīʿ?
A) Interlocking of both sides of the disputed wall with two walls of the claimant, plus a third opposite wall.
B) Interlocking of both sides of the disputed wall with one wall of the claimant, without needing a third opposite wall.
C) Only one side of the disputed wall must interlock with a wall of the claimant.
D) The disputed wall must be completely encased in a square structure like a dome.

Key Takeaway
Irtibāṭ Tarbīʿ, defined as interlocking on both sides of the disputed wall, gives the owner the highest priority in wall disputes, per the Hanafi majority.

Harādī and Bawārī Do Not Establish Wall Ownership
Ruling that poles and mats on a wall do not establish legal possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that Harādī and Bawārī do not establish a legal hand over a disputed wall, and explain why only structural beams confer possession.

Core Idea
Poles and mats on a wall do not prove ownership—only structural beams establish a legal hand.

In neighbor disputes over a wall, not all attachments are equal. While a beam (jidhʿ) can indicate ownership, mere poles or reed mats used for covering do not. This lesson examines why Hanafi law treats Harādī and Bawārī as insufficient to establish a hand.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested items
Note that the dispute involves Harādī (poles) and Bawārī (reed mats) placed on a wall.
These are not structural supports.
2
Understand the legal principle
A 'hand' over a wall is established only if the object is integral to the wall's purpose (roofing via beams).
The wall is built for beams, not for poles or mats.
3
Apply the ruling
If one neighbor has only Harādī or Bawārī and the other has nothing, the wall is shared equally.
Neither party is preferred.
4
Note the contrast with a single beam
If one has a single beam, scholars differ: some say it does not establish a hand, others say it does because the wall might be built for it.
This ikhtilaf shows the principle's nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[١] قوله: توضع على الجذوع ; ويلقى عليها التراب، فإنها غير معتبرة، وكذا البواري ; لأنه لم يكن استعمالاً له وضعاً إذ الحائط لا يبنى لهما بل للتسقيف، وهو لا يمكن على المرادي والبواري. كذا في «الدرر»(۳). [٢] قوله : فهو بينهما ; أي فذلك الحائط يقضي بـه بينهما على السوية ; لاستوائهما ; لأنَّ وضع المرادي والبواري لا يثبت لصاحبهما على الحائط يداً ; لأنّ الحائط للتسقيف ; وذلك بوضع الجذوع عليه، لا بوضع الهرادي والبواري ; لأنَّ وضع المرادي والبواري إنما يكون للاستقلال، والحائط لا يبنى له ، ذكره العيني(٣) ، وغيره. وهذا إذا علم أنه في أيديهما، ولا يرجّح صاحب الهرادي، صرح به الزيلعي في «شرح الكنز»). وأيضاً قال فيه : وإن كان لأحدهما جذع واحد ولا شيء للآخر اختلف المشايخ فيه، قيل : هما سواء ; لأنَّ الواحد لا يعتد به ، وقيل : صاحب الجذع أولى; لأنَّ الحائط قد يبنى لجذع واحد، وإن كان ذلك غير غالب، وإن كان لأحدهما هرادي أو بواري ولا شيء للآخر فهو بينهما، والمرادي لا تعتبر. انتهى.
Rather it is between the two neighbors if they dispute, and an owner of a room in a house is like owners of rooms from it regarding the right of its courtyard. Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: When two neighbors dispute over a wall, and one has Harādī (poles) or Bawārī (reed mats) on the wall while the other has nothing, the wall is shared equally between them. The commentary explains that Harādī and Bawārī are not considered valid attachments because they are not used to support the wall; rather the wall is built to support beams (Jidhūʿ) for roofing. Harādī and Bawārī are only for covering (e.g., to hold earth) and do not establish a 'hand' (legal possession) over the wall. Therefore, the owner of Harādī is not preferred over the neighbor with nothing; they are equal. However, if one has a single beam (Jidhʿ), there is a difference of opinion: some say the single beam is not enough (equal), others say the beam owner is entitled because the wall might be built for that beam. But if one has only Harādī or Bawārī, the wall is between them equally. The source is al-Durar, al-ʿAynī, and al-Zaylaʿī.

Key Terms
هرادي (Harādī): Poles or pieces of wood placed on beams for roofing; not considered a valid legal attachment.
بواري (Bawārī): Reed mats used for covering on beams; same ruling as Harādī.
جذع (Jidhʿ): A single beam placed on the wall for roofing.

Key Takeaway
Harādī and Bawārī do not establish legal possession over a wall; only structural beams can create a hand.

Equal Courtyard Rights for Joint Owners of Rooms
Ruling statement of equality in shared courtyard rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on equal courtyard rights for room owners in a shared house and apply it to a common property dispute.

Core Idea
Every room owner has equal rights to the courtyard, regardless of number of rooms.

When multiple people own different rooms in the same house, questions often arise about who controls the shared courtyard. This lesson examines a clear Hanafi maxim: ownership of a single room gives exactly the same rights to the courtyard as ownership of any other room. The rule prevents larger room owners from dominating common spaces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the shared space
Understand that the scenario involves multiple owners of separate rooms within one house, all sharing a common courtyard (sāḥah).
2
State the maxim
The text declares that an owner of a single room is like (i.e., has the same rights as) owners of all other rooms regarding the courtyard.
This is a straightforward equality principle.
3
Understand the legal implication
No room owner can claim superior rights to the courtyard based on the number of rooms owned. All co‑owners have equal usufruct and management rights in the shared area unless they agree otherwise.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Consider a house with three rooms: Zayd owns Room A, ‘Amr owns Room B, and Bakr owns Room C. Zayd cannot prevent ‘Amr or Bakr from using the courtyard simply because his room is larger.
The ruling prevents arbitrary exclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And an owner of a room in a house is like owners of rooms from it regarding the right of its courtyard. [3] His statement: 'and an owner of a room... etc.' – meaning: if a room in a house that has many rooms is in the hand of Zayd.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents a legal maxim: The owner of a single room in a multi‑room house has the same rights to the courtyard (sāḥah) as the owners of other rooms. In other words, room ownership does not grant superior rights over the common courtyard; all owners share equally. The commentary begins an example (if a room is in the hand of Zayd) but is cut off. The principle is based on the idea that the courtyard is a shared space for all residents, and the number of rooms does not affect each owner's right.

Key Terms
ساحة (Sāḥah): Courtyard or open area within a house shared by all owners.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) He has rights proportional to his room size.
D) It depends on the owner's gender.

Key Takeaway
Every room owner in a shared house has equal rights to the courtyard; no one can claim superior access based on the size or number of their rooms.

Land Dispute: Evidence, Oaths, and Nukul in Hanafi Qada
Procedure for land dispute with evidence and oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when both claimants produce evidence in a land dispute and apply the oath/nukl procedure correctly.

Core Idea
When both claimants prove possession, the land is ruled in both hands; oaths and nukl determine the outcome if evidence is weak.

Land disputes are common in Islamic courts, but physical possession is not directly observable. This passage explains how a Hanafi judge handles conflicting claims: if both bring evidence, the land is treated as jointly possessed. If only one has evidence or physical improvements, his claim is stronger. The passage also details the oath procedure when evidence is lacking, including the effect of refusal (nukl).

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimants and their evidence
Two men each claim the land is in their possession. Determine whether both produce evidence (barhana), or only one, or physical signs like bricks, building, or digging exist.
The type of evidence changes the ruling.
2
Apply the ruling for joint evidence
If both produce evidence, the judge rules the land is in both their hands (qada biyadihima). This is because land cannot be physically brought to court, so evidence establishes possession.
This is a special Hanafi rule for immovable property.
3
Apply the ruling for one-sided evidence or improvements
If only one produces evidence, or the land has bricks, building, or digging (which indicate possession), then that claimant's possession is confirmed.
Physical acts are considered as circumstantial evidence.
4
Walk through the oath and nukl procedure
If each demands an oath from the other that it is in his hand, both swear with certainty that it is in the other's hand; then the land is left pending (tawqif) until truth emerges. If both refuse (nukla), each gets the half that was in the other's hand. If only one refuses, the one who swears gets the entire land. If a third party holds it, nukl does not affect him.
Nukl is considered a form of admission in Hanafi law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
أرض ادعى رجلٌ أنها في يده، وآخر كذلك، وبَرْهَنا، قضى بيدهما، فإن بَرْهَنَ أحدهما أو كان لبن فيها ، أو بَنَى ، أو حَفَر
[١]
(أرض ادعى رجل أنها في يده، وآخر كذلك ، وبَرْهَنا، قضى بيدهما ،
[٢]-
فإن بَرْهَنَ أحدهما أو كان لبن فيها، أو بَنَى ، أو حَفَر مثلاً، والبيوت الباقية في يد بكر، فذو بيت ،واحد كذي بيوت في حق ساحتها ،
فالساحة بينهما نصفان ؛ لأنَّ الترجيح بالقوة لا بالكثرة، كما عرفت غير مرة. وأيضاً الساحة يحتاج الملاك إليها لاستعمالها في أنواع المرافق من المرور فيها ، والتوضؤ، وكسر الحطب، ووضع الأمتعة، ونحو ذلك من أنواع المنافع، وهما في ذلك سواء، فينصف بينهما ، فصارت نظير الطريق، فإنّه يستوي فيه صاحب البيتِ وصاحب المنزل، وصاحب الدار، وصاحب بيت وصاحب بيوت.
بخلاف ما إذا تنازعا في الشرب، فإنّه يقدَّرُ بالأرض لقدر سقيها ؛ لأنَّ الشرب يحتاج إليه لأجل سقي الأرض، فعند كثرة الأراضي تكثر الحاجة إليه، فيتقدر بقدر الأراضي، بخلاف الانتفاع بالساحة، فإنه لا يختلف باختلاف الأملاك كالمرور في
الطريق.
[١] قوله : قضى بيدهما ؛ لأنَّ اليد في الأرض غير مشاهدة ؛ لتعذر إحضارها في مجلس القاضي، وما غاب عن علم القاضي، فالبينة تثبته).
[٢] قوله : فإن برهن.... الخ ؛ وإن طلب كلُّ واحدٍ يمين صاحبه ما هي في يده حلف كل واحد منهما ما هي في يد صاحبه على البتات، فإن حلفا لم يقض باليد لهما، وبرئ كل واحد منهما عن دعوى صاحبه، وتوقفُ الدَّارُ إلى أن يظهر حقيقة الحال.
وإن نكلا قضى لكل واحداً منهما بالنصف الذي في يد صاحبه، وإن نكـلّ أحدهما قضى عليه بكلّها للحالف : نصفُها الذي كان في يده، ونصفها [الذي كان للذي في يد صاحبه بنكوله ، وإن كانت الدار في يد ثالث لم تنزع من يده ؛ لأنَّ النكول
ليس بحجة في حق الثالث. كذا في «الكفاية»(٢).
Cream of the End for the Essence of the Care. A land: a man claimed that it is in his hand, and another similarly, and they both produced evidence; the judge ruled it in their hands. If one produced evidence, or there were bricks in it, or he built, or he dug, for example, and the remaining rooms were in the hand of Bakr, then an owner of one room is like an owner of many rooms regarding its courtyard. So the courtyard is half between them, because preference is by strength not by quantity, as you have learned many times. Also, the owners need the courtyard for its use in various amenities: passing through, performing ablution, breaking wood, placing luggage, and similar types of benefits; they are equal in that, so it is divided in half between them. Thus it becomes analogous to the road, in which the owner of one house and the owner of a mansion, the owner of a house and the owner of many houses are equal. Unlike if they dispute over drinking water, then it is measured by the land according to its irrigation capacity, because drinking water is needed for irrigating land; when there are many lands, the need is greater, so it is measured by the amount of land. In contrast, use of the courtyard does not differ with different ownership, like passing on the road. [1] His statement: 'He ruled it in their hands' because the hand over land is not observable, because it is difficult to bring it to the judge's court, and what is absent from the judge's knowledge, evidence establishes it. [2] His statement: 'If he produced evidence... etc.' And if each one demands the oath of the other that it is in his hand, each one swears with absolute certainty that it is in the other's hand; if they both swear, then it is not adjudged to be in the hand of either, and each is acquitted of the other's claim, and the house is suspended until the truth appears. If they both refuse (nukl), then he adjudges each one the half that was in the other's hand. If one refuses, he adjudges the whole to the one who swore: his half that was in his hand and the half that was in the other's hand due to the refusal. And if the house is in the hand of a third party, it is not taken from his hand because the refusal (nukl) is not a proof against the third party. As in 'al-Kifayah'.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage addresses the procedure for a dispute over land ownership when two claimants both claim the land is in their possession. The commentary explains the Hanafi ruling: if both provide evidence (bayyinah), the judge rules the land is in both hands. This is because physical possession of land is not directly observable in court. The text then discusses the situation where one has additional evidence like bricks, building, or digging—these strengthen his claim. The commentary then applies the principle of courtyard division: when one owns a single room and another owns multiple rooms in the same house, the courtyard is divided equally because the need for amenities (passage, ablution, etc.) is the same regardless of the number of rooms. This analogy is drawn from the road, where all users are equal. The ruling contrasts with water rights (shurb), which are proportional to land area because water is needed for irrigation. The passage also details the oath procedure: if both parties swear that the land is in the other's hand, the land is left pending; if both refuse (nukl), each gets half; if one swears and one refuses, the one who swears gets the whole. If a third party holds the land, his possession is not disturbed because nukl does not bind him. The authority weight is from the later fatawa and commentaries, building on the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
برهنا (barhanā): They both produced evidence (bayyinah) in court to support their claim.
لبن (libn): Unbaked bricks found on the land; their presence indicates improvement and supports the claim of possession.
نكول (nukūl): Refusal to take an oath when demanded; in Hanafi law, this can result in judgment against the refuser.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a Hanafi land dispute, if both claimants produce evidence, the judge rules the land is:
A) In the hands of the one with stronger evidence.
B) In both hands (joint possession).
C) Left pending until further proof.
D) Divided according to the number of rooms each owns.

Key Takeaway
When both claimants prove possession of land, the Hanafi judge rules it jointly held; if evidence is lacking, the oath/nukl procedure determines the outcome, but third parties are unaffected by nukl.

Courtyard Division: Equal Shares vs Proportional Water Rights
Rule for dividing a shared courtyard equally vs proportional water rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school divides a shared courtyard equally regardless of the number of rooms owned, and distinguish this from proportional water rights.

Core Idea
Courtyard use is shared equally because amenities benefit all owners alike, unlike water rights which are proportional to land area.

When a house has a shared courtyard, owners often have different numbers of rooms. Does the owner with more rooms get a larger share of the courtyard? The Hanafi school says no: the courtyard is divided equally because all owners have equal need for passage, ablution, and other common amenities. This contrasts with water rights (shurb), which are proportional to land area because water is used for irrigation – more land means more need.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two rulings
A house has several rooms: one owner has a single room, another has multiple rooms. They share a courtyard (saha). The question: how is the courtyard divided? The passage also contrasts this with a dispute over water rights (shurb).
Two different types of common property: courtyard (shared use) vs water (consumption).
2
Understand the reasoning for equal courtyard division
The courtyard is needed for amenities: passing through, ablution, breaking wood, storing luggage, etc. All owners need these equally, regardless of how many rooms they own. Therefore, the courtyard is divided equally (nisfan).
The analogy is with a road (tariq): all users have equal right to pass, no matter their house size.
3
Contrast with the water rights rule
Water rights (shurb) are needed for irrigation. The amount of land determines the need for water – more land needs more water. Therefore, water rights are divided proportionally to land area (yuqaddaru bi al-ard).
The difference is in the nature of the right: use vs consumption.
4
Apply the principle to other shared amenities
The principle is that if the benefit is the same for all co-owners (like using a courtyard or road), division is equal. If the benefit varies with the size of the property (like water for irrigation), division is proportional.
This helps determine rulings for other shared facilities like stairs, wells, or gardens.

Source Text

Land Dispute Resolution: Evidence and Usage
Ruling with proof on land disputes using evidence and usage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for resolving land disputes when both claimants present evidence or when one claimant's usage indicates possession, including the equal sharing of a courtyard.

Core Idea
When two claimants both present evidence for land, they share it; usage like building or digging proves possession.

This lesson examines the Hanafi procedural rules for land disputes. The qadi (judge) must decide based on evidence (bayyinah) and observable signs of possession (yad). The text explains that if both claimants prove possession, the land is shared equally. If only one has evidence or has used the land (e.g., built or dug), he is awarded possession. The principle 'tarjih bil quwwah la bil kathrah' — preference by strength, not quantity — applies: the owner of one house and the owner of many houses have equal rights to a shared courtyard.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Two claimants both claim possession of land. Determine what the judge does if both bring evidence: he shares it between them.
2
Distinguish when only one has evidence or usage signs
If one presents evidence or there are signs of his usage (brick, building, digging), the judge rules for that person. Usage is evidence of possession.
Usage (istimal) includes building, digging, or placing bricks.
3
Understand the rule for a shared courtyard
The courtyard is divided equally among all owners regardless of the number of houses they own. Why? Because the need for use (passing, ablution, etc.) is equal. The text analogizes it to a public road.
Analogy (qiyas): courtyard is like a road where all have equal passage rights.
4
Contrast water rights (shurb)
Water for irrigation is proportional to land area because need scales with land size. This is unlike courtyard usage.
Shurb is based on the land's irrigation need; courtyard use is equal regardless of property size.
5
Apply the maxim 'al-tarjih bil quwwah la bil kathrah'
Preference is by strength of proof, not by number of parties. The owner of one house and the owner of many houses have equal rights to the courtyard.
Quality of evidence outweighs quantity of claimants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أرض ادعى رجلٌ أنها في يده، وآخر كذلك، وبَرْهَنا، قضى بيدهما، فإن بَرْهَنَ أحدهما أو كان لبن فيها، أو بَنَى، أو حَفَر مثلاً، والبيوت الباقية في يد بكر، فذو بيت واحد كذي بيوت في حق ساحتها، فالساحة بينهما نصفان؛ لأنَّ الترجيح بالقوة لا بالكثرة، كما عرفت غير مرة. وأيضاً الساحة يحتاج الملاك إليها لاستعمالها في أنواع المرافق من المرور فيها، والتوضؤ، وكسر الحطب، ووضع الأمتعة، ونحو ذلك من أنواع المنافع، وهما في ذلك سواء، فينصف بينهما، فصارت نظير الطريق، فإنّه يستوي فيه صاحب البيتِ وصاحب المنزل، وصاحب الدار، وصاحب بيت وصاحب بيوت. بخلاف ما إذا تنازعا في الشرب، فإنّه يقدَّرُ بالأرض لقدر سقيها؛ لأنَّ الشرب يحتاج إليه لأجل سقي الأرض، فعند كثرة الأراضي تكثر الحاجة إليه، فيتقدر بقدر الأراضي، بخلاف الانتفاع بالساحة، فإنه لا يختلف باختلاف الأملاك كالمرور في الطريق. قوله: قضى بيدهما؛ لأنَّ اليد في الأرض غير مشاهدة؛ لتعذر إحضارها في مجلس القاضي، وما غاب عن علم القاضي، فالبينة تثبته. قوله: فإن برهن... الخ؛ وإن طلب كلُّ واحدٍ يمين صاحبه ما هي في يده حلف كل واحد منهما ما هي في يد صاحبه على البتات، فإن حلفا لم يقض باليد لهما، وبرئ كل واحد منهما عن دعوى صاحبه، وتوقفُ الدَّارُ إلى أن يظهر حقيقة الحال. وإن نكلا قضى لكل واحداً منهما بالنصف الذي في يد صاحبه، وإن نكـلّ أحدهما قضى عليه بكلّها للحالف: نصفُها الذي كان في يده، ونصفها الذي كان للذي في يد صاحبه بنكوله، وإن كانت الدار في يد ثالث لم تنزع من يده؛ لأنَّ النكول ليس بحجة في حق الثالث. كذا في «الكفاية». قوله: قضى بيده؛ فإن الاستعمال دليل اليد. قوله: قضى بيده؛ أما إذا برهن أحدهما فلـقـيـام الحجة؛ لأنَّ اليد حق مقصود، فلا يستحق أحدهما بغير حجّة، وأما إذا كان ليس فيها لبن أو مبني أو حفر؛ فلأن الاستعمال فيها دليل اليد، وهو قد وجد.
A piece of land about which a man claimed that it is in his possession, and another likewise, and they both presented evidence, the judge ruled it in both their possession. If one presented evidence, or there was brick in it, or he built, or dug, for example, and the remaining houses are in the possession of Bakr, then the owner of one house is like the owner of many houses with regard to the courtyard: the courtyard is divided between them in half, because preference is by strength not by quantity, as you have known more than once. Also, the courtyard is needed by the owners for use in various facilities like passing through it, performing ablution, breaking firewood, placing goods, and similar types of benefits, and they are equal in that, so it is divided in half between them. Thus it becomes analogous to a road, wherein the owner of a house and the owner of a residence, the owner of a house and the owner of many houses are equal. Contrary to when they dispute about water rights, for it is estimated according to the land and its capacity for irrigation, because water rights are needed for irrigating land; thus when there is more land, the need increases, so it is proportioned according to the amount of land. Unlike the use of the courtyard, it does not vary with the difference in properties, like passing on a road. His statement: "He judged it in their possession" because possession of land is not observable due to the impossibility of bringing it to the judge's court, and what is absent from the judge's knowledge, evidence establishes it. His statement: "If he presented evidence... etc.": And if each requests an oath from the other that it is not in his possession, each of them swears decisively that it is not in the other's possession; if both swear, the judge does not rule possession for them, and each is free from the other's claim, and the house is suspended until the truth appears. If both refuse, he rules for each the half that is in the other's possession. If one refuses, he rules against him for the whole to the one who swore: the half that was in his possession and the half that was in the other's possession due to his refusal. If the house is in the possession of a third party, it is not removed from his possession because refusal (nukul) is not evidence against a third party. Thus in al-Kifayah. His statement: "He judged it in his possession" because usage is evidence of possession. His statement: "He judged it in his possession" means: if one presented evidence, it is due to the establishment of proof, because possession is a valid right, so neither deserves it without proof; and if there was no brick, building, or digging, because usage in it is evidence of possession, and it has been found.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage from the text deals with land disputes between two claimants. It is an expansion of the ruling in the matn. The commentator explains the scenarios: if both claimants produce evidence (bayyinah) of possession, the judge rules that the land is in both their possession, meaning they share it. If only one presents evidence or there are signs of usage like bricks, construction, or digging, the judge rules in favor of that person because such usage indicates possession. However, if the dispute concerns a courtyard shared by multiple houses, the courtyard is divided equally among all owners regardless of the number of houses they own, because the need for usage (like passing, ablution, etc.) is equal. The commentator distinguishes this from water rights (shurb), which are allocated proportionally to land area because the need for water correlates with the size of land. The passage also discusses the procedure when both claimants demand oaths: if both swear that the land is not in the other's possession, the judge suspends judgment until the truth is known; if both refuse to swear (nukul), the judge gives each half; if one refuses, the other gets the whole. If a third party holds the land, refusal of oath does not affect the third party's possession. The legal reasoning (ta'lil) is that possession of land is not observable directly, so evidence or usage substitutes. The Hanafi position is that preference is given by strength of evidence, not quantity of houses.

Key Terms
الترجيح بالقوة لا بالكثرة (al-tarjīḥ bil-quwwah lā bil-kathrah): Preference in evidence is by strength of proof not by quantity of claimants
الشرب (al-shurb): Right to water for irrigation
الاستعمال (al-isti'māl): Usage, practical utilization of property, considered evidence of possession

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, how is a shared courtyard divided among owners if one owner has multiple houses and another has one house?
A) In proportion to the number of houses each owns
B) Equally between them
C) The owner with more houses gets two-thirds
D) It is given entirely to the one who built first

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, land disputes are resolved by sharing if both have evidence, or awarding to the one with usage signs; courtyards are shared equally because need is equal, unlike water rights which are proportional to land size.

Oaths and Refusal (Nukul) in Property Disputes
Ruling on oath procedures in land disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for oaths (yamin) and refusal to swear (nukul) when both claimants demand oaths in a land dispute, and determine the effect on a third party's possession.

Core Idea
If both claimants swear that the land is not in the other's possession, the land is suspended; if one refuses to swear, he loses his claim.

This lesson continues the land dispute scenario. After presenting evidence (or if no evidence is presented), the claimants may demand oaths from each other. The Hanafi rules specify what happens when both swear, both refuse, or one refuses. The text also clarifies that refusal (nukul) does not affect a third party who holds the property. Understanding these procedures is essential for a qadi to reach a just outcome when evidence is weak.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition for oaths
After evidence is presented or if there is no clear evidence, each claimant may demand an oath from the other that the land is not in his possession.
The oath is taken decisively (bil-battat) that the land is not in the other's possession.
2
Both swear: outcome
If both swear, the judge does not rule possession for either. Each is free from the other's claim, and the property is suspended until the truth emerges.
Suspension means the status quo remains; neither gains possession through the oaths.
3
Both refuse to swear (nukul): outcome
If both refuse (nukul), the judge rules that each gets the half that was in the other's possession. So the land is effectively shared.
Nukul is treated as an implicit admission in this context.
4
One refuses: outcome
If only one refuses, the judge rules against him for the entire property. The one who swore gets his own half plus the other's half due to the refusal.
The refuser loses both halves: his own and the opponent's claim.
5
Third party possession: rule
If the property is in the possession of a third party, nukul by either claimant does not affect the third party's possession. The third party cannot be removed based on refusal.
Nukul is evidence only between the two disputants, not against a stranger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أرض ادعى رجلٌ أنها في يده، وآخر كذلك، وبَرْهَنا، قضى بيدهما، فإن بَرْهَنَ أحدهما أو كان لبن فيها، أو بَنَى، أو حَفَر مثلاً، والبيوت الباقية في يد بكر، فذو بيت واحد كذي بيوت في حق ساحتها، فالساحة بينهما نصفان؛ لأنَّ الترجيح بالقوة لا بالكثرة، كما عرفت غير مرة. وأيضاً الساحة يحتاج الملاك إليها لاستعمالها في أنواع المرافق من المرور فيها، والتوضؤ، وكسر الحطب، ووضع الأمتعة، ونحو ذلك من أنواع المنافع، وهما في ذلك سواء، فينصف بينهما، فصارت نظير الطريق، فإنّه يستوي فيه صاحب البيتِ وصاحب المنزل، وصاحب الدار، وصاحب بيت وصاحب بيوت. بخلاف ما إذا تنازعا في الشرب، فإنّه يقدَّرُ بالأرض لقدر سقيها؛ لأنَّ الشرب يحتاج إليه لأجل سقي الأرض، فعند كثرة الأراضي تكثر الحاجة إليه، فيتقدر بقدر الأراضي، بخلاف الانتفاع بالساحة، فإنه لا يختلف باختلاف الأملاك كالمرور في الطريق. قوله: قضى بيدهما؛ لأنَّ اليد في الأرض غير مشاهدة؛ لتعذر إحضارها في مجلس القاضي، وما غاب عن علم القاضي، فالبينة تثبته. قوله: فإن برهن... الخ؛ وإن طلب كلُّ واحدٍ يمين صاحبه ما هي في يده حلف كل واحد منهما ما هي في يد صاحبه على البتات، فإن حلفا لم يقض باليد لهما، وبرئ كل واحد منهما عن دعوى صاحبه، وتوقفُ الدَّارُ إلى أن يظهر حقيقة الحال. وإن نكلا قضى لكل واحداً منهما بالنصف الذي في يد صاحبه، وإن نكـلّ أحدهما قضى عليه بكلّها للحالف: نصفُها الذي كان في يده، ونصفها الذي كان للذي في يد صاحبه بنكوله، وإن كانت الدار في يد ثالث لم تنزع من يده؛ لأنَّ النكول ليس بحجة في حق الثالث. كذا في «الكفاية». قوله: قضى بيده؛ فإن الاستعمال دليل اليد. قوله: قضى بيده؛ أما إذا برهن أحدهما فلـقـيـام الحجة؛ لأنَّ اليد حق مقصود، فلا يستحق أحدهما بغير حجّة، وأما إذا كان ليس فيها لبن أو مبني أو حفر؛ فلأن الاستعمال فيها دليل اليد، وهو قد وجد.
A piece of land about which a man claimed that it is in his possession, and another likewise, and they both presented evidence, the judge ruled it in both their possession. If one presented evidence, or there was brick in it, or he built, or dug, for example, and the remaining houses are in the possession of Bakr, then the owner of one house is like the owner of many houses with regard to the courtyard: the courtyard is divided between them in half, because preference is by strength not by quantity, as you have known more than once. Also, the courtyard is needed by the owners for use in various facilities like passing through it, performing ablution, breaking firewood, placing goods, and similar types of benefits, and they are equal in that, so it is divided in half between them. Thus it becomes analogous to a road, wherein the owner of a house and the owner of a residence, the owner of a house and the owner of many houses are equal. Contrary to when they dispute about water rights, for it is estimated according to the land and its capacity for irrigation, because water rights are needed for irrigating land; thus when there is more land, the need increases, so it is proportioned according to the amount of land. Unlike the use of the courtyard, it does not vary with the difference in properties, like passing on a road. His statement: "He judged it in their possession" because possession of land is not observable due to the impossibility of bringing it to the judge's court, and what is absent from the judge's knowledge, evidence establishes it. His statement: "If he presented evidence... etc.": And if each requests an oath from the other that it is not in his possession, each of them swears decisively that it is not in the other's possession; if both swear, the judge does not rule possession for them, and each is free from the other's claim, and the house is suspended until the truth appears. If both refuse, he rules for each the half that is in the other's possession. If one refuses, he rules against him for the whole to the one who swore: the half that was in his possession and the half that was in the other's possession due to his refusal. If the house is in the possession of a third party, it is not removed from his possession because refusal (nukul) is not evidence against a third party. Thus in al-Kifayah. His statement: "He judged it in his possession" because usage is evidence of possession. His statement: "He judged it in his possession" means: if one presented evidence, it is due to the establishment of proof, because possession is a valid right, so neither deserves it without proof; and if there was no brick, building, or digging, because usage in it is evidence of possession, and it has been found.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage from the text deals with land disputes between two claimants. It is an expansion of the ruling in the matn. The commentator explains the scenarios: if both claimants produce evidence (bayyinah) of possession, the judge rules that the land is in both their possession, meaning they share it. If only one presents evidence or there are signs of usage like bricks, construction, or digging, the judge rules in favor of that person because such usage indicates possession. However, if the dispute concerns a courtyard shared by multiple houses, the courtyard is divided equally among all owners regardless of the number of houses they own, because the need for usage (like passing, ablution, etc.) is equal. The commentator distinguishes this from water rights (shurb), which are allocated proportionally to land area because the need for water correlates with the size of land. The passage also discusses the procedure when both claimants demand oaths: if both swear that the land is not in the other's possession, the judge suspends judgment until the truth is known; if both refuse to swear (nukul), the judge gives each half; if one refuses, the other gets the whole. If a third party holds the land, refusal of oath does not affect the third party's possession. The legal reasoning (ta'lil) is that possession of land is not observable directly, so evidence or usage substitutes. The Hanafi position is that preference is given by strength of evidence, not quantity of houses.

Key Terms
النكول (al-nukūl): Refusal to take an oath after being required to do so
اليمين (al-yamīn): Oath taken by a party in a dispute

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a Hanafi land dispute, if both claimants refuse to swear (nukul), what is the correct ruling?
A) The property is given to a third party
B) Each claimant gets the half that was in the other's possession
C) The property is suspended indefinitely
D) The judge rules against both and declares the property ownerless

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi qada, oaths and nukul resolve land disputes when evidence is lacking: mutual oaths suspend, both refusal shares, one refusal awards all to the swearer, and nukul does not affect third parties.

Usage as Evidence of Possession in Property Disputes
Ruling on possession via usage in property claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that usage establishes possession in property disputes lacking evidence, and explain the evidentiary role of istimal.

Core Idea
When two claimants have no proof, the user of the property is deemed the owner – usage is evidence of possession.

This passage deals with a common qada scenario: two people dispute ownership of a property, but neither has formal evidence. The Hanafi school provides a practical solution by treating continuous usage as a valid indicator of possession. Let's see how the text develops this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two scenarios
The text first states the ruling when one claimant produces evidence—judgment goes to him because possession is a right requiring proof. Then it discusses when there is no evidence and the property is of a type that can only be possessed through use.
Note the contrast: evidence (hujja) vs. usage (istimal).
2
Analyze usage as evidence
The phrase 'الاستعمال دليل اليد' establishes that usage serves as proof of possession. Understand that this is circumstantial evidence based on customary practice (urf).
Usage must be continuous and observable, e.g., occupying a building or drawing water from a well.
3
Recognize the basis in urf
The ruling is grounded in custom: people normally do not use another's property without right. Thus, usage creates a presumption of ownership in the absence of counter-evidence.
This is a zanni (probable) proof, but sufficient for judicial decision in Hanafi law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قضى بيده ) : فإن الاستعمال دليل اليد. قوله : قضى بيده ؛ أما إذا برهن أحدهما فلـقـيـام الحجة ؛ لأنَّ اليد حق مقصود، فلا يستحق أحدهما بغير حجّة، وأما إذا كان ليس فيها لبن] أو مبني أو حفر ؛ فلأن الاستعمال فيها دليل اليد، وهو قد وجد.
He judged by his possession: for usage is evidence of possession. His statement: He judged by his possession. As for when one of them produces evidence, then due to establishment of proof, because the hand (possession) is an intended right, so neither of them deserves it without proof. And as for when there is no milk? or building or excavation, then because usage in it is evidence of possession, and it has been found.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on claims of two men discusses a scenario where two claimants dispute ownership of a property but neither has clear evidence. The Hanafi ruling is that the judge should decide in favor of the one who has been using the property, because usage (istimal) is considered strong evidence of possession (yad). The text first explains that if one of them brings evidence (hujja), the judgment goes to him because possession is a right that cannot be established without proof. If there is no evidence and the property is something like a building or excavation (or possibly a well) that cannot be physically possessed except through use, then the fact that one claimant has been using it indicates his possession. The underlying legal principle is that continuous usage creates a presumption of ownership in the absence of contrary proof. This is a standard Hanafi position derived from custom (urf) and practical reasoning.

Key Terms
اليد (al-yad): Physical control or possession of property; the right of ownership implied by actual holding.
الاستعمال (al-istimal): The act of using or occupying property in a customary manner, indicating possession.
الحجة (al-hujja): Legal evidence or proof that establishes a claim in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is given to the one who can produce witnesses of purchase.
C) It is given to the one who has been using the property.
D) It is left in the hands of the current possessor regardless of usage.

Key Takeaway
Usage is a form of evidence in Hanafi law; when formal proof is absent, the judge may rely on istimal to assign possession.

Lineage of Twins: Necessity of Shared Paternity
Ruling on lineage of both twins when one is claimed after sale and freeing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the lineage of twins when one is sold and freed, and explain the rational proof that establishes both twins’ lineage to the seller.

Core Idea
Proving lineage of one twin necessarily proves lineage of the other due to shared conception.

Imagine a man sells one of two twins born at his home, and the buyer frees that twin. Later, the seller claims the other twin as his child. Does the freed twin's lineage go back to the seller or the buyer? The Hanafi school answers this with a clear ruling rooted in the biological reality of twinship. In this lesson, we unpack the ruling and the rational necessity behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and ruling
Read the passage and determine the factual scenario and the legal outcome: what is the ruling on lineage and the freeing?
Note that the seller claims the other twin after the first was sold and freed.
2
Extract the rational proof
Find the phrase 'li'anna min darurati thubuti nasabi ahadihima thubutu al-akhar' and understand why the lineage of one necessarily entails the other.
The proof is aqli (rational) based on the impossibility of separate conception between twins.
3
Define twins according to the text
Read the definition of taw'am: two children born less than six months apart from the same pregnancy. Understand why this interval is crucial.
The definition supports the rational proof.
4
Apply the ruling to a similar case
Consider: what if the seller claimed the first twin (the one sold) instead? Does the same logic apply? The answer is yes, because the rational necessity works both ways.
The ruling would be symmetric.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو باع أحد توأمين ولدا عنده وأعتقه مشتريه، ثم ادعى البائع الآخر ثبت نسبهما منه، وبطل عتق المشتري؛ لأن من ضرورة ثبوت نسب أحدهما ثبوت الآخر، والتوأمان: ولدان بين ولادتهما أقل من ستة أشهر. [٤] قوله : والتوأم؛ التوأم اسم لولد يكون معه آخر في بطن واحد، لا يقال: توأم إلا لأحدهما، وهو فوعـل، والأنثى توأمه وزان جوهر وجوهرة، والولدان توأمان، والجمع توأم وتوائم، وزان دخان، وأتأمت المرأة وزان أكرمت: وضعت اثنين من حمل واحد، فهي متئم بغير هاء. كذا في المصباح المنير
And if someone sells one of twins born at his place, and the buyer frees him, then the seller claims the other (twin), both twins' lineage is established from him (the seller), and the freeing by the buyer is void; because it is a necessary consequence of establishing the lineage of one of them that the other's lineage is established. And twins are two children between whose births is less than six months. [4] He said: 'al-taw'am' is a name for a child with whom there is another in the same womb; it is not called taw'am except for one of them; it is on the pattern of fu'ʿal; the female is taw'amah like jauhar and jauharah; the two are taw'amān; the plural is taw'am and tawā'im like dukhān; and ata'amat al-mar'ah (like akramat) means she gave birth to two from one pregnancy; she is muti'im without a tā'. This is from al-Misbāḥ al-Munīr.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school on a specific case of lineage claim involving twins. The ruling: If a person sells one of two twins (both born at his place), and the buyer frees that twin, then later the seller claims the other twin as his child, the lineage of both twins is established from the seller, and the freeing of the first twin by the buyer becomes void. The proof given is rational: it is a necessary consequence ('ضرورة') that proving the lineage of one twin entails proving the lineage of the other. The reason is that twins come from one conception (one water), and it is impossible for the second twin to be conceived separately after the first birth because there is no pregnancy between two births if they are less than six months apart; also, when a woman conceives, the mouth of her womb closes and does not open until childbirth. Therefore, both twins must share the same father. The definition of twins is also given: two children born less than six months apart from the same pregnancy. This definition is crucial for the application of the ruling.

Key Terms
التوأم (al-taw'am): A child born from a pregnancy that also produces another child, with less than six months between births.
ضرورة (darura): Necessity; here, the logical necessity that one ruling entails another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for establishing that both twins share the same father?
A) The seller's oath
B) The buyer's admission
C) The necessity of proving one twin's lineage entails the other's
D) The court's discretion

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that proving one twin's lineage necessarily proves the other's lineage due to the impossibility of separate conception within six months.

Paternity Claim Overridden by Prior Acknowledgment
Ruling on invalid paternity claim after prior statement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that a claim of paternity is invalid when it contradicts a prior statement attributing the child to another person.

Core Idea
A later paternity claim is void if it contradicts a prior statement assigning the child to someone else.

In Hanafi family law, consistency in paternity claims is crucial. Once a person has stated that a boy is another man's son, he cannot later claim the boy as his own without strong evidence of error. This principle upholds the rights of the previously named father and prevents arbitrary disruption of lineage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Note the scenario: a person says of a boy with him, 'He is Zayd's son,' then later says, 'He is my son.' The ruling is that the second claim does not establish paternity.
The act is the second paternity claim; its hukm is that it is invalid (bāṭil).
2
Understand the immediate invalidity
At the time of the second claim, it is invalid because the prior statement created a right for Zayd. The claimant cannot unilaterally revoke that right.
The 'right' here refers to the potential legal relationship between Zayd and the boy.
3
Analyze the future invalidity
Even later, the claim remains invalid. Zayd may affirm, deny, or remain silent; in all cases the second claim is not accepted by consensus unless new evidence proves the first statement was mistaken.
The consensus referred to is among Hanafi jurists.
4
Recognize the condition of possession
The phrase 'معه' (with him) does not require that the boy be in the claimant's physical possession; it simply indicates the boy's presence. The condition of possession mentioned in some texts was coincidental.
Al-Zayla‘ī and others clarified this to avoid misapplication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال لصبي معه : هو ابن زيد، ثم قال : هو ابني، لم يكن ابنه [٣] قوله : معه ; ولا يشترط لهذا الحكم أن يكون الصبي في يده، واشتراطه في «الكتاب» وقع اتفاقاً. صرَّحَ به الزَّيْلَعِيُّ (۳) ، وغيره. [٤] قوله : لم يكن ابنه ; لا حالاً ولا استقبالاً ، أما حالاً فظاهر ; لوجود المانع هو تعلق حق مثلاً الغير وأما استقبالاً ; فلان زيد إما أن يصدّقه أو يكذبه، أو يسكت عن التصديق والتكذيب، ففي الوجه الأول والثالث : لا تصح دعوته بالاتفاق ; لأنه لم
And if he says about a boy with him: 'He is Zayd's son,' then says: 'He is my son,' he is not his son. [3] He said: 'معه' (with him): it is not a condition for this ruling that the boy be in his possession; its condition in the Book (Al-Kitab) coincidentally occurred. Al-Zayla‘ī and others explicitly stated this. [4] He said: 'لم يكن ابنه' (he is not his son): neither currently nor in the future. As for currently, it is obvious because of the existence of a preventative, which is that a right of another, e.g., is attached. As for the future, because Zayd will either affirm him or deny him or remain silent about affirmation and denial. In the first and third cases, his claim is not valid by consensus, because he did not...
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on claims of lineage. The ruling: If a person says of a boy who is with him, 'He is Zayd's son,' and then later says, 'He is my son,' his second claim is not accepted, and the boy is not established as his son. The commentary clarifies two points. First, the word 'معه' (with him) does not require that the boy be physically in his possession; it is sufficient that the boy is in his presence or custody. This condition mentioned in the original text was coincidental. Second, the statement 'لم يكن ابنه' means the boy is not his son either at the time of the claim or in the future. At present, the claim is blocked because the earlier statement (that he is Zayd's son) created a right for Zayd or others. In the future, the claim is invalid because Zayd may affirm or deny the relationship, or remain silent; in either case, the later claimant's assertion does not override the prior acknowledgment unless supported by evidence. The Hanafi school emphasizes that a prior declaration of another's paternity cannot be simply retracted; it requires demonstration of error or new evidence.

Key Terms
دعوى النسب (da'wā al-nasab): A legal claim of paternity or lineage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the boy is not in his possession.
B) Because the first statement created a right for Zayd, and Zayd's potential affirmation or silence prevents the claim.
C) Because the claimant is known to be unreliable.
D) Because the boy is already married.

Key Takeaway
A prior acknowledgment of another's paternity cannot be overridden by a later contradictory claim unless proven erroneous.

Zaid's Denial of Paternity: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on effect of denial of paternity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the effect of denial of paternity and explain the qiyas used to support the rebuttability of lineage acknowledgment.

Core Idea
Lineage acknowledgment can be undone by denial because it is like other revocable acts, not absolute.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about what happens when a man who initially acknowledged a child later denies paternity. Abu Hanifa and his two leading students disagree on whether the denial transfers the child's lineage to the current custodian. The dispute hinges on whether acknowledgment of lineage is permanent or rebuttable, and qiyas is used to argue that it is rebuttable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disagreement
State the exact difference: Abu Hanifa holds that Zaid's denial does not make the child a son of the current holder; Sahibayn hold it does.
The base text says: 'وعند أبي حنيفة له، وعندهما: إن جحد زيد بنوته يصير ابناً للذي في يده الصبي'.
2
Examine the proof structure
The text uses qiyas to show that acknowledgment of lineage can be rebutted, like other revocable acknowledgments (e.g., under compulsion).
The illa is that both matters are not absolutely permanent.
3
Analyze the asl (original case)
In compulsion for divorce/emancipation, acknowledgment under duress does not take effect because it is revocable.
Text: 'فإنَّ من أكره على الطلاق والعتاق ففعل يقع الطلاق والعتاق، ولو أكره على الإقرار بهما فأقرّ لا يقع'.
4
Apply the illa
The common effective cause is that both involve matters that can be annulled, hence lineage acknowledgment is also rebuttable.
The illa is derived from the effect of compulsion.
5
Work through examples
The text gives examples like buyer/seller freedom acknowledgment and li'an to illustrate the principle.
E.g., if a buyer acknowledges on behalf of the seller freeing a slave, then the seller denies, the acknowledgment is rebutted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى باب دعوى النسب
وإن جَحَد زيد بنوته
۲۲۳
وإن جَحَدَ زيد بنوته ) : هذا عند أبي حنيفة له ، وعندهما : إن جَحَدَ زِيدٌ بنوتَهُ يصير ابناً للذي في يدِهِ الصَّبيَّ؛ لأن الإقرار في النسب" يرتدُ بالرَّد
يتصل بإقراره تكذيب من جهة المقرّ له فبقي ،إقراره، وفي الوجه الثاني لم تصح دعوته عند الإمام، خلافاً لهما لما صرح به الشارح .
[١] قوله : وإن جحد زيد بنوته ؛ كلمة : إنّ وصليّة ؛ أي لا يثبت نسب ذلك الصبي إن لم يجحد زيد، وإن جحد فلا يثبت أيضاً ، وفائدة إن الوصلية أن يكون نقيضه أولى بالحكم من المذكور، والمذكور صورة الجحود، ونقيضه صورة عدم الجحود، فكان صورة عدم الجحود أولى؛ لعدم ثبوت النسب، ففيه الاتفاق، وفي المذكور خلاف كما
عرفت.
(٢) قوله : لأن الإقرار في النسب.... الخ ؛ تفصيلُه : إن إقراره بطل مجحود المقر له، فصار كأن لم يقرّ ؛ ولهذا أعتق عليه بدعوته لو كان عبداً له، والسر فيه : أن الإقرار بالنسب مما يرتدّ بالردّ ، إذ الإقرار بما لا يحتمل النقض يلحق بالإقرار بما يحتمل النقض ؛ ولهذا يعمل فيه الإكراه والهزل.
فإنَّ من أكره على الطلاق والعتاق ففعل يقعُ الطلاق والعتاق، ولو أكره على الإقرار بهما فأقرّ لا يقع، كما لو أكره على البيع وغيره مما يحتمل النقض ففعل لا يثبت ، فإذا ثبت بأن الإقرار بما لا يحتمل النقض ثبت أنه يرتد بالرد.
فصار كما إذا أقر المشتري على البائع بإعتاق من اشتراه ، فكذبه البائع، ثم قال : أنا أعتقته، يتحول إليه الولاء، ويرتد الإقرار به، والمقرّ له فكذا بهذا ؛ ولأنّ إقراره له بالنسب نفي، كنسب عن نفسه، وإنكار لوجوب الحقوق عليه، وذلك لا يمنع الإقرار به بعده ، بأن قال : ليس هو ابني، ثم قال : هو ابني، فكذا هذا.
ألا ترى أنه يصح إكذاب الملاعنة . عن : نفسه بعد نفي النسب، بخلاف ما إذا صدق العبد المولى، حيث لا يصح فيه دعوى المولى؛ لأنَّ المقر يدعي بعد تصديق المقر له إياه نسباً ثابتاً من الغير، وبخلاف ما إذا لم يصدقه ولم يكذبه، حيث لا يصح فيه دعوة المولى أيضاً ؛ لأنه تعلق به حق المقرّ له على اعتبار تصديقه فيصير كولد الملاعنة لا يثبت نسبه من غير الملاعن لتعلق حقه به بتكذيب نفسه.
Book of Claims, Chapter of Claim of Lineage. And if Zaid denies his paternity (this is according to Abu Hanifa it is [valid?], and according to the two companions: if Zaid denies his paternity, the child becomes a son for the one in whose custody the child is; because acknowledgment of lineage is rebutted by rejection. It is connected to his acknowledgment by a denial from the acknowledged party, so his acknowledgment remains. In the second aspect, his claim is not valid according to the Imam, contrary to the two companions as the commentator clarified. [1] He said: 'And if Zaid denies his paternity'; the word 'in' is a conjunctive; i.e., the lineage of that child is not established if Zaid does not deny, and if he denies it is also not established. The benefit of the conjunctive 'in' is that its opposite is more deserving of the ruling than the mentioned. The mentioned is the case of denial, and its opposite is the case of no denial, so the case of no denial is more deserving due to non-establishment of lineage, and there is agreement on that, while in the mentioned case there is disagreement as you have learned. (2) He said: 'Because acknowledgment of lineage...' etc.; its detail: His acknowledgment is nullified by the denial of the acknowledged party, so it is as if he did not acknowledge; for this reason, if the child was his slave, he would be free due to his claim. The secret is that acknowledgment of lineage can be rebutted by denial, because acknowledgment of something that cannot be annulled is attached to acknowledgment of something that can be annulled; for this reason, compulsion and jest affect it. For if someone is compelled to divorce or emancipate and does so, the divorce and emancipation occur, but if he is compelled to acknowledge them and acknowledges, they do not occur, just as if he is compelled to a sale or other revocable act and does it, it is not valid. So if it is established that acknowledgment of what cannot be annulled actually can be rebutted by denial, then it is as if a buyer acknowledges on behalf of the seller freeing the purchased person, then the seller denies, then the buyer says: 'I freed him,' the patronage transfers to him, and the acknowledgment is rebutted. Similarly here. And because his acknowledgment of lineage is a negation of lineage from himself, and a denial of the obligation of rights upon him, that does not prevent his acknowledgment afterwards, as if he says: 'He is not my son,' then says: 'He is my son,' so likewise this. Do you not see that it is valid for the one who commits li'an to deny himself after negating lineage, unlike when the slave confirms the master, where the master's claim is not valid; because the acknowledger claims after the acknowledged party's confirmation a lineage established from another. And unlike when he neither confirms nor denies, where the master's claim is also not valid; because the right of the acknowledged party is attached based on his confirmation, so it becomes like the child of li'an, whose lineage is not established from anyone other than the one who made li'an, due to attachment of his right by his self-denial.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of Zaid's denial of paternity of a child in his custody. The base text states that according to Abu Hanifa, the denial does not negate the prior acknowledgment? Actually the text is ambiguous: 'هذا عند أبي حنيفة له' may mean that Abu Hanifa holds that the denial is valid, but the context suggests that the two companions (Sahibayn) hold that if Zaid denies, the child becomes a son of the one in whose hands the child is (i.e., the denial is effective). The commentator explains that the word 'in' is a conjunctive, emphasizing that the opposite case (no denial) is more deserving of non-establishment of lineage, but in the mentioned case of denial, there is disagreement. The detailed argument follows: acknowledgment of lineage can be rebutted by denial, analogizing it to cases like compulsion in divorce/emancipation, where acknowledgment under duress does not take effect because it can be annulled. The commentary uses qiyas to show that if lineage acknowledgment were not rebuttable, it would be like acknowledgment of something that cannot be annulled, but since compulsion affects it, it is rebuttable. Examples are given: if a buyer acknowledges on behalf of the seller freeing the purchased person, and the seller denies, then the buyer's subsequent claim that he himself freed him transfers the patronage. Similarly, if one first says 'he is not my son' then says 'he is my son', the second statement is valid because the first was a negation. This is contrasted with the case of li'an, where the husband's self-denial is effective, and with the case where the slave confirms the master, where the master's later claim is invalid. The passage reveals the subtle mechanism: the right of the acknowledged party (the child) attaches, and once attached, it is not easily undone by the denier's claim.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment; a legal declaration that creates rights or obligations.
نسب (nasab): Lineage; the legal relationship of paternity.
رد (radd): Rebutal; denial by the acknowledged party that nullifies the acknowledgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), what is the effect of Zaid's denial of paternity of a child in his custody?
A) The child remains status unchanged
B) The child becomes a son of the one in whose hands the child is
C) The child is declared free
D) The child is deemed a foundling

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school is divided on whether a paternity acknowledgment can be undone by denial; the qiyas on revocable acts supports Abu Hanifa's view that it can be rebutted.

Child Claimed by Muslim as Slave and by Non-Muslim as Son
Ruling on a child with conflicting claims of slavery and lineage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling and explain the reasoning for a child claimed as slave by a Muslim and as son by a non-Muslim.

Core Idea
Freedom and lineage prevail: the child is free and the son of the non-Muslim.

When a Muslim claims a child as his slave and a non-Muslim claims him as his son, which claim takes precedence? The Hanafi school rules that the child is free and belongs to the non-Muslim’s lineage. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning, including the principle that freedom is the default and that an acknowledgment of lineage is binding on the acknowledger.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting claims
Note the two parties: a Muslim claims the child as his slave; a non-Muslim claims the child as his son. The child is in their joint custody.
2
State the immediate ruling
The child is free and is considered the son of the non-Muslim. This is the core ruling.
The Arabic phrase: 'فهو حر ابن للكافر'
3
Analyze the reasoning: freedom and Islam
The child attains freedom immediately (because freedom is the default) and will eventually become Muslim (since he is with a non-Muslim but Islamic evidence is apparent).
The text mentions 'دلائل الوحدانية ظاهرة' (evidence of monotheism is apparent).
4
Examine the analogy of acknowledgment
The commentator draws an analogy: just as acknowledging the freedom of another's slave is binding on the acknowledger (even if the master denies), so too an acknowledgment of lineage is binding and not rebutted by denial.
The analogy uses qiyas: asl = acknowledgment of freedom; far' = acknowledgment of lineage.
5
Distinguish lineage from patronage (walā')
Lineage is more binding than patronage; patronage can transfer (e.g., upon apostasy and recapture), but lineage cannot be transferred or annulled once established.
This refutes any objection that lineage might be treated like patronage.

Source Text

And if there is [a child] with a Muslim and a non-Muslim, and the Muslim says: He is my slave, and the non-Muslim says: He is my son, then he is free, son of the non-Muslim; because he attains freedom immediately, and Islam in the future, since evidence of monotheism is apparent. In the opposite case, Islam is established by following him, and he is prevented from freedom, and it is not in his capacity to acquire it.
[1] His statement: 'And he has that lineage does not admit annulment...' etc.; its clarification: After establishment, lineage does not admit annulment by agreement, and acknowledgment of what does not admit annulment is not rebutted by denial, so it remains valid with respect to himself; because his acknowledgment is a proof against himself, like one who acknowledges the freedom of another's slave and the master denies, it remains valid against the acknowledger, so it is not rebutted by his acknowledgment, such that if he later owns him, he is free due to his acknowledgment.
·
And like one who testifies concerning a person's lineage of a minor and his testimony is rejected due to sin or kinship or the like, then he claims him for himself, his claim is not valid due to his acknowledgment of him for another; because the right of the acknowledged party is attached, so if he confirms him after the denial, his lineage is established from him. Similarly, if the right of the child is attached, it is not rebutted by the denial of the acknowledged party, so it becomes like his claim before the rebuttal. And because his acknowledgment entails two things: first, establishment of lineage from another; second, nullification of his own right to claim. If the first is rebutted due to lack of authority over him, the second is not rebutted; because his acknowledgment is a proof against him, and the case of patronage does not apply. And if we concede that that case is by agreement, we say: lineage is more binding than patronage, for it can transfer from the mother's side to the father's side upon the father's emancipation, and similarly if a freed slave woman apostatizes and joins the enemy territory, is captured, and then freed by a second master, the patronage belongs to him, but lineage does not accept such transfer, so analogy is not valid.
[2] His statement: 'Because he attains...' etc.; its explanation: Islam always prevails, and prevailing necessitates
(1) See: Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, 4:334.
