Commentary
This passage gives the ruling on a child in the custody of both a Muslim and a non-Muslim, with each making conflicting claims: the Muslim claims the child as his slave, the non-Muslim as his son. The ruling is that the child is free and is considered the son of the non-Muslim. The reason is that he attains freedom immediately, and he will eventually become Muslim (since he is with a non-Muslim but Islamic evidence is apparent). The opposite scenario is also considered. Then the commentator elaborates on the nature of lineage, emphasizing that once established, lineage cannot be annulled, and an acknowledgment of lineage is not rebutted by denial. He provides various examples to illustrate: if someone acknowledges the freedom of another's slave, and the master denies, the acknowledgment remains binding on the acknowledger; if someone testifies to a minor's lineage and his testimony is rejected, he cannot later claim the child for himself because his prior acknowledgment attached a right of the child to another. The difference between lineage and patronage is highlighted: lineage is more binding and does not transfer like patronage. The commentary ends with a statement that Islam always prevails.

Key Terms
حرية (hurriyya): Freedom; the legal status of being free rather than slave.
إسلام (islām): Submission to Allah; the religion of the child in the case is determined by following the non-Muslim father? Actually the child is eventually Muslim.
ولاء (walā'): Patronage; the legal relationship between a freed slave and his emancipator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He is a slave of the Muslim
B) He is free and the son of the non-Muslim
C) He is a foundling and free
D) He is the son of both

Key Takeaway
When freedom and lineage are at stake, the Hanafi school gives priority to default freedom and the binding nature of lineage acknowledgment over a claim of slavery.

Ruling on a Child Claimed as Slave by Muslim and as Son by Non-Muslim
Ruling with proof on conflicting claims of slavery and paternity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a Muslim claims a child as a slave and a non-Muslim claims him as a son, and explain the reasoning based on prioritization of freedom over immediate Islam.

Core Idea
When a Muslim claims ownership and a non-Muslim claims paternity, the child is free and a son of the non-Muslim — freedom cannot be acquired later, but Islam can.

This lesson examines a nuanced case from the Hanafi school where two competing claims — one of slavery, one of paternity — are made over a child. The ruling prioritizes the child's immediate freedom over immediate religious affiliation. We will explore the legal reasoning, including the irreversibility of lineage and the principle of considering the child's best interest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting claims
A Muslim claims the child as his slave; a non-Muslim claims the child as his son.
The claims are of different natures: ownership vs. paternity.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The child is declared free and is the son of the non-Muslim father.
3
Understand the primary reasoning
Freedom is obtained immediately and cannot be regained if lost, while Islam can be embraced later through manifest proofs of monotheism.
The child's best interest (maslahah) demands prioritizing freedom.
4
Analyze the objection
Sheikh al-Babarti objected citing Quran 2:221 that a believing slave is better than a polytheist.
This would suggest the child should be a Muslim slave.
5
Examine the Hanafi response
Quran 33:5 commands calling children to their fathers; lineage once established cannot be revoked. The immediate harm of enslavement outweighs the potential risk of disbelief.
The two verses are reconciled by prioritizing lineage and avoiding irreparable harm.

And if there is a child with a Muslim and a disbeliever, and the Muslim says: 'He is my slave,' and the disbeliever says: 'He is my son,' then he is free, a son of the disbeliever. And it is said: Indeed lineage (nasab) does not admit of revocation, and an acknowledgment of something like that is not rejected by a denial. 1. And if a child is with a Muslim and a disbeliever, and the Muslim says: 'He is my slave,' and the disbeliever says: 'He is my son,' then he is free, a son of the disbeliever—because he obtains freedom immediately, and Islam later, since the proofs of monotheism are manifest. In the opposite case (if we made him a slave of the Muslim), he would become a Muslim by following him but would be deprived of freedom, which he cannot acquire by himself. [1] His saying: 'And it is said: lineage... etc.' Clarification: Verily lineage does not admit of revocation after it is established, and this is by consensus. And acknowledgment of what does not admit of revocation is not rejected by denial; it remains effective with respect to the person himself, because his acknowledgment is a proof against himself, like one who acknowledges the freedom of another's slave and the master denies it—it remains effective against the acknowledger, so it is not nullified by his acknowledgment to the extent that if he becomes the owner one day, the slave becomes free due to his acknowledgment. Similarly, one who testifies for a man regarding a child's lineage, but his testimony is rejected due to sin or kinship or the like, then he claims the child for himself—his claim is invalid because of his acknowledgment of the child belonging to another; because the right of the acknowledger is attached to it, so if he affirms him after the denial, his lineage from him is established. Likewise, if the child's right is attached to it, it is not nullified by the denial of the one in whose favor it was acknowledged; it becomes like his claim before the denial. And because his acknowledgment entails two things: first, establishing lineage from another, and second, nullifying his own right in the claim. If the first is nullified due to his lack of authority over him, the second is not nullified, because his acknowledgment is a proof against him. The case of wala' does not apply here, because it is subject to disagreement and thus not a proof. For if the buyer acknowledges that the seller manumitted the sold slave and the seller denies it, then the buyer says: 'I manumitted him,' the wala' does not transfer to the buyer according to Imam Abu Hanifa. Even if we accept that the case of wala' is by consensus, we say: lineage is more binding than wala', because wala' can transfer from the mother's side to the father's side when the father manumits, and also if a manumitted female slave apostatizes, joins the abode of war, is captured, and is manumitted by a second master, the wala' belongs to him, but lineage does not accept transfer, so analogy upon it is invalid. [2] His saying: 'Because he obtains... etc.' Its explanation: Verily Islam gives preference forever, and preference requires conflict, but there is no conflict here, because conflict only occurs when there is equality, and there is no equality here; because consideration for the child is obligatory, and his benefit in being the son of the Christian and free is greater, because he obtains the honor of freedom immediately and the honor of Islam later, since the proofs of monotheism are manifest, so he can acquire the honor of Islam by himself. If we made him a slave of the Muslim, we would make him a Muslim as a follower of him, but deprive him of freedom, because he cannot acquire freedom by himself. This is unlike when each of the two claims him as his son; then the Muslim is more entitled, because they are equal in the claim of sonship, so the Muslim is preferred due to Islam, and this is better for the child to obtain Islam immediately as a follower of his father. (2) Sheikh al-Akmal al-Babarti objected that it contradicts His statement: 'And a believing slave is better than a polytheist' (2:221) and the proofs of monotheism, though manifest, yet familiarity with disbelievers is a strong deterrent—do you not see that fathers disbelieved despite the clear proofs of monotheism? And it is supported by the fact that an absolutely divorced woman is more entitled to her Muslim child as long as he has not discerned religions, or it is feared he might become accustomed to disbelief, and the possibility of harm after that. (3) It was answered: His statement: 'Call them by their fathers' (33:5) necessitates calling children to their fathers, and the claimant of lineage is a father, because his claim does not admit of revocation, so the two verses conflict. The disbelief of fathers is denial, and the principle is its absence. Do you not see the spread of Islam after disbelief in all regions? As for custody, its abandonment does not entail slavery, unlike abandoning lineage here; because the consequence is slavery, which is a great harm inevitably. And this is a favor, he is a benefactor, and the benefactor is not subject to blame. And it is unlike marriage, because it is structured for procreation and seeking offspring, as he said: 'Marry, beget, multiply... the hadith.'
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the commentary on Al-Hidayah, addressing the case where a child is claimed simultaneously as a slave by a Muslim and as a son by a non-Muslim. The ruling is that the child is free and is the son of the non-Muslim. The reasoning is multifaceted: first, the principle that lineage (nasab) once established cannot be repudiated, and an acknowledgment of such lineage is not displaced by a disavowal. Second, the child's best interests require immediate freedom (hurriyah) even if it follows the non-Muslim father in religion, because freedom cannot be acquired later, whereas Islam can be acquired independently through the manifest proofs of monotheism. The author then explains that if the Muslim were to claim the child as his son, he would be preferred due to his Islam, but here the claims are of different natures (mastery vs. paternity). Sheikh al-Babarti objects that making the child a son of a non-Muslim contradicts the Quranic verse 'A believing slave is better than a polytheist' (2:221), and that familiarity with disbelievers is harmful. The response is that the verse 'Call them by their fathers' (33:5) requires attributing the child to his biological father, and that lineage, once determined, cannot be undone. The danger of disbelief is outweighed by the immediate harm of enslavement, and the child can later embrace Islam. This illustrates the Hanafi prioritization of freedom over immediate religious affiliation in such conflicting claims.

Key Terms
نَسَب (nasab): Lineage; the biological relationship of a child to his father, which once established is irrevocable.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment; a statement by a person that confirms a right against himself, which is binding on him.
حُرِّيَّة (hurriyah): Freedom; the legal status of being a free person rather than a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a Muslim claims a child as his slave and a non-Muslim claims him as his son, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The child is a slave of the Muslim and becomes Muslim.
B) The child is free and is the son of the non-Muslim.
C) The child is free and becomes a Muslim under the Muslim's care.
D) The child is divided between them, being a slave for part of the year.

Key Takeaway
In a conflict between a Muslim's claim of slavery and a non-Muslim's claim of paternity, the Hanafi school prioritizes the child's immediate freedom over immediate Islamic affiliation, because freedom cannot be later acquired, while Islam can.

When Both Spouses Claim a Child: Hanafi Ruling
Ruling on lineage when both spouses claim a child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when both husband and wife claim a child's lineage and explain the qiyas to a shared garment.

Core Idea
When both spouses claim a child, he is deemed both their child, analogous to a shared garment.

In this passage, we encounter a unique scenario in family law: a husband and wife both claim that a boy in their mutual custody is their own child, but from a prior relationship. The Hanafi school resolves the conflict by ruling that the child belongs to both. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind that rule, including the use of analogy (qiyas) and the important limitations of that analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Dispute
Read the scenario: a husband and wife have a boy with them. The husband says 'He is my son from another woman' and the wife says 'He is my son from another man.' Both claim the child but attribute him to a third party (not to each other).
The key is that each parent claims the child but excludes the other parent from the lineage.
2
Extract the Ruling
The passage states 'فهو ابنهما' – so he is the son of both. The child's lineage is established to both the husband and the wife.
3
Trace the Qiyas
The ruling is based on qiyas (analogy) to a case of a garment in the hands of two men, each claiming it belongs to him and a third party. In that case, the garment is considered shared. Similarly, the boy is considered shared between the couple.
The analogy uses possession (yad) and the fact that each claim excludes the other.
4
Note the Objection and Response
An objection arises: lineage cannot be split like property. The Hanafi response acknowledges the difference but maintains the ruling based on the strength of possession and marital context.
The difference is noted but does not change the ruling because the underlying rationale (zahir al-hal) supports both parents.
5
Consider Sub-Scenarios
The passage also discusses variations: if the child can speak, his testimony is accepted; if the child is in one parent's sole custody, that parent's claim prevails; if witnesses are brought, the ruling may adjust.
These sub-rules refine the application of the main ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال زوج امرأة لصبي معهما : هو ابني من غيرها، وقالت : هو ابني من غيره فإذا لم يسلم ما هو المقصود به رجعَ بذلك على من غرّه ، والمقصود بوضع الهبة إظهار الجود والسماحة وثبوت الملك، وهذا المقصود يتحقق بدون الاستيلاد. كذا في «المنح»، و«التبيين»، وغيرهما. قوله : لصبي ؛ أراد صبياً لا يعبر عن نفسه، فأما إذا عبر فالقول له أيهما صدقه يثبت نسبه منه بتصديقه . كذا في «العناية»، و«السراج الوهاج». قوله : معهما ؛ قال الشامي في حاشيته على الدر المختار : وفي أيديهما ؛ احتراز عما إذا كان في يد أحدهما ، قال في «التاتارخانية»: وإن كان الولد في يد الزوج أو يد المرأة فالقولُ للزوج فيهما. وقيد بإسنادِ كل منهما الولد إلى غير صاحبه أيضاً لما فيها أيضاً عن «المنتقى»: صبي في يد رجل وامرأة، قالت المرأة : هذا ابني من هذا الرجل ، وقال : ابني من غيرها، يكون ابنُ الرجل ولا يكون للمرأة، فإن جاءت بامرأة شهدت على ولادتها إياه كان ابنها منه ، وكانت زوجته بهذه الشهادة ، وإن كان في يدِهِ وادعاه وادعت امرأتُهُ أنه ابنها منه ، وشهدتِ المرأةُ على الولادة لا يكون ابنها منه بل ابنه ؛ لأنه في يده. واحترز عما فيها أيضاً : صبي في يد رجلٌ لا يدعيه، أقامت المرأة أنه ابنها ولدته ولم تسم أباه، وأقام رجلٌ أنه ولد في فراشه ولم يسم أمه يجعل ابنه من هذه المرأة، ولا يعتبر الترجيح باليد كما لو ادعاه رجلا، وهو في يد أحدهما، فإنه يقضى لذي اليد. . انتهى. فهو ابنهما.
And if a husband and wife have a boy with them, and he says: 'He is my son from another woman,' and she says: 'He is my son from another man,' then if the intended purpose is not achieved, he (the one who misled) is liable. The purpose of hibah is to show generosity and establish ownership, which is achieved without istilad. ... The commentator says: 'with them' means in their hands, to exclude when he is in one's hand. And it is restricted to both attributing the child to someone else. From al-Muntaqa: A boy in the hands of a man and a woman; if she says he is her son from this man, and he says he is his son from another woman, the boy is the man's son and not hers. If she brings a woman who testifies to his birth, he becomes her son and the man becomes her husband by that testimony. If the boy is in his hand and he claims him, and his wife claims he is her son from him, and a woman testifies to the birth, he is not her son but his, because he is in his hand. Also, if a boy is in a man's hand and he does not claim him, and a woman claims he is her son without naming the father, and a man claims he was born on his bed without naming the mother, the boy is made the son of that woman and no preference is given by possession, as if two men claimed him and he is in one's hand, then possession decides. End. So he is the son of both.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where a husband and wife both have a boy in their custody, and each claims the boy as their own child but attributes him to someone else (the husband says the boy is his son from another woman, and the wife says the boy is her son from another man). The ruling is that the boy is considered the son of both of them. The reasoning is based on the apparent situation: since both have physical custody (hand) and they are married, the boy is presumed to be from their marital bed (firash). Each one's claim to exclude the other is not accepted because it would negate the other's right. The passage analogizes this to a garment in the hands of two men, where each claims it belongs to him and a third party; the garment is considered shared. However, a difference is noted: in the case of the garment, the person admitted (muqarr lahu) enters into the share of the admitter because the object can be shared, but lineage cannot be shared. The passage also details various sub-scenarios: if the boy can speak, his statement is accepted; if the boy is in the hand of only one, that one's claim prevails; if the wife brings a witness to birth, the boy becomes hers, etc.

Key Terms
دعوى النسب (da'wa al-nasab): A legal claim regarding lineage (nasab) of a child.
اليد (al-yad): Physical possession or custody of a child or object.
الفراش (al-firash): The marital bed, indicating a legal presumption that a child born to a married couple is from the husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a husband and wife both claim a boy in their custody, each attributing him to a different person (the husband says the boy is his son from another woman, the wife says the boy is her son from another man), what is the Hanafi ruling regarding the boy's lineage?
A) The boy is the son of the husband only.
B) The boy is the son of the wife only.
C) The boy is the son of both the husband and the wife.
D) The boy's lineage is undetermined until a witness testifies.

Key Takeaway
When both spouses claim a child attributing him to a third party, the Hanafi school rules the child is the son of both based on mutual possession and marital context.

Definition and Proof of Iqrar (Confession) in Islamic Law
Definition and proof of iqrar from linguistic to legal with dalil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define iqrar linguistically and legally, state its ruling (binding), and identify the proofs (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, reason) that establish its validity.

Core Idea
Iqrar is a binding confession against oneself, proven by Quran, Sunnah, consensus, and reason, with a preponderance of truthfulness.

We now begin the Book of Iqrar, the legal institution of confession. The author places it after the Book of Sulh (settlement) because confession naturally precedes settlement: a person admits a right and then settles it. This passage defines iqrar both linguistically and legally, establishes its binding nature, and marshals evidence from the Quran, the Prophet's practice, consensus, and rational argument. Understanding this foundation is essential for all subsequent rulings on confession in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Trace the linguistic meaning of iqrar
Identify the root q-r-r meaning 'to become established' and the two verbal forms: aqarrahu (sensory establishment) and aqarra bihi (verbal acknowledgment). Understand that iqrar etymologically means 'making something firm after it was wavering between denial and confirmation.'
The text draws from al-Misbah and al-Tahtawi to clarify the shades of meaning.
2
Grasp the legal definition
Note the author's definition: 'ihbar bihaqq' — an assertion of a right. Iqrar is a statement that a right is due from the speaker to another. It is binding (mulzim) on the confessor because it indicates the existence of the confessed right.
The binding nature is based on the preponderance of truthfulness: people do not normally admit liabilities falsely, especially against their natural love for wealth.
3
Identify the proofs of validity
The Hanafi school cites four sources: Quran (2:282 commanding the debtor to dictate, implying confession; 4:135 commanding witness even against self), Sunnah (the Prophet enforced stoning based on Ma'iz's and al-Ghamidiyyah's confessions), consensus (Ijma), and rational argument (if hadd is established by confession, financial rights are more certainly established).
Each proof demonstrates that iqrar is a legitimate and sufficient means of establishing a legal right.
4
Understand the preponderance of truth (tarjih al-sidq)
Recognize the underlying principle: iqrar is considered truthful because human nature avoids false self-incrimination, especially when it involves financial loss. This principle gives iqrar its evidentiary weight.
This is a unique feature of Hanafi fiqh, distinguishing it from other schools that may require additional corroboration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

كتاب الإقرار

11) قوله : كتاب الإقرار؛ مناسبته السابقه ظاهرة، فإن المدعي إما أن يصير له المطلوب، أو يُنكره، واللائق بالمسلم الإقرار بالحق، كما أن اللائق بالمدعي أن تكون دعواه حقا، فقدمه على ما بعده، وهو الصلح.

والإقرار أفعال من قرّ الشيء : إذا ثبت وأقرّ غيره : إذا أثبته، قال في «المصباح»: «أقرّ الرجل إقراراً، أصابه بالقُرّ، فهو مقرور على غير قياس، وأقره بالشيء : اعترف به». انتهى
(1)

وثبوت جوازه بالكتاب والسنة والإجماع والمعقول : أما بالكتاب، فقوله : وَلْيُمْلِلِ الَّذِى عَلَيْهِ الْحَقُّ ) (۳)، قال في «المدارك»: لا يكن المملي إلا من وجب عليه الحق ؛ لأنه هو المشهود على ثباته في ذمته، وإقراره، فيكون ذلك إقرار على نفسه بلسانه، والإملالُ والإملاء نعتان» (انتهى) وقوله : كُونُوا قَوَّامِينَ بِالْقِسْطِ شُهَدَاءَ لِلَّهِ وَلَوْ عَلَى أَنفُسِكُمْ *

Book of Iqrar. The connection to the preceding is clear, for the claimant either gets what he claims or denies it, and it is befitting for a Muslim to confess to the truth, as it is befitting for the claimant for his claim to be true. So he [the author] placed it after what precedes, i.e., after [the book of] sulh (settlement). And iqrar is derived from 'qarra' meaning 'it became established' and 'aqarra ghayrahu' meaning 'he established it.' Al-Misbah says: 'A man said 'aqarra' iqrar having been struck by cold, he is maqrur, irregularly. And aqarra bi al-shay': acknowledged it.' End. And our teacher al-Tahtawi narrated from al-Hamawi: 'If it is sensory, one says aqarrahu; if verbal, one says aqarra bihi. So iqrar is the establishment of what was wavering between denial and confirmation.' End. And in Sacred Law: what the author defined by saying: 'It is an assertion of a right...' etc. And it is binding on the confessor and on what he confessed to, because it indicates the existence of the thing confessed to, since iqrar oscillates between truth and falsehood, and the side of truth is preponderant because a person does not lie, especially since wealth is naturally beloved to a person, and he does not say it to another falsely while being of complete intellect and piety. And its validity is established by the Book, the Sunnah, consensus, and reason. As for the Book: His saying (Quran 2:282): 'Let the one who owes the right dictate' – it is said in al-Madarik: 'The dictator must be the one upon whom the right is due because he is the one who is witnessed for the establishment of the right in his liability and his confession, so that is a confession against himself with his tongue, and imlal and imla' are attributes.' End. And His saying (Quran 4:135): 'Be maintainers of justice, witnesses for Allah, even if against yourselves' – and what is meant is iqrar as they mentioned. As for the Sunnah: The Messenger of Allah ﷺ imposed stoning on Ma'iz due to his confession, and on al-Ghamidiyyah due to her confession, as related by al-Bukhari and Muslim. So if the fixed punishment is established by a person's confession against himself, then wealth is more worthy of being established by it.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Iqrar in the commentary. It first explains the placement of the book after Sulh. Then it provides a linguistic definition of iqrar, noting its derivation from 'qarra' meaning to establish. It distinguishes sensory (aqarrahu) from verbal (aqarra bihi) usage. The legal definition follows that of the matn: an assertion of a right. The ruling is that iqrar is binding on the confessor. This is because iqrar is preponderantly truthful given human nature – people are reluctant to admit debts against themselves unless true. The author then provides the proofs: from the Quran, the verse commanding the debtor to dictate (2:282) implies his confession; and the verse commanding to bear witness even against oneself (4:135). From the Sunnah, the precedent of Ma'iz and al-Ghamidiyyah, where the Prophet enforced punishment based on confession. This shows that confession creates legal obligations. The argument from reason (a fortiori) is that if the more severe punishment (hadd) is established by confession, then lesser obligations like financial rights are even more so.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): A legal term meaning the admission or confession of a right by a person against himself, binding upon him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence from the Quran used to prove the validity of iqrar?
A) The verse on stoning
B) The verse 'Let the debtor dictate'
C) The verse on witnesses
D) The verse on justice

Key Takeaway
Iqrar is a valid and binding establishment of a right against oneself, supported by Quran, Sunnah, consensus, and reason, with a preponderance of truthfulness due to human nature.

The Minimum for 'Great Wealth' in Acknowledgement
Rulings on ambiguous acknowledgment of debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum amounts used to interpret 'great wealth' in ambiguous acknowledgements and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on the standard.

Core Idea
In ambiguous acknowledgements, 'great wealth' is measured by the nisab of zakah — but not all Hanafi authorities agree.

When someone acknowledges a debt with an ambiguous description like 'great wealth,' the qadi must interpret it. The Hanafi school provides clear thresholds based on the nisab of zakah, with variations from Abu Hanifah and later scholars. This lesson unpacks those thresholds and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general principle for 'mal' (wealth)
When someone says 'I owe mal (wealth),' he is not believed if he claims less than one dirham, because customarily 'mal' refers to at least a dirham.
This is a baseline: less than a dirham is not considered wealth in common usage.
2
Identify the standard for 'mal 'adhim' (great wealth)
When 'great wealth' is acknowledged, the description 'great' must be satisfied. The nisab of zakah is the shari'i measure of greatness.
For specific categories (gold, silver, camels), the nisab is fixed: 20 dinars, 200 dirhams, 25 camels.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Abu Hanifah has a second narration setting the minimum at 10 dirhams (nisab of theft). Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi argues it depends on the declarant's financial status.
The Sahibayn's view (nisab of zakah) is the relied-upon position, but the alternative views are also considered.
4
Apply the rule to non-zakat wealth
For items like wheat, donkeys, etc., 'great wealth' means the value of the nisab (e.g., value of 200 dirhams). A narration from Abu Hanifah sets it at 10 dirhams.
Analogous reasoning extends the nisab standard to all property.
5
Handle plurals and special expressions
If 'amwal (properties)' is used, minimum is three nisabs in value. Expressions like 'valuable' default to 200 dirhams. Diminutives like 'durayhim' imply full weight.
The qadi uses objective criteria to avoid subjective manipulation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإقرار/ تعريفه وأحكامه

ولا يُصَدِّقُ في أقل من درهم في علي ،مال ومن النصاب في : علي مال عظيم من الذهب ، أو من الفضة، ومن خمس وعشرين في الإبل ولا يُصدق في أقل من درهم في علي ،مال، ومن النصاب" في : علي مال عظيم من الذهب ، أو من الفضة، ومن خمس وعشرين في الإبل

وتمامه في «الجواهر» و«التحفة». انتهى. 11 ] قوله : ولا يصدق ... الخ ؛ يعني إذا قال له : علي مال لم يصدق في أقل من درهم ؛ لأنَّ ما دونه من الكسور لا يطلق عليه اسم المال عادة، وهو المعتبر. قال في «البحر»: ولو قال : لفلان علي دار أو عبد لا يلزمه بشيء، أو مال قليل، أو درهم عظيم، أو درهم لزمه درهم. انتهى. فظاهره أنه يلزمه درهم ولا يجبر على بيان.

[2]قوله : ومن النصاب ... الخ ؛ يعني إذا قال له : علي مال عظيم، وأجبره القاضي على البيان، فقال: عنيتُ أقل من النصاب، لم يصدق ؛ لأنه أقر بمال موصوف بالعظيم، فيعتبر هذا الوصف، والنصاب عظيمٌ في الشرع، حتى يصير صاحبه غنيّاً، وأوجب عليه مواساة الفقراء. وكذا عرفاً، حتى يعد من الأغنياء عادة، وهذا قولهما، وهو مروي عن الإمام، وعنه له أيضاً : إنّه لا يصدق في أقل من عشرة دراهم، وهي نصاب السرقة ؛ لأنه عظيم حيث يقطع به اليد المحترمة، ويستباح به البضع المحترم. قال في «النتائج»(۱) : قال شمس الأئمة السَّرَخْسِيُّ له : والأصح على قول أبي حنيفة أنه يبنى على حال المقرّ في الفقر والغناء، فإن القليل عند الفقير عظيم، وأضعاف ذلك عند الغني حقير. انتهى والله أعلم.

[3] قوله : من الذهب ... الخ ؛ فلا يصدق في أقل من عشرين ديناراً، إذا قال: من الدنانير، وفي أقل من مئتي درهم إذا قال : من الدراهم، وفي أقل من خمس وعشرين إذا قال : من الإبل، فإنَّ خمسا وعشرين أدنى نصاب منها يجب فيه من جنسه. قال البرْجَنْدِي : وكان القياس أن يقدر بخمس، فإنّه يجب فيها شاء، إلا أنه عظيم

Book of Acknowledgment: Its Definition and Rulings. He is not believed for less than a dirham in 'I owe money', and from the nisab in 'I owe great wealth' of gold or silver, and from twenty-five in camels. He is not believed for less than a dirham in 'I owe money', and from the nisab in 'I owe great wealth' of gold or silver, and from twenty-five in camels. Its completion is in al-Jawahir and al-Tuhfah. End. His saying: 'He is not believed... etc.' means when he says 'I owe money,' he is not believed for less than a dirham because less than that is not customarily called wealth, and that is the consideration. He said in al-Bahr: If he says 'I owe him a house or a slave,' nothing is binding; or 'a little wealth,' or 'a great dirham,' or 'a dirham,' he owes a dirham. End. Its apparent meaning is that he owes a dirham and is not forced to explain. [2] His saying: 'And from the nisab... etc.' means when he says 'I owe great wealth' and the judge forces him to explain, and he says 'I meant less than the nisab,' he is not believed because he acknowledged wealth described as great, so this description is considered, and the nisab is great in the Shari'ah to the extent that its owner becomes rich and is obligated to support the poor. Likewise by custom, such that he is counted among the rich usually. This is their [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's] saying, and it is narrated from the Imam [Abu Hanifah]; and from him also that he is not believed for less than ten dirhams, which is the nisab of theft, because it is great as the protected hand is cut off for it and the protected private part is rendered permissible. He said in al-Nata'ij: Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: The most correct according to Abu Hanifah's saying is that it depends on the situation of the acknowledger in poverty and wealth; for what is little for a poor person is great, and multiples of that for a rich person are insignificant. End. And Allah knows best. [3] His saying: 'Of gold... etc.' So he is not believed for less than twenty dinars if he says 'from dinars,' and for less than two hundred dirhams if he says 'from dirhams,' and for less than twenty-five if he says 'from camels,' because twenty-five is the lowest nisab of camels in which zakat of the same kind is due. Al-Burjandi said: The analogy would be to estimate it at five, because sheep are due for five camels, but it is not great in terms of zakat not being due from its own kind, so what was mentioned was considered to make it absolutely great. End. And from the value of the nisab for non-zakat wealth, and from three nisabs for great properties, and three dirhams. [1] And from the value of the nisab for non-zakat wealth, and from three nisabs for great properties, and three dirhams (3). Because zakat is due on it, but it is not great because zakat is not due from its own kind, so what was mentioned was considered to make it absolutely great. End. [1] His saying: 'And from the value of the nisab... etc.' means he is not believed for less than the value of the nisab for non-zakat wealth, such as wheat and other things. So if he says 'I owe great wealth of donkey or mule or wheat or other,' and the judge forces him to explain, and he says 'I meant less than the value of the nisab,' he is not believed because the value is also great. And it is narrated from the Imam that it is estimated at ten dirhams, as in al-Ikhtiyar. And he said in al-Kifayah: If he says 'I owe valuable, generous, dangerous, or sublime wealth,' al-Natifi said: I did not find it textual; al-Jurjani used to say: Two hundred are binding. And if he says 'I owe a little dirham or a little dinar,' he owes a complete dirham or a complete dinar; because the small [weight] might be mentioned with the diminutive for small size, so it does not reduce the weight. And if he says 'much wheat,' then it is five wasaq, and one wasaq is sixty sa', and this according to their saying because the nisab in the chapter of tithes is estimated by this. But according to Abu Hanifah's saying, there is no nisab for wheat, so it refers to the declarant's explanation. End. [2] His saying: 'And from three nisabs for great properties' means he is not believed for less than three nisabs for great properties. So whoever says 'I owe great properties' and the judge forces him to explain, and he says 'I meant less than three nisabs,' he is not believed because properties is a plural, the minimum of which is three, and the great is the nisab as we clarified, so he is not believed for less than that because of certainty. He said in al-Minh: It is appropriate by analogy to what was narrated from Abu Hanifah that the condition of the declarant is considered as we mentioned. End. [3] His saying: 'And three dirhams' means if he says 'I owe dirhams,' then three dirhams are binding.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage from the commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'aya on Sharh al-Wiqaya addresses the fiqh of iqrar (acknowledgment/confession) in the Hanafi school. The central issue is how to interpret ambiguous statements about debts. The text begins by stating the general principle: when someone says 'I owe money (mal),' he is not believed if he claims he meant less than one dirham. The reason is that customarily, the term 'mal' is not applied to fractional amounts below a dirham. This is supported by a quote from al-Bahr. Next, the passage discusses when the debt is described as 'great wealth (mal 'adhim).' In this case, if the judge forces specification and the person says he meant less than the nisab of zakah, he is not believed because the nisab is considered great both in shari'ah and custom. This is the position of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and it is narrated from Abu Hanifah as well. A second narration from Abu Hanifah sets the minimum at ten dirhams (nisab of theft). However, the text cites Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi from al-Nata'ij, who says the most correct view of Abu Hanifah is that it depends on the financial status of the declarant: what is little for a poor person is great, and what is great for a rich person is insignificant. This is a key intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The passage then specifies fixed minima when the wealth is explicitly from a particular category: for gold (20 dinars), silver (200 dirhams), and camels (25), because these are the respective nisabs. Al-Burjandi notes that the analogy would be to estimate camels at five (the minimum for giving a sheep), but that would not be considered 'great' in terms of zakah. For non-zakat wealth (like wheat, donkeys), the minimum is the value of the nisab (e.g., the price of 200 dirhams). A narration from Abu Hanifah sets it at ten dirhams. When the phrase is 'properties (amwal)' in the plural, meaning at least three, the minimum is three nisabs in value, because 'amwal' is a plural requiring at least three, and 'great' equals nisab. Finally, if someone says 'I owe dirhams' (in the plural), three dirhams are binding. The passage also discusses expressions like 'valuable,' 'generous,' etc., where al-Jurjani says 200 dirhams are binding, and diminutives like 'durayhim' imply full weight. For wheat, the nisab is five wasaq (300 sa'), according to the Sahibayn; but Abu Hanifah held that there is no nisab for wheat, so the declarant's explanation is accepted. Throughout, the commentary emphasizes that the rulings balance the literal meaning, customary usage (urf), and shari'i definitions. The mufta bihi position is generally the Sahibayn's view, but the variation based on the declarant's status is also considered.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment or confession of a right or debt, which is binding on the declarant if it meets legal requirements.
مال عظيم (mal 'adhim): Wealth described as 'great,' which in acknowledgment is interpreted to mean the nisab of zakah or its equivalent in value.
نصاب (nisab): The minimum amount of wealth that makes zakat obligatory (e.g., 20 dinars gold, 200 dirhams silver). In this context, it is the standard for 'great wealth.'
يصدق (yusaddaq): He is believed (the declarant). In fiqh, it means the judge accepts his clarification regarding his intention in the acknowledgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone says 'I owe great wealth (مال عظيم) from silver' and the judge forces him to clarify, and he says 'I meant less than 200 dirhams,' is he believed?
A) Yes, because he is the one acknowledging.
B) No, because 200 dirhams is the nisab, and great wealth must reach that level.
C) Yes, only if he is poor.
D) No, unless he provides witnesses.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, ambiguous acknowledgements of 'great wealth' are objectively interpreted using the nisab of zakah, but the school also preserves alternative views that consider the declarant's personal financial status.

Numerical Iqrar: The Hanafi Dispute on Endpoints
Intra-Hanafi dispute on inclusion of endpoints in numerical iqrar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions (Abu Hanifa, Sahibayn, Zufar) on numerical iqrar and explain the legal reasoning (ta‘līl) behind each.

Core Idea
In numerical iqrar, the inclusion of endpoints depends on necessity and the nature of boundaries.

When someone acknowledges a debt 'from one to ten dirhams,' how many is owed? The Hanafi school is divided: Abu Hanifa says nine, the two disciples say ten, and Zufar says eight. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each view, focusing on how the concepts of ghāyah (endpoint) and ḥadd (boundary) are applied to numbers versus physical objects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
The dispute is about the interpretation of the phrase 'from X to Y' in an iqrar (acknowledgment). Does it include the first and/or last number mentioned?
2
State the Three Positions
Abu Hanifa: 9 dirhams (first included, last excluded). Sahibayn: 10 dirhams (both included). Zufar: 8 dirhams (neither included).
3
Analyze Each Position's Proof
For each, trace the dalīl. Zufar uses qiyās with physical walls. Sahibayn argue the endpoint must exist. Abu Hanifa combines necessity for the first with qiyās for the last.
4
Identify the Thamarah al-Khilāf
The practical consequence is the amount owed: 8, 9, or 10 dirhams. This matters in court when enforcing the iqrar.
5
Consider Tarjīh
The text does not specify a muftā bihī, but later authorities often prefer Sahibayn or Abu Hanifa. Recognize the methodological divergence.

وفي من درهم إلى عشرة وما بين درهم إلى عشرة عليه تسعة، وفي له من داري ما بين هذا الحائط إلى هذا الحائط له ما بينهما [۱] (وفي من درهم إلى عشرة ، وما بين درهم إلى عشرة ، عليه تسعة)، هذا عند أبي حنيفة ؛ لأن الغاية الأولى تدخل ضرورة، والأخيرة لا تدخل ، وعندهما تدخل الغايتان، فتجب عشرة ، وعند زُفَر له لا يدخل شيء منهما، فيجب ثمانية [٢] (وفي له من داري ما بين هذا الحائط إلى هذا الحائط له ما بينهما) [١]قوله : وفي من درهم ؛ يعني لو قال : له علي من درهم إلى عشرة، أو قال: له علي ما بين درهم إلى عشرة تلزمه تسعة، وهذا عند الإمام، وعندهما: تلزمه العشرة كلها. وقال زفر : تلزمه ثمانية، وهو القياس؛ لأنه جعل الدرهم الأَوَّلَ والآخر حداً، والحد لا يدخل في المحدود، فلا تدخل الغايتان، فصار كما إذا قال لفلان: من هذا الحائط إلى هذا الحائط، أو ما بين هذين الحائطين، فإنَّ الحائطين لا يدخلان في الإقرار، فكذا هذا. ولهما : إنَّ الغاية لا بد أن تكون موجودة، إذ المعدوم لا يصلح أن يكون حداً للموجود، ووجودها بوجوبها، فتدخل الغايتان بخلاف ما ذكر من المحسوس ؛ لأنه موجود، فيصلح حداً فلا يدخلان فيه. وللإمام: إن الغاية لا تدخل في المغيا ؛ لأنَّ الحد يغاير المحدود، فهذا هو الأصل، كما قال زفر ، لكن هاهنا لا بُدَّ من إدخال الغاية الأولى ضرورة ؛ لأنَّ الدرهم الثاني والثالث لا يتحقق بدون الأوّل، فدخلت الغاية الأولى ضرورة. ولا ضرورة في إدخال الغاية الثانية ، فأخذنا فيها بالقياس، فلا يدخل ؛ لأنَّ العدد يقتضي ابتداء، فإذا أخرجنا الأول من أن يكون ابتداءً صار الثاني هو الأول، فيخرج هو أيضاً من أن يكون ابتداءً كالأول، وكذا الثالث والرابع، فيؤدي إلى خروج الكلِّ من أن يكون واجباً فكان باطلاً).
And in 'from one dirham to ten' and 'what is between one dirham and ten' he owes nine, and in 'he has from my house what is between this wall and that wall' he has what is between them. [1] (And in 'from one dirham to ten' and 'what is between one dirham and ten' he owes nine) this according to Abu Hanifa because the first endpoint (ghaya) is included necessarily, and the last is not included. And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) both endpoints are included, so ten is obligatory. And according to Zufar, none of the two enters, so eight is obligatory. [2] (And in 'he has from my house what is between this wall and that wall' he has what is between them). [1] His saying: 'And in from one dirham...' means if someone says 'I owe from one dirham to ten' or 'I owe what is between one dirham and ten' then nine is binding upon him. This is according to Imam (Abu Hanifa). And according to them (the two), ten is binding. Zufar said: eight is binding, and it is the analogy (qiyas) because he made the first and last dirham a boundary (hadd) and the boundary is not included in the bounded, so both endpoints are not included. So it becomes like when he says to someone: from this wall to that wall, or what is between these two walls, for the two walls are not included in the iqrar, so here too. And for them (the two): the endpoint must exist, for non-existence cannot be a boundary for existence, and its existence is through its obligation, so both endpoints are included, unlike the sensory example mentioned because it exists and qualifies as a boundary so they are not included. And for the Imam: the endpoint is not included in that which it bounds because the boundary differs from the bounded; this is the principle as Zufar said, but here there is a necessity to include the first endpoint because the second and third dirham are not realized without the first, so the first endpoint is included by necessity. There is no necessity to include the second endpoint, so we take analogy therein and it is not included because the number requires a beginning; if we remove the first from being a beginning, the second becomes the first, so it also becomes removed from being a beginning like the first, and similarly the third and fourth, leading to all being removed from being obligatory, so that would be invalid.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi position on interpreting the phrase 'from one dirham to ten' in an iqrar (acknowledgment of debt). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the phrase obligates nine dirhams: the first endpoint (one dirham) is included out of necessity because without it the count cannot begin, but the last (ten) is excluded because no such necessity exists. Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that both endpoints are included, thus ten dirhams are due. Imam Zufar, applying strict analogy (qiyas) to physical boundaries, argues that neither endpoint enters, so eight dirhams are due. The text provides each view's reasoning: Zufar likens it to saying 'from this wall to that wall' where the walls themselves are not included; the Sahibayn argue that the endpoint must exist to be a boundary, and in numerical iqrar the endpoint exists by being owed itself; Abu Hanifa agrees with the principle that the boundary does not include the bounded, but notes that in counting, the initial number is necessarily included to make the subsequent numbers meaningful. The last number, however, can be excluded by analogy to physical boundaries. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute where the mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is often that of Sahibayn or Abu Hanifa? The text does not give tarjih, so we note the disagreement.

Key Terms
الغاية (al-ghāyah): The endpoint or limit in a phrase like 'from X to Y'; the last point mentioned.
الحد (al-ḥadd): The boundary that defines the limits of something; in iqrar, the limits of the acknowledged amount.
الإقرار (al-iqrār): A voluntary acknowledgment of a right or liability owed to another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if someone says 'I owe from one dirham to ten dirhams', how many dirhams does he owe?
A) Ten
B) Nine
C) Eight
D) Eleven

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, 'from X to Y' in iqrar does not automatically include Y; inclusion depends on necessity and the nature of boundaries.

Physical vs Numerical Endpoints in Iqrar
Ruling that physical endpoints are excluded in iqrar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iqrar of physical boundaries and explain why physical endpoints are excluded while numerical endpoints are included.

Core Idea
Physical endpoints are not included, but numerical endpoints are — because numbers require the starting point to exist.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies a key principle in Hanafi iqrar: when you acknowledge ownership of 'what is between this wall and that wall', the walls themselves are not part of the gift. The commentator then distinguishes this from numerical iqrar ('from one dirham to ten') where the first dirham is included. Understanding this distinction requires grasping the nature of physical vs abstract boundaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
Recognize the physical iqrar ('between this wall and that wall') and the numerical iqrar ('from one dirham to ten') mentioned in the passage.
These are the two scenarios being compared.
2
State the ruling for each
For physical: only the space between the walls is owed, not the walls. For numerical: the first dirham is included in what is owed.
This is the direct legal outcome.
3
Examine the reason
Physical space exists independently of its boundaries. In contrast, the numbers between a starting point and an endpoint only come into existence if the starting point is included.
The key is existential dependence.
4
Apply the Hanafi principle
The ghaya (endpoint) is not included in mahsus (physical objects), but the ibtida (beginning) is included when necessary for the existence of what lies between.
This principle guides many similar cases.
5
Test with other cases
Apply the same logic to other physical boundaries (e.g., 'between this date and that date') and numerical ones (e.g., 'from five to ten' where five is included).
Helps solidify the rule.

وفي له من داري ما بين هذا الحائط إلى هذا الحائط له ما بينهما [٢]قوله : وفي لـه...الخ ؛ يعني مَن قال له: من داري ما بين هذا الحائط إلى هذا الحائط يلزمه ما بين الحائطين فقط دون الحائطين ؛ لأنَّ الغاية لا تدخل تحت المغيا في المحسوس ولا الابتداء بخلاف ما تقدم. [١] قوله : والفرق ...الخ ؛ تقرير الفرق بين المسألتين على مذهب الإمام أنَّ في المسألة الأولى، وهي من درهم إلى عشرة أو من بين درهم إلى عشرة لا يتحقق ما بين الواحد والعشرة إلا إذا انضم الأول. وهذا كما إذ قيل : سنّي ما بين خمسين إلى سنين : أي مع انضمام الآحاد التي دون الخمسين ، بخلاف المسألة الثانية، وهي له من داري ما بين هذا الحائط إلى هذا الحائط ، فإن تحقق ما بين الحائطين ووجوده لا يتوقف على تحقق الحائط الأول ووجوده.
And in 'he has from my house what is between this wall and that wall' he has what is between them. [2] His saying: 'And in he has...' means whoever says 'He has from my house what is between this wall and that wall' is liable for what is between the two walls only, not including the two walls, because the endpoint (ghaya) is not included in the bounded in physical objects, nor is it the beginning, unlike the previous case. [1] His saying: 'And the difference...' details the difference between the two cases according to the Imam: in the first case, which is 'from one dirham to ten' or 'what is between one dirham and ten', what is between one and ten is not realized except with the inclusion of the first. This is like when it is said: 'My age between fifty and sixty' i.e., including the units below fifty. Unlike the second case, which is 'He has from my house what is between this wall and that wall', for the realization of what is between the two walls does not depend on the realization of the first wall.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on physical boundaries in iqrar. When someone acknowledges that another person has the right to 'what is between this wall and that wall', the acknowledged property is only the space between the walls, not the walls themselves. The reason given is that in physical objects (mahsus), the endpoint is not included in the bounded entity. The commentator then clarifies the difference between this case and the numerical case ('from one dirham to ten') according to Abu Hanifa. In the numerical case, the first number must be included because the numbers between one and ten only exist by including the one; without it, the count cannot begin. In contrast, the space between two walls exists independently of the walls themselves. This distinction explains why the first dirham is included in the numerical iqrar while the walls are not included in the physical iqrar.

Key Terms
المحسوس (al-mahṣūs): Physical, sensory objects as opposed to abstract numbers.
الغاية (al-ghāyah): The endpoint or limit in a statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone says 'You have from my house what is between this wall and that wall', what is owed according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) The entire house including the walls
B) Only the space between the walls, not the walls
C) The walls only
D) Nothing, the statement is invalid

Key Takeaway
Physical endpoints are not included in iqrar; numerical endpoints are included due to existential dependence.

Validity of Iqrar for a Fetus: Interpretation as a Bequest
Hanafi ruling that iqrar for a fetus is valid and interpreted as a bequest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iqrar for a fetus, explain its interpretation as a bequest, and identify the conditions for its validity along with the positions of other schools.

Core Idea
Iqrar for a fetus is valid by construing it as a bequest from a third party.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that an acknowledgment (iqrar) made for a fetus (al-haml) is valid. The key legal mechanism is to interpret such an iqrar as a bequest (wasiyyah) from an unspecified third person, even if no cause is mentioned. We will also compare the views of the other schools and identify the conditions that must be met for the iqrar to confer ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and the Act
The passage clearly states that an iqrar for a fetus is valid (sahih). The act is the acknowledgment by a person that a fetus belongs to another person. Note that the fetus is not yet a legal person, so the ruling requires a special interpretation.
2
Locate the Dalil (Proof)
No direct textual proof is cited. Instead, the proof is a rational inference (wajh): the iqrar can be validated by interpreting it as a bequest from someone other than the person making the iqrar. This makes the iqrar legally effective because bequests to an unborn child are valid.
The argument is based on istihsan (juristic preference) rather than qiyas.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The link: when a person says 'the fetus of my slave-girl belongs to X', it cannot be a direct gift or sale because the fetus is not owned separately. However, it can be understood as an acknowledgment that a third party (e.g., the previous owner) had made a bequest of that fetus to X. Thus the iqrar is an admission of a prior legal event.
This interpretation preserves the validity of the iqrar without requiring an impossible transfer.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf (Dispute) with Other Schools
The passage reports that al-Shafi'i has two reports: one invalidating general iqrar, another validating it (the latter being sounder). Malik requires certainty of the fetus's existence at the time of iqrar. Ahmad ibn Hanbal validates it unconditionally. Compare these with the Hanafi position that always validates it by interpretation.
The practical difference: under the Shafi'i weakest opinion, even a clear iqrar would be rejected.
5
Apply the Conditions for Ownership
If the fetus belongs to a slave-girl, the claimant only becomes owner if the fetus existed at the time of iqrar. This is presumed if the slave-girl gives birth within six months of the iqrar (indicating conception before). Additionally, if the person making iqrar mentions a valid cause (inheritance or bequest), the iqrar is also valid.
These conditions ensure the iqrar is not a mere speculation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أقر بالحمل صح، وحُمِلَ على الوصية من غيره، وكذا له إن بين المقر سبباً صالحاً كالإرث والوصية [٢] قوله : ولو أقر ... الخ ; يعني لو أقرّ الرجلُ بالحمل بأن قال : حمل جاريتي هذه لفلان، أو حمل شاتي هذه لفلان، فإنّه يصح؛ لأنَّ في تصحيحه وجها وهو الوصية من جهة غيره ، فيحمل عليه، وإن لم يبين السبب. وقال الشافعي له في قول نقله المزني له عنه : إن أطلق لا يصح، وفي قول يصح ; وهو الأصح، وبه قال أحمد له ، وقال مالك : يصح إن تيقن وجود الحمل عند الإقرار. ذكره السمني في «كمال الدراية). ثم إن كان المقر به حمل جارية، فإنما يستحقه المقر له إذا علم وجوده عند الإقرار، واحتمل ذلك بأن تضعه لأقل من ستَّة أشهر. ذكره العيني في «شرح الكنز» (۳). (۳) قوله : وكذا له...الخ ; يعني وكذا صح الإقرار للحمل إن بين المقر سبباً صالحاً كالإرث والوصية.
And if he (the person) makes an iqrar (acknowledgment) for a fetus, it is valid, and it is interpreted as a bequest from someone else, and likewise for him [the fetus] if the person making iqrar mentions a valid cause such as inheritance or bequest. [2] His saying: 'And if he makes iqrar...' means if a man acknowledges a fetus by saying 'The fetus of my slave-girl is for so-and-so' or 'The fetus of my sheep is for so-and-so', then it is valid because there is a way to validate it, which is a bequest from another person, so it is carried upon that, even if he does not specify the cause. And al-Shafi'i, according to a report al-Muzani transmitted from him, said: if he makes a general iqrar (without specifying) then it is not valid, and in another report it is valid, which is the sounder opinion, and Ahmad (ibn Hanbal) also said it is valid. And Malik said: it is valid if the existence of the fetus at the time of iqrar is certain. This is mentioned by al-Samani in 'Kamal al-Diraya'. Then if the acknowledged item is the fetus of a slave-girl, the person to whom it is acknowledged only becomes entitled to it if its existence at the time of iqrar is known, and that is possible if she gives birth within less than six months. This is mentioned by al-Ayni in 'Sharh al-Kanz'. (3) His saying: 'And likewise for him...' means similarly the iqrar for a fetus is valid if the person making iqrar mentions a valid cause such as inheritance or bequest.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage addresses the validity of iqrar (acknowledgment) for a fetus (haml) in Hanafi fiqh. The Hanafi position is that such an iqrar is valid and must be interpreted as a bequest (wasiyya) from a third party, meaning that the person making iqrar is actually acknowledging that someone else bequeathed the fetus to the claimant. Even if the person does not explicitly mention a cause, the iqrar is still effective because it can be understood as a bequest. The text also notes the positions of other schools: al-Shafi'i reportedly has two opinions—one invalidating general iqrar for a fetus, another validating it (the latter being stronger); Ahmad ibn Hanbal also validates it; Malik requires certainty of the fetus's existence at the time of iqrar. Additionally, if the fetus belongs to a slave-girl, the claimant only becomes owner if the fetus existed at the time of iqrar, which is presumed if the slave-girl gives birth within six months of the iqrar (indicating the fetus was conceived before). Finally, if the person making iqrar mentions a valid cause such as inheritance (irth) or bequest (wasiyya), the iqrar is also valid.

Key Terms
الحمل (al-ḥaml): A fetus in the womb; something that will be born.
الوصية (al-waṣiyyah): A bequest; a testamentary disposition made by a deceased person.
الإقرار (al-iqrār): A legal acknowledgment by a person of a right or obligation against themselves.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The iqrar requires a written document to be valid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates iqrar for a fetus by interpreting it as a bequest from a third party, making it a flexible tool for future interests.

The Oath of the Confessee When the Confessor Claims False Confession: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath requirement after alleged false confession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute over whether al-muqarr lahu must swear an oath when the confessor claims he lied, and state the mufta bihi position and its later restriction to duress cases.

Core Idea
When a confessor claims falsehood, the school rules the confessee must swear truth—but later scholars limit this to cases where the confessor was forced to lie.

This passage addresses a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school: when a person who made a judicial confession (iqrar) later claims he lied, must the beneficiary of that confession (al-muqarr lahu) take an oath that the confession was true? Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the confessor's claim is not heeded, so no oath is required. However, Abu Yusuf argues that due to widespread deceit, the confesee should be made to swear. The passage reveals how fatwa evolves: the school initially adopted Abu Yusuf's absolute ruling, but later scholars restricted it to situations where the confessor was compelled to lie. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi approach to judicial oaths and the role of changing social conditions in ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
State the positions of the three imams
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani: the confessor's claim of falsehood is not heeded, so no oath is required. Abu Yusuf: the confessee (or his heir) must swear that the confessor was truthful. The fatwa initially followed Abu Yusuf absolutely.
The later restriction (duress condition) is added by later scholars.
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
The opposing view comes from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. They argue that the heir's right was not established at the time of confession (if the claimant is an heir), and the original confession is presumed true. This is based on istishab (presumption of continuity).
This is the Zahir al-Riwayah position.
3
Analyse Abu Yusuf's proof and the later restriction
Abu Yusuf argues that due to 'change in people's conditions' and prevalence of deceit, the oath is necessary. The plaintiff (confessee) is not harmed by the oath if truthful. Later scholars (al-Shami, Shaykhuna) qualify: the oath is only required when the confessor was compelled to lie (e.g., under duress).
This qualification comes from cases in the Book of Sulh.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
If the confessor claims falsehood without duress, under Abu Hanifa/Muhammad no action; under absolute Abu Yusuf, oath required; under the restricted view, oath only if duress. The mufta bihi is the restricted view.
Later sources like Hashiyat Miskin confirm this restriction.
5
Apply to scenarios involving heirs
The passage also covers claims by or against heirs. If the confessor's heir claims falsehood, the correct view is that the heir of the confessee must swear. If the claim is against the confessee's heirs, they swear 'we do not know he was lying'.
This shows the principles applied to inheritance cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعند أبي حنيفة له ومحمد : لا يلتفت إلى قوله لكن يُفْتَى على قول أبي يوسف الله : إِنَّ الْمُقَرَّ له يَحْلِفُ أَنَّ الْمُقِرَّ لم يكن كاذباً.
وكذا لو ادعى وارث المُقِرِّ فعند البعض لا يلتفت إلى قوله ؛ لأنَّ حق الورثة لم يكن ثابتا في زمان الإقرار، والأصح التحليف، لأنَّ الورثة ادعوا أمراً لو أقر به المقر له يلزمه ، فإذا أنكر يُسْتَخْلَفَ، وإن كان الدعوى على ورثة المقر له فاليمين عليهم بالعلم أنا لا نعلم أنه كان كاذباً
ويقول أبي يوسف يفتى ؛ لتغير أحوال الناس وكثرة الخداع والخيانات، وهو يتضرر بذلك، والمدعي لا يضره اليمين إن كان صادقاً فيصار إليه.
[1] قوله : إن المقر له يحلف ... الخ ؛ قال الشامي في «حاشيته على الدر المختار») : وظاهره أن المقر إذا ادعى الإقرار كاذباً يحلف المقر له أو وارثه على المفتى من قول أبي يوسف الله مطلقاً، سواء كان مضطراً إلى الكذب في الإقرار أو لا.

حيث نقل الشارح له عن «شرح الوهبانية» للشرنبلالي ما يدلُّ على أنه يفتى بقول أبي يوسف الله من أنه يحلف له : إنَّ المقرَّ ما أقرَّ كاذباً في صورة يوجد فيها اضطرار المقر إلى الكذب في الإقرار كالصورة التي تقدمت ونحوها. كذا في «حاشية مسكين»(۲) للشيخ محمد أبي السعود المصري.
وفيه : إنّه لا يتعين الحمل على هذا ؛ لأنَّ العبارة هناك في هذا ونحوه، فقوله :
ونحوه، تحتمل أن يكون كلُّ ما كان من قبيل الرجوع بعد الإقرار مطلقاً، ويدل عليه ما
بعده من قوله : وبه جزم المصنف له، فراجعه. انتهى.
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: his statement is not heeded, but fatwa is given on the statement of Abu Yusuf: Indeed, the one in whose favor the confession is made takes an oath that the confessor was not lying. Similarly, if the heir of the confessor claims, according to some his statement is not heeded because the right of the heirs was not established at the time of the confession, but the more correct view is that he is made to take an oath, because the heirs claimed a matter which, if the confessee had admitted, would obligate him, so if he denies, he is made to take an oath. And if the claim is against the heirs of the confessee, the oath is upon them by knowledge that 'we do not know that he was lying'. And Abu Yusuf says: fatwa is given due to the change in people's conditions and the prevalence of deceit and betrayals, and he is harmed by that, while the plaintiff is not harmed by the oath if he is truthful, so it is resorted to. [1] His statement: 'Indeed, the confessee takes an oath...' etc; al-Shami said in his Hashiyah on al-Durr al-Mukhtar: Its apparent meaning is that if the confessor claims that the confession was false, the confessee or his heir takes an oath according to the fatwa from the statement of Abu Yusuf, absolutely, whether the confessor was compelled to lie in the confession or not. Our teacher said: It is not so; due to various issues coming later before the Book of Sulh, at the statement of the author: 'He confessed to property in a document and had witnesses testify to it, then claimed that some of that confessed property was a loan and some was interest... etc.' Where the commentator for him from 'Sharh al-Wahbaniyya' by al-Shurunbulali indicates that fatwa is given on the statement of Abu Yusuf that he takes an oath: 'Indeed, the confessor did not confess falsely' in a situation where the confessor is compelled to lie in the confession, like the situation that preceded and similar ones. This is in 'Hashiyat Miskin' by Shaykh Muhammad Abi al-Su'ud al-Misri. In it: It is not necessarily restricted to this; because the expression there is about this and similar, so his statement 'and similar' possibly means everything that is of the kind of retraction after confession absolutely, and it is indicated by what follows his statement: 'and the author resolved upon it', so refer to it. End.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the oath required when a person who made a confession (iqrar) later claims that his confession was false. The positions are: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani say that the statement of the confessor (that he lied) is not heeded, meaning no oath is imposed on the confessee (the one in whose favor the confession was made). However, fatwa is given on the view of Abu Yusuf, who requires the confessee to take an oath that the confessor was truthful. This is because people have become deceitful, and the plaintiff (the confessee) is not harmed by taking an oath if he is truthful. The passage then addresses cases involving heirs. If the heir of the confessor claims (that the confessor lied), some say his claim is not heeded because the heir's right was not established at the time of the confession, but the correct view is that the heir must take an oath. If the claim is against the heirs of the confessee, the oath is upon them that they do not know the confessor lied. Later, a commentary from al-Shami and a teacher (shaykhuna) clarifies that Abu Yusuf's ruling may be restricted to cases where the confessor was compelled to lie, as seen in future sections of the book. This is a nuanced discussion within the Hanafi school, showing how fatwa evolves with changing circumstances and how later scholars qualify earlier general rulings.

Key Terms
المقر له (al-muqarr lahu): The person in whose favor a confession (iqrar) is made.
المقر (al-muqirr): The person who makes the confession (iqrar).
اليمين (al-yameen): The legal oath taken in court to affirm or deny a claim.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's mufta bihi requires an oath from the confessee when the confessor claims false confession, but only if the confessor was under duress to lie.

Liability in Acknowledgment: Usurpation, Deposit, and Loan
Comparison of three acknowledgment cases affecting liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between three acknowledgment scenarios and state whether the acknowledger is liable based on the wording of his admission.

Core Idea
Liability follows admission of taking, not mere receipt of property.

In disputes over property, the wording of an acknowledgment determines liability. This lesson examines three common scenarios from Hanafi fiqh: when the acknowledger admits taking but claims permission, when he says he received a deposit, and when the claimant reclassifies the transaction as a loan. The key is whether the acknowledger's words admit a cause of liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
List the three cases: (a) acknowledger says 'I took from you' but claims permission, (b) acknowledger says 'you gave me as a deposit' and it was lost, (c) acknowledger says 'you gave me as a deposit' but claimant says 'it was a loan'.
These are the core comparisons in the passage.
2
Determine if the acknowledger admitted a cause of liability
In Hanafi law, the cause of liability (sabab al-daman) for property is taking (akhdh) without right. If the acknowledger admits taking, he admits the cause. If he attributes the act to the other party (as in deposit), he does not.
The distinction hinges on who performed the taking.
3
Apply the rule for each scenario
Scenario (a): admits taking → liable, his claim of permission is a defense denied by claimant. Scenario (b): attributes taking to claimant → not liable, claimant's allegation of usurpation is denied. Scenario (c): both agree on permission (loan requires permission) → liable because the taking creates a loan contract.
The exception is loan where both parties agree on permission.
4
Understand the reasoning for deposit vs. loan distinction
In deposit, the owner gives voluntarily, so the taker does not assume liability for loss without transgression. In loan, the taker becomes liable because the loan contract obligates return of the exact property.
This is why the ruling differs even though both involve permission.
5
Summarize the overarching principle
The ruling depends on whether the acknowledger's statement admits a cause of liability. If he admits taking, he is liable unless he can prove an exception (e.g., permission) with evidence. If he does not admit taking, the claimant must prove liability.
This principle applies broadly in qada cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الآخر : بل غصباً، ضَمِنَ، وفي : أعطيتنيه وديعة، وقال الآخر: غصبتنيه لا : والفرق أن في الأَوَّلِ أقرَّ بوجوب الضمان ، وهو الأخذ، وفي الثاني : لم يُقِرَّ بذلك، بل الآخر يدعى عليه الغصب، وهو ينكره ، فالقول له. وديعة، فهلكت تلك الدراهم في يدي من غير تعد، وقال المقر له : لا بل أخذتها مني حال كونها غصباً، ضَمِنَ القِرُّ ما أقرَّ بأخذه له ؛ لأنه أقرَّ بسبب الضمان وهو الأخذ، ثمّ إنّه ادعى ما يوجب البراءة وهو الإذن بالأخذ ، والآخر ينكر، فالقول قوله مع يمينه. بخلاف ما إذا قال له المقَرُّ له : بل أخذتها قرضاً، حيث يكون القولُ للمقر له؛ لأنهما تصادقا على أنّ الأخذ حصل بإذنه ، وهذا لا يوجب الضمان على الآخذ إلا بسبب الأخذ، باعتبار عقدِ الضمان ، فالمالك يدعي عليه العقد، وذلك ينكر، فالقول قول المنكر.
[١] قوله : وفي أعطيتنيه وديعة...الخ ؛ يعني لو قال المقر : أعطيتني ألف درهم وديعة فهلكت، وقال الآخر : لا ، غصبت ذلك الألف منّي لا يضمن المقر؛ لأنه لم يقر الضمان، بل أقر بالإعطاء ، وهو فعلُ المقر له، فلا يكونُ مُقِرِّاً على نفسه بسبب الضمان، والمقر له يدعي عليه بسبب الضمان ، وهو ينكر، فكان القول قول المنكر. فإن قيل : إعطاؤه والدفعُ لا يكون إلا بقبضه ، فوجب أن يكون إقراراً بالقبض. أجيب : بأن الدفع قد يكون بالتخلية والوضع بين يديه، ولـو اقـتـضـى ذلـك فالمقتضي لا عموم له ؛ لأنه ثابت ضرورة، فيتقدَّرُ بقدر الضرورة، فلا يظهر في حق انعقادِهِ سبباً للضمان ؛ لأنه عدم في حق غيره.
[٢] قوله : والفرق...الخ ؛ حاصلُ الفرق أنَّ في المسألة الأولى وهي ما إذا قال : أخذتُ منك ألفاً...الخ، أقر المقر بموجب الضمان، وهو الأخذ، ثمّ ادّعى المقر موجب البراءة وهو الإذن، والمقر له ينكره، والقول قول المنكر مع يمينه. وفي المسألة الثانية، وهي ما إذا قال : أعطيتنيه وديعة...الخ أضاف المقر الفعل إلى غيره، وذلك الغير يدعي عليه بسبب الضمان وهو الغصب، فكان القولُ لمنكر.
And the other said: 'Rather, as usurpation,' so he is liable. And (the case): 'You gave it to me as a deposit' and the other said: 'You usurped it from me' – no liability. The difference is that in the first case, he acknowledged the obligation of liability, which is the taking; in the second, he did not acknowledge that, rather the other claims usurpation against him and he denies it, so the statement is his. (Case of) deposit: those dirhams perished in my hand without transgression, and the person in whose favor the acknowledgment was made said: 'No, rather you took them from me while they were usurped' – the acknowledger is liable for what he acknowledged taking because he acknowledged the cause of liability, which is the taking, then he claims what necessitates exemption (permission), and the other denies, so the statement is his with his oath. Unlike if the person in whose favor the acknowledgment was made said: 'Rather, you took it as a loan' – then the statement is of the person in whose favor the acknowledgment was made because they both agree that the taking occurred with his permission, and this does not impose liability on the taker except due to the taking by virtue of the contract of liability; the owner claims the contract against him and he denies it, so the statement of the denier is accepted. [1] His saying: 'And in: You gave it to me as a deposit, etc.', meaning if the acknowledger said: 'You gave me a thousand dirhams as a deposit and they perished', and the other said: 'No, you usurped that thousand from me', the acknowledger is not liable because he did not acknowledge liability; rather he acknowledged the giving, which is the act of the beneficiary, so he does not acknowledge a cause of liability against himself. The beneficiary claims liability against him, and he denies it, so the statement of the denier is accepted. If it is said: 'Giving and delivery cannot occur except by his taking, so it must be an acknowledgment of taking', the answer is that delivery may be by vacating and placing in his hands, and even if that implied it, the implication is not general because it is established by necessity, so it is limited to the extent of necessity, so it does not appear as a cause of liability in terms of its formation because it is absent in respect of others. [2] His saying: 'And the difference, etc.': The gist of the difference is that in the first case, where he said: 'I took a thousand from you, etc.', the acknowledger acknowledged the requirement of liability, which is taking, then claimed the cause of exemption, which is permission, and the beneficiary denies it, so the statement of the denier with his oath is accepted. In the second case, where he said: 'You gave it to me as a deposit, etc.', the acknowledger attributed the act to the other, and that other claims against him a cause of liability, i.e. usurpation, so the statement of the denier is accepted.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with a comparative analysis of three scenarios involving acknowledgment (iqrar) and liability (daman). The author distinguishes cases based on whether the acknowledger's words admit a cause of liability. In the first scenario, the acknowledger says 'I took a thousand from you as usurpation' (or similar), but then claims he took with permission. Since he admitted the taking—which is the cause of liability—his claim of permission is a defense against the liability, but the other party denies permission; thus the acknowledger is liable and must pay compensation (daman). The second scenario: the acknowledger says 'you gave me a thousand as a deposit' and it was lost without his fault; here he does not admit any cause of liability because he attributes the act to the other party. The other party argues it was usurpation, but since the acknowledger denies that, his statement is accepted, and he is not liable. The third scenario: the other party says it was a loan; here both agree the taking was with permission (since a loan requires permission), so the acknowledger is liable because the taking itself creates a contract of liability (loan must be returned). The key principle: if the acknowledging party admits the cause of liability (taking), his denial of the specific claim (e.g., usurpation) does not absolve him; but if he does not admit the cause, his denial is accepted. The text also addresses an objection about delivery implying taking, but the response is that delivery can be constructive (takhliya) and any implication is limited by necessity.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): A statement by which a person acknowledges a right (haqq) in favor of another, binding upon himself.
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation: taking another's property without right and without permission.
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for loss or damage, or to return the property.
وديعة (wadi'ah): Deposit: property left with another for safekeeping without compensation.
قرض (qard): Loan of fungible property that is to be returned in kind.
أخذ (akhdh): Taking; the physical act of receiving property.
تخلیة (takhliyah): Vacating; leaving property in a place where the recipient can take possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first scenario (acknowledging taking allegedly in usurpation but claiming permission), why is the acknowledger liable despite claiming permission?
A) Because he admitted the taking, which is the cause of liability, and his claim of permission is a defense that the other party denies.
B) Because he is the one who spoke first.
C) Because the property was lost in his hands.
D) Because he did not return the property immediately.

Key Takeaway
The key factor determining liability in acknowledgment cases is whether the acknowledger admitted a cause of liability, such as taking.
