Claim of Deposit and Ownership: Abu Hanifa vs. the Two Disciples
Intra-Hanafi dispute on who must prove ownership in deposit claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's ruling on proving ownership in a deposit claim and distinguish it from the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Core Idea
Acknowledgment of a deposit creates liability; a later claim of ownership does not overcome it without immediate return.

When a person admits that an item was a deposit with him and then claims he took it back as his own, whose word prevails? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents the Hanafi position and the disagreement of the two disciples. You will learn the ruling, the reasoning behind it, and the practical difference it makes in court.

Guided Path
1
State the legal issue
Identify the scenario: a person acknowledges that an item was a deposit with another, then claims he took it back and now owns it. The other person claims it as his own. Who must prove ownership?
The acknowledger has admitted possession, which weakens his claim.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's ruling and its rationale
Abu Hanifa holds that the acknowledger must immediately surrender the item to the claimant and then present evidence of ownership. The rationale: he acknowledged the possession and taking, which is a cause of liability.
The acknowledger's own admission binds him to return the item.
3
Identify the opposing view of the two disciples
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad analogize this to the case of a deposit where the item must be handed to the claimant first, and then the depositary may sue. They allow the acknowledger to retain the item until the claimant proves ownership.
Their analogy is based on the principle that possession is a proof of ownership until rebutted.
4
Compare the proofs and Hanafi response
The disciples rely on qiyas to the deposit case. Abu Hanifa responds that the analogy is imperfect because the acknowledger's admission creates a stronger presumption against him.
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Abu Hanifa, the item changes hands immediately; under the disciples, it stays with the acknowledger pending proof.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text indicates that the relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's view. The acknowledger must return the item first and then prove ownership.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي : هذا كان وديعة لي عندك ، فأخذته ، فقال : هو لي أَخَذَه : أي الْمُقَرُّ له ؛ لأنه أقر بيده ، ثُمَّ ادَّعى أنَّه كان لي فأخذتُه ، فيسلمه إلى المقر له، ويقيم البينة. [١] قوله : وفي هذا ...الخ ؛ يعني لو قال المقر للمقرّ له : إن هذا الشيء كان لي عندك وديعة، فأخذتُها منك ، وقال المقوله : هو لي، فهو يأخذه. [٢] قوله : لأنه أقر...الخ ؛ يعني لأنَّ المقر أقرَّ باليد له، ثم بالأخذ منه وهو سبب الضمان ، ثمَّ ادَّعى استحقاقه عليه فلا تقبل دعواه ، فوجب عليه رد عينه إن كان قائماً ، وقيمته إن كان هالكاً، ثمّ يقيم البينة على صدق دعواه إن قدر على ذلك. [٣] قوله : وصدق من قال....الخ ؛ يعني قال : أجرت فرسي هذا فلاناً فركبه ورده إلي أو قال : أجرتُ ثوبي هذا فلاناً فلبسه وردَّه إلي ، وقال فلان : كذبت بل الفرس والثوب لي ، فالقول قول المقر ويصدق. وكذا الإعارة والإسكان، فمن قال : أعرتُك داري هذه ثم رددت علي، أو أسلفتك داري هذه ثم رددت علي، وقال الآخر : كذبت، وهذه الدار اليا، فصدق المقر على الخلاف، ولو قال : خاط فلان ثوبي بدرهم مثلاً ، ثم قبضه، وقال فلان : كذبت والثوب ثوبي ، فصدق المقر، ويكون القول قوله. [٤] قوله : وعندهما يجب أن يسلم ... الخ ؛ يعني عند أ. أبي يوسف ومحمد له يكون القولُ قولُ الذي أخذ منه الفرس أو الثوب، ويجب على المقر أن يسلّمه إلى المقر له، ثم يدعيه عند القاضي، كما في مسألة الوديعة. قال في «الكفاية": هذا إذا لم يكن الدابة والثوب معروفاً أنه للمقر، أما لو كان...
And (the case): 'This was a deposit with you and I took it,' and he said: 'It is mine,' he takes it, i.e., the person in whose favor the acknowledgment was made, because he acknowledged (the other's) possession, then claimed it was his and he took it, so he surrenders it to the beneficiary and presents evidence. [1] His saying: 'And in this, etc.': meaning if the acknowledger said to the beneficiary: 'This thing was my deposit with you and I took it from you', and the beneficiary said: 'It is mine', then he takes it. [2] His saying: 'Because he acknowledged, etc.': meaning because the acknowledger acknowledged possession for him, then taking from him which is a cause of liability, then claimed entitlement against him, so his claim is not accepted; therefore he must return the physical item if it still exists, or its value if it perished, then he may present evidence of the truth of his claim if he is able. [3] His saying: 'And the one who said is believed, etc.': meaning he said: 'I leased my horse to so-and-so and he rode it and returned it to me', or said: 'I leased my garment to so-and-so and he wore it and returned it to me', and the other said: 'You lie, rather the horse and garment are mine', then the statement of the one who made the acknowledgment is accepted and he is believed. Likewise lending (i'ara) and lodging (iskan): whoever says: 'I lent you my house and you returned it to me', or 'I gave you my house as a dwelling and you returned it to me', and the other said: 'You lie, this house is mine', then the one who acknowledged is believed, according to the disagreement. And if he said: 'So-and-so tailored my garment for a dirham, then he took it', and the other said: 'You lie, the garment is mine', then the one who acknowledged is believed, and his statement is accepted. [4] His saying: 'And according to them, it is obligatory to surrender, etc.': meaning according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the statement is of the one who took the horse or garment, and it is obligatory for the acknowledger to surrender it to the beneficiary, then he may claim it before the judge, as in the case of the deposit. He said in al-Kifayah: 'This is if the animal or garment is not known to belong to the acknowledger; but if it is known...'
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a claim where a person says 'This item was my deposit with you and I took it back' and the other claims 'It is mine'. According to Imam Abu Hanifa (as indicated by 'he said'), the person who took the item (the acknowledger) must surrender it to the claimant and then present evidence of his ownership. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ: they say the acknowledger must first hand the item over to the beneficiary (the one claiming it), and then the beneficiary may claim it in court. The text also extends this principle to cases of lease, loan, and tailoring. The key is whether the item is known to be the property of the acknowledger; if it is known, the ruling might differ. The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's view.

Key Terms
وديعة (wadi'ah): A deposit: property left with another for safekeeping without compensation.
يد (yad): Possession/hand; acknowledgment of possession implies a claim of right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what must a person do when he acknowledges that an item was a deposit but then claims it as his own?
A) Keep the item until he presents evidence of ownership.
B) Return the item to the claimant immediately and then present evidence.
C) Swear an oath and keep the item.
D) Pay the value of the item.

Key Takeaway
In disputes over deposits where the depositary admits the deposit but claims ownership, Hanafi law requires him to return the item immediately and then prove his ownership.

Belief of Claimant in Lease and Tailoring Cases
Ruling on who is believed when claiming lease/use and return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on who is believed in lease/lending return disputes and apply it to distinguish from deposit cases.

Core Idea
Owner's claim of return is believed because possession was purpose-specific, unlike deposit.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on judicial credibility when the owner claims to have leased or lent an item and received it back, while the other party denies and asserts ownership. Imam Abu Hanifa gives priority to the owner's statement, while the two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, analogize to the deposit case where the person in possession is believed. Understanding the reasoning behind each view sharpens your ability to apply Hanafi procedural fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what Imam Abu Hanifa rules: the owner who claims to have leased out his horse/garment and received it back is believed, and the item remains with him.
This applies also to lending (i'ara), lodging (iskan), and tailoring (khayat).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree. They hold that the person currently in possession (the alleged lessee/borrower) should be believed, and the owner must surrender the item then prove his claim.
Their position is analogous to the deposit (wadi'a) case.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand that the Sahibayn use qiyas: just as in deposit the bailee is believed, here the person who took the item should be believed because he is in possession.
The source text explicitly mentions the deposit analogy.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Note Abu Hanifa's distinguishing factor: the owner transferred possession for a specific purpose (lease, loan, tailoring), and his claim of return aligns with that purpose being completed. In deposit, possession is for safekeeping, not for use.
Thus, the owner's statement is more plausible in lease/lending cases.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the owner retains the item immediately; under the Sahibayn, he must surrender it and then sue. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Abu Hanifa's.
The text also hints at a condition (from al-Kifayah) if the item is known to belong to the owner, but that is cut off.

And the one who said is believed: 'I leased my horse or this garment, and he rode it or wore it and returned it to me' or 'I had my garment tailored for so much and he took it' – this is according to Abu Hanifa. And according to the two disciples: he must surrender it to the beneficiary, then claim it as in the case of deposit. He said in al-Kifayah: 'This is if the animal or garment is not known to belong to the acknowledger; but if it is known...'
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage extends the previous discussion to cases of lease (ijarah), lending (i'ara), lodging (iskan), and tailoring (khayat). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, someone who claims to have leased out his horse or garment and received it back, or to have had his garment tailored and the tailor took it, is believed over the other party's denial of ownership. This is because the person making the claim is the original owner who transferred possession for a purpose, and his claim of return is plausible. According to the two disciples, however, the person in possession (the one who allegedly took the item) is to be believed, and the claimant must surrender the item first and then prove his case, similar to the deposit scenario. The relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's. The text mentions a condition in al-Kifayah: if the animal or garment is known to belong to the acknowledger, the ruling may differ, but the text cuts off before completion.

Key Terms
إجارة (ijarah): Leasing: a contract for temporary use of a property in exchange for rent.
إعارة (i'arah): Lending: giving temporary use of property without charge.
تصديق (tasdiq): Believing/accepting the statement of a party in a dispute without requiring proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) A, the one who claims to have leased the horse.
B) B, because he is in possession.
C) Neither, they must both present evidence.
D) The horse is divided equally.

Key Takeaway
In lease, lending, and tailoring return disputes, the original owner's claim is believed over the possessor's, contrary to the deposit analogy.

Legal Effect of Nukool in Deposit Claims: Badl or Iqrar?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the nature of nukool

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on nukool to a deposit dispute and explain the intra-Hanafi difference between Abu Hanifa (badl) and the Sahibayn (iqrar).

Core Idea
Nukool makes the depositary liable whether it is badl or iqrar — the concept differs, but the ruling is the same.

The Hanafi school is unanimous that when a depositary refuses the oath (nukool) in a claim over a deposit, the depositary becomes liable for the claimed amount. However, the great imams differ on why: Is the refusal an implicit admission (iqrar) as the Sahibayn hold, or a mere concession of the right to deny (badl) as Abu Hanifa holds? This lesson unpacks that dispute through a classic case of two claimants, equipping you to see how a shared outcome can rest on different legal theories.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the legal scenario
Two men each claim that a sum held by a third party is a deposit from him. There is no evidence, and the depositary denies. The judge demands an oath, but the depositary refuses (nukool). Recognise this as a case where nukool triggers liability.
The deposit cannot logically belong to both entirely; each claim is considered separately.
2
2. Understand the two characterisations of nukool
According to Abu Hanifa, nukool is badl (concession) – the defendant gives up his right to deny, but does not admit. According to the Sahibayn, nukool is iqrar (admission) – the refusal implies the claim is true.
In Hanafi usul, iqrar is a stronger proof than badl, but both lead to liability here.
3
3. Apply the ruling to the case
If the depositary refuses to swear to both claimants, the deposit is divided equally between them, and the depositary must pay an additional sum of the same amount (also divided). This outcome is the same under both views.
The additional sum compensates for the fact that the deposit cannot be held by both; it is a form of guarantee.
4
4. Compare with a different scenario: deposit from a usurper
Abu Hanifa distinguishes this case from one where the deposit originally came from a usurper – there, the second depositary (who received it from the usurper) is liable because he took from a transgressor. Here, the deposit came from a trustee (the depositor) so no transgression.
This analogy (garment blown into one's lap) shows that possession without transgression does not create liability.
5
5. Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of the Sahibayn (nukool as iqrar), as it is stronger in establishing liability and supported by later authorities like Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani.
For fatwa, one may rely on either view since the outcome is identical, but the iqrar view is preferred.

ولو ادعى كل من رجلين ألفاً مع ثالث أنه له أودعه إياه، فنكل لهما ، فهذا وألف آخر عليه لهما
وفرق أبو حنيفة بأن المودَعَ إذا دَفَعَ إلى الغير لا يضمن ما لم يفارقه ، فإذا فارقَ ترك الحفظ فيضمن، ولا يضمن الآخر ؛ لأنه صارَ مودَعاً حيث غاب الآخر ولا صنع له في ذلك، كثوب الفتْهُ الرَّيحُ في حجرِ إنسان.
ولو ادعى كل من رجلين ألفا مع ثالث أنه له أودعه إياه، فنكل لهما ، ام فهذا وألف آخر عليه لهما ) : ادَّعى زيد على عمرو أنَّ هذا الألف الذي في يدك أودعته إياك، وأدعى بكر على عمرو كذلك ، ولا بينة لأحد، وعمرو منكر، فالقاضي يُحلفه علم فكذلك في الظاهر، وحكى أبو اليسر له : إنه لا يرجع وإليه أشار شمس الائمة . كذا في «كمال الدراية»، و«النهاية»، وغيرهما.
(1) قوله : وفرّق أبو حنيفة ... الخ ؛ حاصله : إن الفرق لأبي حنيفة حيث لم يضمن الثاني في مودَع المودع ، وضَمِنَ في مودع الغاصب : أن المال وصل إلى مودع المودع من أمين ، فلم يكن متعدياً بوضع يده عليه، ووصل إلى مودع الغاصب من متعد، وكان متعدياً بوضع يده عليه). [٢] قوله : ولو ادعى...الخ ؛ يعني ومَن كان في يده ألف فادعاها رجلين كلُّ واحدٍ منهما أن هذه الألف أودعها إياه ، وصاحب اليد أبى أن يحلف لهما، فهذه الألف تكون بينهما على السوية، وعلى صاحب اليد تكون ألف أخرى بين المدعيين.
[٣] قوله : فنكل ؛ هذا معطوف على فعل محذوف وهو أنكر، وتقدير الكلام هكذا : فأنكر ذلك الثالث، وليس للمدعيين بينةً فعرض على المنكر اليمين فنكل، وأبى عنه للمدعيين. [٤] قوله : فالقاضي يحلّفه ؛ لأنَّ دعوى كلّ واحدٍ تحتمل الصدق فتكون صحيحة على سبيل الانفراد دون الاجتماع ؛ لكون الألف مودعاً من اثنين بكامله مستحيلاً، ولا بينة للمدعيين ، فيستحق الحلف على المنكر للحديث المشهور، أعني : «البينة على المدعي واليمين على المنكر»
لكلِّ واحدٍ على الانفراد ، ويبدأ بأيهما شاء"، فإن تشاحا" أقرع بينهما ، وإن نَكَلَ لأحدهما يُحلّفُه للآخر، فأن نَكَلَ له أيضاً ، فهذا الألفُ مع ألف آخر عليه يكون لهما ؛ لأنه أوجب الحق لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما سواء بالبذل ، أو بالإقرار، وذلك حجّةٌ في حقه، ويصرفُ الألفُ إليهما، وصار قاضياً نصف حق كل منهما بنصف حق الآخر فيغرمه.
[1]قوله : على الانفراد ؛ لتغاير الحقين ؛ فإنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ منهما ادعاه بانفراده. [٢] قوله : ويبدأ بأيهما شاء ؛ لأنَّ الجمع بينهما متعذر، وأولوية أحدهما من الآخر معدومة ؛ لعدم المرجّح ، ولا ضرر للآخرِ في التأخير على تقدير النكول الأول ؛ لأنه لا يعطى به حتى نكل للثاني. [٣] قوله : فإن تشاحا...الخ ؛ أي تنازعا في البداية بالحلف، أقرع القاضي بينهما تطيباً لقلبهما، ونفياً لتهمة الميل إلى أحدهما ، قال في «المصباح» : تـشـاح الـقــوم بالتشديد : إذا شح بعضهم بعضاً ونازع. انتهى. قال في «الصراح»: تشاح الرجلان عن الأمر لا يريدان أن يفوتهما. انتهى.
[٤] قوله : وإن نكل لأحدهما ...الخ ؛ توضيحه : إنَّ ذلك الثالث المدعى عليه إن حلف لأحد المدعيين يحلف للثاني، فإن حلف فلا شيء لهما لعدم الحجة من جهة المدعيين، وإن نكل للثاني بعدما حلف للأوَّل يقضى للثاني ؛ لوجود الحجة، أعني النكول، وإن نكل للأوّل يحلف للثاني، ولا يقضي للأوّل بالنكول ؛ لأن من جهة الآخر أن يقول : لو بدأت بالاستحلاف لكان نكل أيضاً، ولو نكل للثاني أيضاً يقضي بالألف بين المدعيين نصفين على ما ذكر في «الكتاب»؛ لاستوائهما في الحجة. كما إذا أقاما البيئة ويغرم صاحب اليد ألفاً أخرى بينهما ؛ لأن المودع المنكر أوجب الحقِّ لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما يبذله على مذهب الإمام ؛ لأنَّ النكول بذل عنده بإقراره على مذهب صاحبيه، فإن النكول إقرار عندهما ، والإقرار أو البذل حجة في حق المودع
And if each of two men claims a thousand from a third [saying] that he deposited it with him, and he refused the oath to both, then this [thousand] and another thousand against him are for them. Abu Hanifa distinguished [the case] saying: the depositary, when he gives [the deposit] to another, does not become liable unless he parts with it. If he parts with it, he abandons safekeeping and becomes liable. And he does not become liable for the other because he became a depositary when the other was absent and he had no hand in that — like a garment that the wind blew into the lap of a person. And if each of two men claims a thousand from a third [saying] that he deposited it with him, and he refused the oath to both, well this and another thousand against him are for them: Zaid claimed against Amr that this thousand in your hand I deposited with you, and Bakr claimed against Amr similarly; there is no evidence for anyone, and Amr denies, so the judge makes him swear... The learned one said, such is in the outward [madhhab]; and Abu al-Yusr narrated that he does not return, and Shams al-A'imma indicated this. Thus in 'Kamal al-Dirayah', 'al-Nihayah', and others. (1) His statement: 'And Abu Hanifa distinguished...' is summary: The distinction for Abu Hanifa where he did not make the second liable in the depositary's deposit, and made liable in the usurper's deposit: because the wealth reached the depositary's depositary from an amin (trustee), so he was not a transgressor by placing his hand on it; and it reached the usurper's depositary from a transgressor, and he was a transgressor by placing his hand on it. [2] His statement: 'And if each of two men...' means: and the one in whose hand is a thousand, two men each claim that this thousand is a deposit from him, and the possessor refuses to swear to both, then this thousand is divided equally between them, and upon the possessor is another thousand divided between the two claimants. [3] His statement: 'he refused the oath (nukl)' is a conjunct of a deleted verb: he denied. The estimation of the speech: So the third denied, and there is no evidence for the claimants, so the oath was presented to the denier and he refused, and he refused it to the claimants. [4] His statement: 'The judge makes him swear' because the claim of each is possibly true, so it is valid individually but not jointly, since the whole thousand cannot be deposited by two [simultaneously]. And there is no evidence for the claimants, so the denier deserves the oath due to the famous hadith: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the denier.' For each individually, and he starts with whichever he wishes. If they dispute, he casts lots between them. If he refuses the oath to one, he makes him swear for the other. If he also refuses to him, then this thousand and another thousand against him are for them both, because he obligated the right for each of them equally either by badhl (concession) or by iqrar (admission). That is a proof against him, and the thousand is disbursed to them, and he becomes liable for half of each's right due to half of the other's right. [1] His statement: 'individually' because the rights are different; each claimed it individually. [2] His statement: 'starts with whichever he wishes' because combining between them is impossible, and priority of one over the other is non-existent due to lack of a preferential factor, and there is no harm to the other in delay on the supposition of the first nukool because nothing is awarded thereby until he refuses to the second. [3] His statement: 'if they dispute' meaning they conflict over starting with the oath, the judge casts lots between them to give comfort to their hearts and to remove suspicion of bias to one. He said in 'al-Misbah': 'Tashahha al-qawm' with tashdid: when one covets from another and contends. End. He said in 'al-Surah': 'Tashahha al-rajulan' about the matter: both do not want it to escape them. End. [4] His statement: 'And if he refuses to one' its clarification: The third defendant, if he swears for one claimant, he swears for the second. If he swears, there is nothing for them due to lack of proof from the claimants. If he refuses to the second after swearing for the first, judgment is given for the second due to existence of proof, i.e., the nukool. If he refuses to the first, he swears for the second, and no judgment is given for the first by the nukool, because from the perspective of the other [claimant], one could say: if he had started with the oath against me, he would also have refused. And if he also refuses to the second, judgment is given dividing the thousand equally between the two claimants, as mentioned in the book, due to their equality in proof, just as if they had brought evidence. And the possessor pays another thousand divided between them, because the denying depositary obligated the right for each of them by his badhl according to the Imam's madhhab, since nukool is badhl according to him, or by his iqrar according to his two companions, for nukool is iqrar according to them, and iqrar or badhl is a proof against the depositary.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific dispute within the Hanafi school concerning the legal effect of nukool (refusal to take an oath) in a case of conflicting claims over a deposit. The matn states that if two men each claim that a sum of money in the hand of a third is a deposit from him, and the depositary refuses to swear to both, the money is divided equally between the two claimants, and the depositary must pay an additional sum equal to the deposit, also divided between them. The commentary then explains the reasoning: The judge makes the depositary swear for each claimant individually, starting with whichever he wishes. If he refuses to one, he is made to swear for the other; if he refuses for both, the judgment is given against him. The key point is the nature of nukool. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, nukool is a form of badhl (concession or relinquishment of right), which creates an obligation on the refuser. According to the two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, nukool is considered an iqrar (admission), which also creates an obligation. Whether badhl or iqrar, the effect is the same: the depositary becomes liable for the claimed amounts. The commentary also distinguishes this case from another where a deposit is given to a depositary by a usurper; Abu Hanifa holds that if the deposit comes from a trustee (amin), the second depositary is not liable because he is not a transgressor, but if it comes from a usurper, he is liable because he is a transgressor. The analogy of a garment blown by wind into a person's lap illustrates that taking possession without transgression does not create liability. The practical consequence: The deposit is divided equally, and an equal amount is also owed by the depositary.

Key Terms
نكول (nukool): Refusal of a defendant to take an oath when required by the judge, after the claimant lacks evidence. It is considered either as iqrar (admission) or badl (concession) by the Hanafi imams.
بذل (badhl): According to Imam Abu Hanifa, nukool is a form of badl, meaning the defendant voluntarily relinquishes his right to deny, thus conceding the claim without formally admitting.
إقرار (iqrar): Admission or confession by a party. In this dispute, the Sahibayn consider nukool as an implicit iqrar (admission) of the claim.
مستودع (mustawda'): The depositary: the person who holds the deposit (wadi'ah) and is entrusted with its safekeeping.
مودع (mudi'): The depositor: the person who entrusts his property to another for safekeeping, making the other a mustawda'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi view, what is the immediate consequence when a depositary refuses the oath (nukool) to both claimants in a deposit dispute?
A) The case is dismissed because no evidence exists.
B) The claimants must bring another witness.
C) The deposit is divided equally between the claimants, and the depositary must pay an additional equal amount.
D) The depositary is imprisoned until he confesses.

Key Takeaway
Nukool establishes liability regardless of whether it is seen as badl (concession) or iqrar (admission); the depositary must pay the claimed deposit plus an equivalent sum.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
8 min
Two Hanafi Riwayat on Oath After Judgment
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on oath procedure after judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi narrations on whether a second oath is required after judgment in a multi-claimant case and identify the scholarly authorities behind each.

Core Idea
Al-Bazdawi requires a second oath; al-Khassaf does not because nukul equals iqrar.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents two transmitted positions (riwayat) within the Hanafi school regarding the judge's procedure when one claimant refuses to swear an oath. The differing views come from two great Hanafi scholars: Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi and Abu Bakr al-Khassaf. Understanding this ikhtilaf reveals how the school treats the legal effect of nukul (refusal to swear) and its interaction with multi-party claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Recognize that the passage addresses a scenario where a judge has ruled for one claimant after the other refused to swear an oath. The question is whether the judge must then offer an oath to the second claimant.
Focus on the two rulings: al-Bazdawi says yes, al-Khassaf says no.
2
Analyze al-Bazdawi's position
According to al-Bazdawi, the judge must administer an oath to the second claimant. If the second also refuses, the judge decides between them (presumably dividing the claim). The reasoning is that the judgment for the first does not nullify the right of the second.
3
Analyze al-Khassaf's position
Al-Khassaf holds that no second oath is needed. His reasoning: the judgment was already passed in a matter open to ijtihad, and nukul (refusal) is treated like iqrar (acknowledgment). In cases of iqrar, no second oath is required.
Note the concept of 'nukul kama iqrar' – refusal is akin to confession.
4
Examine the scholars' biographies
The passage provides biographical sketches. Al-Bazdawi (d. 482 AH) was a leading Hanafi imam from Transoxiana, known for preserving the madhhab and writing difficult works. Al-Khassaf (d. 261 AH) was a Hanafi muhaddith and jurist, author of 'Kitab al-Hiyal' and 'Kitab al-Kharaj', known for his piety and expertise in law.
These biographies help assess the authority of each riwaya.
5
Understand the thamarah (practical result)
The practical difference: in a case with two claimants and one refuses, under al-Bazdawi's riwaya the judge proceeds to offer the oath to the second before finalizing; under al-Khassaf's riwaya the judge does not offer the oath and the original judgment stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلى رواية فخرِ الإسلام" البَزْدَوِي يَحْلِفُ للثاني ، فإن نَكَل يقضي بينهما ; لأنَّ القضاء للأول لا يبطل حقَّ الثَّاني، وعلى رواية الحصاف " لا يحلف للثاني ; لأنَّ القضاء وَقَعَ في مجتهد فيه ; لأنَّ بعض العلماء قال : إذا نَكَلَ لأحدهما يقضى له ، ولا يؤخر ليحلف للثاني ; لأنَّ النُّكول كالإقرار، وفي الإقرار لا يؤخر وهاهنا أبحاث وتفاصيل مزبورة في حواشي الهداية إن شئت الاطلاع عليها فارجع إليها.[ 1 ] قوله : فخر الإسلام ; وهو علي بن محمد بن الحسين بن عبد الكريم النَّسَفِيِّ البَزْدَوِي، نسبة إلى بزدة، وهي قلعة حصينة، والنسف أصله نخشب، وهو بلدة معروفة ، لفظ فخرِ الإسلام يدلُّ على وصف صاحبه ، وهو كان كذلك ; لأنه كان إماماً مرتضى ممتازاً في أصحاب ما وراء النهر ، وكان ممن يضرب المثل في حفظ المذهب. وكان يكنى بأبي العسر ; لكون تصانيفه عسيرة، وأخوه صدر الإسلام محمد بن محمد بن الحسين بن عبد الكريم كان يكنى بأبي اليسر; لكون تصانيفه يسيرة، وفخرُ الإسلام قد توفّي بكش في رجب سنة (٤٨٢) «اثنين وثمانين وأربع مئة».الخصف، بمعنى نعل[٢] قوله : وعلى رواية الخصاف ، فالخصافُ مشتق من ذو ختن ، قال في «المصباح» (۲) : خصف الرجلُ نعله خـصـفـاً مـن بـاب ضرب، فهو خصاف. انتهى.والمراد به أبو بكر أحمد بن عمرو الشيباني، كان محدثاً رئيساً للأحناف، زاهداً، عالماً بالرأي، ورعاً كبيراً في العلم ، وكان يأكل من صنعه، وله: «كتاب الحيل»، و «كتاب الخراج»، وغيرهما، وكان يروي عن بشر بن الوليد عن أبي يوسف، توفي ببغداد سنة (٢٦١) (( أحد ) وستين ومئتين. ذكره في «البناية»، وغيرها.
According to the narration of Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, he (the judge) administers an oath to the second (claimant). If he refuses, he judges between them because the judgment for the first does not nullify the right of the second. According to the narration of al-Khassaf, he does not administer an oath to the second because the judgment occurred in a matter of ijtihad; because some scholars said: When one (claimant) refuses (to swear), judgment is given for the other, and it is not delayed to swear the second because the refusal is like an acknowledgment, and in acknowledgment it is not delayed. There are here investigations and details recorded in the glosses of al-Hidayah; if you wish to consult them, refer to them. [1] His saying: Fakhr al-Islam; he is 'Ali ibn Muhammad ibn al-Husayn ibn 'Abd al-Karim al-Nasafi al-Bazdawi, attributed to Bazda, which is a strong fortress; and al-Nasaf, its original name is Nakhshab, a well-known town. The word 'Fakhr al-Islam' indicates the description of its bearer, and he was indeed so, because he was an accepted, distinguished imam among the scholars of Transoxiana, and he was one of those who are proverbial for preserving the madhhab. He was known as Abu al-'Usr because his writings were difficult, and his brother Sadr al-Islam Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn al-Husayn ibn 'Abd al-Karim was known as Abu al-Yusr because his writings were easy. Fakhr al-Islam died in Kash in Rajab of the year 482 (two and eighty and four hundred). Al-Khassaf, meaning a sandal. [2] His saying: And according to the narration of al-Khassaf; al-Khassaf is derived from 'dhū khitān' (possessor of circumcision). He said in al-Misbah: Khassafa the man his sandal, khassfan, from the conjugation of daraba, so he is a khassaf. End. What is meant by him is Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn 'Amr al-Shaybani; he was a muḥaddith, chief of the Hanafis, ascetic, knowledgeable in opinion, very pious in knowledge, and he used to eat from his own craftsmanship. He wrote 'Kitab al-Hiyal' and 'Kitab al-Kharaj' and others. He used to narrate from Bishr ibn al-Walid from Abu Yusuf. He died in Baghdad in the year 261 (one and sixty and two hundred). Mentioned in al-Binayah and others.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of a discussion from the hashiyah 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, which is a supercommentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah. The author mentions two narrations (riwayat) emanating from two major Hanafi scholars: Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi and Abu Bakr al-Khassaf. The issue concerns a judge who has ruled for one claimant after the other refused to swear an oath. According to al-Bazdawi, the judge must then offer an oath to the second claimant; if he also refuses, the judge gives judgment between them (i.e., presumably divides the claim). Al-Khassaf's narration holds that no second oath is needed because the judgment was already passed in a matter open to ijtihad, and the refusal (nukul) is treated like an acknowledgment (iqrar), which does not require a second oath. The text then provides biographical details: al-Bazdawi was a leading imam of Transoxiana, known for his deep preservation of the madhhab and difficult writings; al-Khassaf was a Hanafi hadith scholar and jurist who authored famous works like 'Kitab al-Hiyal' and 'Kitab al-Kharaj', and died in 261 AH. The passage directs the reader to the glosses of al-Hidayah for further discussion.

Key Terms
النكول (al-nukūl): Refusal to take an oath in a legal proceeding; treated akin to an acknowledgment (iqrar) in this context.
فخر الإسلام (Fakhr al-Islām): Title of 'Ali al-Bazdawi (d. 482 AH), a leading Hanafi jurist of Transoxiana known for his mastery of the madhhab.
الخصاف (al-Khaṣṣāf): Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn 'Amr al-Shaybani (d. 261 AH), a Hanafi jurist and muhaddith, known for works on legal stratagems (hiyal) and taxation (kharaj).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the practical difference between the two narrations mentioned regarding the oath after judgment?
A) Al-Bazdawi says judge gives oath to second claimant; al-Khassaf says no oath needed.
B) Both agree the judge must give oath to the second claimant.
C) Al-Bazdawi says no oath needed; al-Khassaf says give oath.
D) The difference is only in the wording, not in the ruling.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school transmits two positions on whether a second oath is required after a claimant's refusal, reflecting different views on the effect of nukul and the finality of judgment.
